The Obligation of Imamate: Revelation vs. Reason
Imam Razi classifies opinions on the obligation of imamate, focusing on those who say it is obligatory and the first subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main perspectives on the obligation of imamate: those who base it on revelation and those who base it on reason, and the first subgroup that places the obligation on the people.

Core Idea
The obligation of imamate is not agreed upon; some ground it in revelation, others in reason.

Imam al-Razi begins his discussion of imamate by classifying the different opinions on whether appointing an imam is obligatory. He first categorizes the views into those who say it is obligatory and those who say it is not, then focuses on the obligatory view. This lesson covers the initial classification up to the view that the obligation is upon the people themselves by reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the classification system Imam Razi uses.
Notice the logical structure: first a division, then a subdivision.
2
Identify the two main groups
Within those who say obligatory, find the two groups: those who rely on revelation and those who rely on reason. Note their examples.
The Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydiyya are in the revelation group.
3
Examine the 'reason on the people' view
Understand why some Mu'tazila argue that reason obligates the people to appoint a leader to repel harm.
This view is attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and earlier figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
في الإمامة
وفيها فصول :
الفصل الأول : في وجوب الإمامة
اختلف الناس فيه، وضبط المذاهب فيه أن يقال : نصب الإمام إما أن يقال: واجب أو غير واجب، أما القائلون بأنه واجب فهم فريقان :
أحدهما : الذين قالوا : نصبه واجب والطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب السمع دون العقل، وهذا هو قول أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة والزيدية.
والثاني : الذين يقولون: الطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب العقل، ثم هؤلاء فريقان، منهم من قال إنه يجب عقلا على الخلق أن ينصبوا لأنفسهم رئيساً، وذلك لأن نصب هذا الرئيس يتضمن دفع الضرر عن النفس ودفع الضرر عن النفس واجب عقلا. وهذا هو قول أبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة ومن قدمائهم قول الجاحظ وأبي الحسين الخياط وأبي القاسم الكعبي.
ومنهم من قال : بل يجب على الله نصب الإمام للخلق، وهؤلاء فريقان: قالت الملاحدة والإسماعيلية : إنه لا سبيل إلى معرفة الله إلا بتعليم الرسول والإمام، فوجب على الله تعالى أن لا يخلي العالم عن المعصوم حتى إن ذلك المعصوم يرشد الخلق إلى معرفة الله تعالى .
وقالت الإثنا عشرية : لا حاجة في معرفة الله تعالى إلى المعصوم إلا أنه يجب على الله تعالى نصب الإمام المعصوم ليكون لطفا في أداء الواجبات العقلية، والاجتناب عن القبائح العقلية، وليكون أيضًا حافظا للدين عن الزيادة والنقصان. وقال بعض قدماء الشيعة إنه يجب على الله نصب الإمام ليعلمهم أحوال
The Thirty-Ninth Issue
On Imamate
And it contains chapters:
The First Chapter: On the Obligation of Imamate
People differed regarding it, and the summary of the schools regarding it is that the appointment of an imam is either said to be obligatory or not obligatory. As for those who say it is obligatory, they are two groups:
One of them: Those who said: His appointment is obligatory, and the way to know this obligation is revelation without reason. This is the view of our companions (Ash'aris) and most of the Mu'tazila and the Zaydiyya.
The second: Those who say: The way to know this obligation is reason. Then these are two groups: among them are those who said that it is obligatory by reason upon the people to appoint for themselves a leader, because the appointment of this leader entails repelling harm from oneself, and repelling harm from oneself is obligatory by reason. This is the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu'tazila, and among their early ones the view of al-Jahiz, Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi.
And among them are those who said: Rather, it is obligatory upon God to appoint an imam for the people. These are two groups: The Mulahida and the Isma'iliyya said: There is no way to know God except through the teaching of the Messenger and the imam, so it is obligatory upon God Most High not to leave the world without an infallible one, so that that infallible one guides the people to knowledge of God Most High.
And the Ithna 'Ashariyya said: There is no need in knowing God Most High for the infallible one, but it is obligatory upon God Most High to appoint the infallible imam to be a grace (lutf) in performing the rational duties and refraining from rational abominations, and also to be a protector of the religion from increase and decrease. And some early Shi'a said that it is obligatory upon God to appoint the imam to teach them the conditions...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on the obligation of imamate. Imam al-Razi begins by stating that people differ, and he frames the classification around whether the appointment of an imam is obligatory or not. He then focuses on those who say it is obligatory. They divide into two: one group says the obligation is known by revelation (the Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydiyya). The other group says it is known by reason, and they subdivide: some say reason imposes the obligation upon the people themselves to appoint a leader (citing repelling harm as a rational duty). This view is attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and earlier Mu'tazila like al-Jahiz, al-Khayyat, and al-Ka'bi. Others say the obligation is upon God to appoint an imam. These are further divided: the Mulahida (here meaning Batinis/Ismailis) and Ismailis hold that knowledge of God requires an infallible teacher, so God must provide one. The Twelvers say there is no need for an infallible in knowing God, but God must appoint one as a grace (lutf) to assist in performing rational duties and to protect the religion from alteration. Finally, some early Shi'a held that God must appoint an imam to teach people the conditions (of religion). The text cuts off here, but the classification is complete as far as the available text goes.

Key Terms
إمامة
نصب الإمام
وجوب
سمع
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all Muslims agree that the imam must be appointed by divine command.
Correction: This passage shows that there is disagreement on the source of obligation (revelation vs reason) and on whom the obligation falls (people vs God).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that the obligation of imamate is known by revelation?
A) Twelver Shi'a
B) Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydiyya
C) Ismailis
D) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers

Key Takeaway
The obligation of imamate is a contested issue, with some basing it on revelation and others on reason, and even among the rationalists, there are different views on who bears the obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even the 'obligation' of imamate is debated among scholars. When you hold a religious opinion, do you assume it is the only valid one? How can you guard against dismissing contrary views without understanding their evidence? Commit today to read one primary source from a differing position on a contested issue.
?
The rationalists argue that repelling harm (by appointing a leader) is a self-evident duty. Reflect: have you ever avoided participating in community affairs because it was inconvenient, while harm was being done? What step will you take this week to actively contribute to the well-being of your community?
?
The Ash'aris ground the obligation in revelation. Do your political and communal decisions truly follow revelation, or do you prioritize your own reasoning and desires? How can you realign your loyalties so that the Qur'an and Sunnah are your ultimate guides, even when they challenge your preferences?

The Obligation upon God to Appoint an Imam
Imam Razi classifies the views that impose the obligation of imamate on God, including Ismaili, Twelver, and early Shi'a opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opinions of those who hold that the obligation to appoint an imam falls upon God, including the Ismaili, Twelver, and early Shi'a views.

Core Idea
Some groups argue that God must provide an infallible imam for guidance or as a grace.

After discussing those who place the obligation on the people, Imam al-Razi turns to those who say the obligation is upon God. He further divides this camp into the Ismailis, who see the infallible imam as necessary for knowing God, the Twelvers, who see him as a grace (lutf) for performing duties and preserving the religion, and some early Shi'a who hold a similar view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this section, noting the three subgroups mentioned.
The text cuts off at the end, but the main views are clear.
2
Compare the Ismaili and Twelver perspectives
Notice the difference: Ismailis say the infallible is required for knowing God, while Twelvers say he is not needed for that but is a grace.
Both agree on the obligation upon God to appoint an imam.
3
Reflect on the early Shi'a view
The text mentions that some early Shi'a held that God must appoint an imam to teach people. Consider how this differs from the other views.
The passage ends abruptly, but the idea is clear.

On Imamate

One of them: Those who said: His appointment is obligatory, and the way to know this obligation is revelation without reason. This is the view of our companions (Ash'aris) and most of the Mu'tazila and the Zaydiyya.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Twelver view on the appointment of an infallible imam?
A) It is necessary for knowing God
B) It is a grace (lutf) for performing duties and preserving religion
C) It is not obligatory
D) It is obligatory only if the people request it

Among those who say the obligation is on God, there are differences on why God must appoint an infallible imam: for necessary guidance (Ismaili), as a gracious aid (Twelver), or for teaching (early Shi'a).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Isma'ilis claim you cannot know God without an infallible teacher. Do you rely on any human to know God, or do you trust that God has made Himself known sufficiently through revelation and reason? Examine your own sources of certainty: are they truly independent of any fallible human authority?
?
The Twelvers say an infallible imam is not needed to know God, but is a grace to help you perform duties. If you had an infallible guide today, would your obedience increase? Or does the absence of such a guide expose a weakness in your own resolve to follow God's commands without external enforcement?
?
Early Shi'a said the imam is to teach people. Whom do you turn to for religious teaching? How does your choice reflect your belief – or lack thereof – that God has provided adequate guidance through the Qur'an and the Prophet's example alone?

Classification of Schools on the Obligation of the Imam
Razi classifies schools on imamate obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different classifications of Islamic schools regarding the obligation of appointing an Imam, including those who base it on revelation vs. reason, and the various subgroups.

Core Idea
The obligation of imamate is divided by source (revelation/reason) and subject (people/God).

Imam al-Razi begins the discussion of imamate by classifying the major schools on the fundamental question: Is appointing an Imam obligatory, and how is that obligation known? He presents a detailed taxonomy that maps the positions of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Zaydis, and various Shi'a groups. This lesson guides you through that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the hierarchical structure: first division, then sub-divisions.
2
Identify the main branches
The passage first distinguishes those who say appointment is obligatory vs non-obligatory, then focuses on the obligatory group. Within that, identify the two main groups: those who base obligation on revelation and those who base it on reason.
3
Trace the subdivisions
Among the reason-based group, note two further splits: obligation on the people themselves vs. obligation on God. Then among the 'obligation on God' group, three Shi'a sub-schools are listed.
Pay attention to the specific names: Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Zaydis, Jahiz, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون في الإمامة وفيها فصول : الفصل الأول : في وجوب الإمامة اختلف الناس فيه، وضبط المذاهب فيه أن يقال : نصب الإمام إما أن يقال: واجب أو غير واجب، أما القائلون بأنه واجب فهم فريقان : أحدهما : الذين قالوا : نصبه واجب والطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب السمع دون العقل، وهذا هو قول أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة والزيدية. والثاني : الذين يقولون: الطريق إلى معرفة هذا الوجوب العقل، ثم هؤلاء فريقان، منهم من قال إنه يجب عقلا على الخلق أن ينصبوا لأنفسهم رئيساً، وذلك لأن نصب هذا الرئيس يتضمن دفع الضرر عن النفس ودفع الضرر عن النفس واجب عقلا. وهذا هو قول أبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة ومن قدمائهم قول الجاحظ وأبي الحسين الخياط وأبي القاسم الكعبي. ومنهم من قال : بل يجب على الله نصب الإمام للخلق، وهؤلاء فريقان: قالت الملاحدة والإسماعيلية : إنه لا سبيل إلى معرفة الله إلا بتعليم الرسول والإمام، فوجب على الله تعالى أن لا يخلي العالم عن المعصوم حتى إن ذلك المعصوم يرشد الخلق إلى معرفة الله تعالى . وقالت الإثنا عشرية : لا حاجة في معرفة الله تعالى إلى المعصوم إلا أنه يجب على الله تعالى نصب الإمام المعصوم ليكون لطفا في أداء الواجبات العقلية، والاجتناب عن القبائح العقلية، وليكون أيضًا حافظا للدين عن الزيادة والنقصان. وقال بعض قدماء الشيعة إنه يجب على الله نصب الإمام ليعلمهم أحوال الأغذية والأدوية، ويعلمهم السموم المهلكة، ويعرفهم الحرف والصناعات، ويصونهم عن الآفات والمخاوف. فهذا تفصيل قول من قال : إنه يجب نصب الإمام.
The thirty-ninth question concerning imamate. It contains chapters. The first chapter: On the obligation of the Imam. People have differed concerning it, and the summary of the schools therein is that the appointment of an Imam is either said to be obligatory or not obligatory. As for those who say it is obligatory, they are two groups: one of them: those who say his appointment is obligatory and the way to know this obligation is revelation, not reason, and this is the saying of our companions (Ash'aris) and most of the Mu'tazila and the Zaydis. The second: those who say the way to know this obligation is reason. Then these are two groups: among them are those who say that it is obligatory by reason upon the people to appoint a leader for themselves, because the appointment of this leader entails repelling harm from oneself, and repelling harm from oneself is obligatory by reason. This is the saying of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila, and among their early figures the saying of al-Jahiz, Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi. And among them are those who say: rather it is obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam for the people. These are two groups: the Mulahida (heretics) and the Isma'ilis said: there is no way to know God except through the teaching of the Messenger and the Imam, so it is obligatory upon God the Exalted that He not leave the world empty of an infallible one, so that that infallible one guides the people to knowledge of God the Exalted. The Ithna-asharis said: there is no need in knowing God the Exalted for an infallible one, but it is obligatory upon God the Exalted to appoint the infallible Imam to be a grace (lutf) in performing the intellectual duties and avoiding intellectual abominations, and also to be a preserver of the religion from increase and decrease. And some early Shi'ites said that it is obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam to teach them the conditions of foods and medicines, and to teach them deadly poisons, and to make them know crafts and industries, and to protect them from calamities and fears. This is the detail of the saying of those who say: the appointment of an Imam is obligatory.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the various schools that hold the appointment of an Imam to be obligatory. Razi begins by stating the subject: the thirty-ninth question on imamate, with the first chapter on the obligation of the Imam. He frames the discussion by noting that people differ, and the positions can be summarized as either saying appointment is obligatory or not. He then focuses on those who say it is obligatory, dividing them into two main groups. The first group holds that the obligation is known through revelation (sam') alone, not reason. This is the position of the Ash'aris (his own school), most Mu'tazila, and the Zaydis. The second group holds that the obligation is known through reason. He further subdivides this group. One subgroup says it is obligatory by reason upon the people to appoint a leader, because appointment repels harm from oneself, and repelling harm is rationally obligatory. He attributes this view to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and earlier Mu'tazila like al-Jahiz, al-Khayyat, and al-Ka'bi. Another subgroup says it is obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam for the people. This subgroup is further divided. The Mulahida and Isma'ilis claim that knowledge of God is impossible without the teaching of a messenger or Imam, so God must provide an infallible guide. The Ithna-asharis (Twelver Shi'a) hold that while knowledge of God does not require an infallible, God must appoint an infallible Imam as a grace (lutf) to help people perform rational duties and avoid rational evils, and to preserve the religion. Finally, some early Shi'ites held that God must appoint an Imam to teach practical knowledge like foods, medicines, crafts, and protection from harms. Razi concludes this section by stating that this completes the detail of those who say the Imam must be appointed.

Key Terms
الإمامة (imamate)
نصب الإمام (appointment of the Imam)
واجب (obligatory)
سمع (revelation)
عقل (reason)
لطف (grace)
معصوم (infallible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree that imamate is rationally obligatory.
Correction: Many schools (Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, Zaydis) hold that the obligation is known only through revelation, not reason. Even among rationalists, there is disagreement on whether the obligation falls on people or on God.

Checkpoint
Which group holds that the obligation of the Imam is known only through revelation, not reason?
A) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and the early Mu'tazila
B) The Ash'aris, most Mu'tazila, and Zaydis
C) The Isma'ilis and Mulahida
D) The Ithna-asharis

Key Takeaway
The obligation of imamate is not universally derived from reason; many schools base it solely on revelation, while even among rationalists there is disagreement on whom the obligation falls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the different views on how imamate is established, which view resonates most with your own understanding? Why? Are you willing to re-examine your assumptions if they are based on mere tradition rather than evidence?
?
The Ash'aris rely solely on revelation for the obligation of imam. Do you truly submit to revelation in matters where reason might give a different answer? Ask yourself honestly: Is your commitment to revelation absolute, even when it contradicts what seems rational to you?
?
The Isma'ilis claim knowledge of God is impossible without an infallible teacher. How do you personally gain certainty about God? Have you ever felt lost without a guide? What does this say about your relationship with Allah and His messengers?

Three Positions Denying Absolute Obligation of Imamate
Razi enumerates three groups that reject the absolute obligation of Imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced positions of three groups that deny the absolute obligation of Imamate, including their conditional reasoning based on societal conditions.

Core Idea
Imamate's obligation depends on context — not everyone agrees it's always required.

Razi now turns to those who challenge the obligation of Imamate. He carefully records their arguments, showing that even when denying obligation, scholars did so based on conditions like justice or social stability. This passage presents three distinct positions, each with its own logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage fully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the three groups. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
Notice that Razi uses enumeration (الأولى, الثانية, الثالثة).
2
Analyze al-Asamm's view
The first group (al-Asamm) makes obligation conditional on fitna. Under fitna, it's obligatory; under security and justice, it's not. Think about why he might have argued this way.
3
Analyze the second group's reversal
The second group reverses the condition: during fitna, appointment is not obligatory (may increase strife), but during justice, it is obligatory. Compare this to al-Asamm.
Note the reasoning that appointment could cause rebellion.
4
Analyze the Kharijite position
Most Kharijites deny obligation at any time; it is purely permissible. This is the most extreme position. Consider how this differs from the others.
5
Reflect on the classification
Think about why Razi presents these three views. How does this contribute to the overall discussion of Imamate? Recognize the range of opinions even among those who reject absolute obligation.
This shows Razi's thoroughness as a scholar.

وأما الذين قالوا : إنه غير واجب فهم فرق : الأولى : قال الأصم إن نصب الإمام عند ظهور الفتن واجب، وأما عند الأمن والعدل فلا. الثانية : قال بعضهم : نصب الإمام عند ظهور الفتن غير واجب ؛ لأنه ربما يصير نصبه سببًا لتمرد بعضهم عن الطاعة فتزداد الفتن أما عند ظهور العدل فهو واجب. الثالثة : قول أكثر الخوارج : إنه لا يجب الإمام في شيء من الأوقات، فإن فعلوه جاز، وإن تركوه جاز أيضًا. فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في هذه المسألة.
And as for those who said that it is not obligatory, they are groups: The first: al-Asamm said that the appointment of an Imam at the time of the emergence of fitna (strife) is obligatory, but at the time of security and justice, it is not. The second: some of them said: the appointment of an Imam at the time of the emergence of fitna is not obligatory, because perhaps his appointment becomes a cause for some of them to rebel against obedience, so fitna increases; but at the time of the emergence of justice it is obligatory. The third: the saying of most of the Kharijites: that the Imam is not obligatory at any time; if they do it, it is permissible, and if they abandon it, it is also permissible. So this is the detail of the schools of people in this matter.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage classifies the positions of those who deny the absolute obligation of Imamate, presenting three distinct groups. The first group is associated with al-Asamm, who makes the obligation conditional: if there is fitna (strife), appointing an Imam becomes obligatory; but if there is security and justice, it is not obligatory. The second group reverses the condition: they say that at the time of fitna, appointing an Imam is not obligatory because it might cause rebellion and worsen the fitna; but when justice prevails, it becomes obligatory. The third group, representing most of the Kharijites, holds that Imamate is never obligatory; it is purely optional: if people appoint an Imam, it is permissible; if they do not, it is equally permissible. Razi concludes by stating that this completes the enumeration of the different schools on this question.

Key Terms
غير واجب (not obligatory)
الأصم (al-Asamm)
فتنة (fitna/strife)
أمن (security)
عدل (justice)
خوارج (Kharijites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all who deny obligation simply reject it outright, but they have nuanced conditions based on social circumstances.
Correction: As seen in the passage, al-Asamm and the second group condition obligation on the presence or absence of fitna and justice. Only the Kharijites reject outright.

Checkpoint
According to most Kharijites, what is the ruling on appointing an Imam?
A) Obligatory at all times
B) Obligatory only during fitna
C) Permissible but not obligatory at any time
D) Obligatory only during justice

Key Takeaway
The obligation of Imamate is not universally accepted; different groups condition it on social circumstances like fitna and justice, while the Kharijites deny obligation entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Asamm believed Imamate is obligatory only during fitna. Reflect on your own life: Do you only seek righteous leadership and guidance when there is crisis, or do you actively pursue it even in times of peace and comfort? What does your pattern of action reveal about the depth of your commitment to divine order?
?
The Kharijites considered Imamate entirely optional. Examine your heart: Is there any area of religious duty—like commanding good, forbidding evil, or seeking knowledge—that you have treated as optional rather than obligatory? What would change if you treated it as a binding command from Allah?

Three Positions on the Obligation of Imamate
Classifies the three groups who deny the absolute obligation of imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions among those who deny the absolute obligation of appointing an imam, and how they differ based on the presence of strife or justice.

Core Idea
Imamate's obligation depends on circumstances, according to some groups.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the views of those who do not consider the appointment of an imam absolutely obligatory. He lists three distinct positions, each with its own rationale regarding the presence of strife or justice. This classification helps us understand the diversity of early Islamic thought on political leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the entire passage to get an overview of the three groups. Notice the structure: an introduction, then three numbered positions.
The passage begins with a reference to those who affirm obligation, but focuses on those who deny it.
2
Identify the first group (al-Asam)
Find al-Asam's view: he makes obligation conditional on the emergence of strife. Obligation exists only during strife, not during security and justice.
Al-Asam is a well-known Mu'tazili figure.
3
Identify the second group
This group holds the opposite: obligation during strife is not obligatory (to avoid worsening it), but during justice it is obligatory.
This view tries to prevent strife from escalating.
4
Identify the third group (Kharijites)
Most Kharijites hold that imamate is never obligatory; it is merely permissible to appoint or not.
This is the most permissive view.
5
Reflect on the spectrum
Compare the three views: one conditional on strife, one conditional on justice, and one unconditional permissibility. This shows how different premises lead to different rulings.
These positions represent a spectrum from limited obligation to no obligation.

The third: the saying of most of the Kharijites: that the Imam is not obligatory at any time; if they do it, it is permissible, and if they abandon it, it is also permissible. So this is the detail of the schools of the people concerning this issue.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different views on the obligation of appointing an imam. The author first states that some groups affirm the obligation of appointing an imam (referring to his own view, which will be detailed later). Then he lists three groups among those who deny absolute obligation, starting with al-Asam, who distinguishes between times of strife and times of peace. The second group holds the opposite: it is not obligatory during strife (to avoid exacerbating it) but obligatory during peace. The third group, the majority of the Kharijites, holds that imamate is never obligatory; it is merely permissible. The passage concludes by stating that this summarizes the positions of people on this matter.

Proof that God is Not Obliged to Appoint an Imam
Razi constructs a dilemma to disprove divine obligation of imam appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument (dilemma) that God is not obligated to appoint an imam, and the refutation of the Ismaili and Ithna Ashari positions.

Core Idea
If God were obliged to appoint an imam, He would have sent one accessible to all — but He didn’t, so He is not obliged.

The author, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, addresses a key sectarian dispute regarding the imamate. The Ismailis and Ithna Ashari claim that God must appoint an imam for guidance. Razi presents a logical dilemma: either the imam is accessible and beneficial, or not. The first is contrary to observable reality, and the second is useless. This passage establishes the Sunni position that the imamate is not a divine obligation but a communal necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure of the dilemma and the two divisions presented.
The passage is a proof (dalil) against divine obligation.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author splits the assumption of divine obligation into two possibilities: either the imam is accessible and beneficial, or he is not. Find these in the text.
These are labeled as 'القسم الأول' and 'القسم الثاني'.
3
Understand why each division is false
The first division is false because no such accessible imam exists empirically. The second is false because an inaccessible imam cannot provide benefit.
The refutation relies on observation and logical necessity.
4
Consider the objection and its rebuttal
The opponent argues that even a hidden imam provides benefit (as a guide or grace). The author's rebuttal is that the fault lies with the oppressors, but the text is incomplete. Reflect on the argument despite the corruption.
The rebuttal is cut off; the exact argument is unclear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بيان المقام الثاني . وهو أنه لا يجب على الله نصب الإمام - فالخلاف فيه مع الإسماعيلية والإثنا عشرية على ما شرحناه فنقول : الدليل على أنه غير واجب أنه لو جب على الله نصب الإمام لكان الواجب إما نصب إمام يتمكن المكلف من الرجوع إليه والانتفاع به في دينه ودنياه، أو الواجب نصب إمام سواء كان كذلك أو لم يكن، والقسمان باطلان فالقول بالوجوب باطل . إنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل لأنه غير موجود ولو كان ذلك واجبًا على الله تعالى لفعله، ولكنه ما فعله، فإن الواحد منا إذا احتاج إلى هذا الإمام في أن يستفيد منه علما أو دينا أو يجلب بواسطته إلى نفسه منفعة أو يدفع عنها مضرة فلو أتى بأي حيلة كانت لم يجد منه البتة أثرًا ولا خبرًا، والعلم بذلك ضروري. وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الثاني باطل لأن المقصود من نصب هذا الإمام إما منفعة دينية أو دنيوية لا محالة والانتفاع به يعتمد إمكان الوصول إليه، ولما تعذر إمكان الوصول إليه تعذر ذلك الانتفاع به ، وإذا تعذر الانتفاع به لم يكن في نصبه فائدة أصلا، فكان القول بوجوب نصبه عبئًا. فإن قيل : إن في نصبه أعظم الفوائد والمنافع ، وهو أن يكون هاديًا إلى معرفة الله تعالى على قول الإسماعيلية أو أن يكون لطفا في أداء الواجبات العقلية إلا أن إلا الاختفاء والاستتار فالذنب منهم ؛ حيث الظلمة خوفوه تخويفا احتاج معه أحوجوه إلى الاختفاء.
And the explanation of the second position: that it is not obligatory for God to appoint the imam. The dispute with the Ismailis and the Ithna Ashari regarding it is as we have explained. So we say: The proof that it is not obligatory is that if appointing the imam were obligatory for God, then the obligatory would be either the appointment of an imam whom the accountable person can refer to and benefit from in his religion and his world, or the obligatory would be the appointment of an imam whether he is like that or not. Both divisions are false, so the claim of obligation is false. We say that the first division is false because it does not exist; if it were obligatory for God Almighty, He would have done it, but He did not do it. For if one of us needs this imam to derive from him knowledge or religion, or to bring about by means of him a benefit or repel a harm, then if he were to try any stratagem, he would not find from him any trace or news at all. Knowledge of this is necessary. And we say that the second division is false because the purpose of appointing this imam is inevitably either a religious or worldly benefit, and benefiting from him depends on the possibility of reaching him. Since the possibility of reaching him is impossible, that benefit becomes impossible; and if benefit from him becomes impossible, there is no benefit in his appointment at all, so the claim of its obligation is a burden. If it is said: In his appointment there are the greatest benefits and advantages, namely that he be a guide to knowledge of God Almighty according to the saying of the Ismailis, or that he be a grace (lutf) in performing the intellectual duties, except that the concealment and hiding, the fault is from them, because the oppressors frightened him with such frightening that he needed, they forced him to hide.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is not obligated to appoint an imam, directed against the Ismaili and Ithna Ashari sects. The author constructs a dilemma: if God were obliged, the imam would either be accessible and beneficial to the people, or not necessarily so. The first is impossible because such an accessible imam does not exist; if it were obligatory, God would have provided him, but there is no trace of him. The second is impossible because an inaccessible imam cannot provide benefit, so his appointment would be useless. Therefore, the obligation is false. An objection is raised that the hidden imam still provides benefits (as a guide or a grace). The author's rebuttal is cut off in the text but seems to argue that the fault lies with the oppressors who forced him into hiding, not with God.

Key Terms
وجوب نصب الإمام على الله
الإسماعيلية
الإثنا عشرية
لطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to appoint an imam for the guidance of humanity.
Correction: The source shows that if such an obligation were real, God would have provided an accessible imam. Since He did not, the obligation is false. An inaccessible imam is useless.

Checkpoint

A) Because such an imam would still provide spiritual guidance
B) Because benefit from him requires access, which is impossible, so his appointment is useless
C) Because God promised to provide such an imam but did not
D) Because the Kharijites already rejected the imam

Key Takeaway
God is not obliged to appoint an imam because an accessible imam does not exist and an inaccessible imam provides no benefit.

?
If an inaccessible ‘grace’ is no grace at all, what ‘graces’ in your own life are you holding onto that are actually out of reach—and what would it cost you to let go of the claim that God must provide them?

Razi's Proof That Imam Is Not Obligatory Upon God
Razi disproves obligation of imam via two-part division argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's disjunctive argument that the appointment of an Imam is not obligatory upon God, and why both possible divisions are false.

Core Idea
If the Imam is inaccessible, obligation is pointless; if accessible, it doesn't exist.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses the question of whether God is obligated to appoint an Imam. He presents a simple but powerful two-part division argument to show that such obligation cannot hold. The argument relies on empirical reality (no accessible Imam exists) and logical purpose (an Imam without access has no benefit). Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Razi's own argumentative structure come through before you interpret.
Notice how Razi sets up a conditional and then divides it.
2
Identify the two divisions
Find where Razi divides the supposed obligation into two scenarios. Label them clearly: accessible beneficial Imam vs. any Imam regardless.
These are the two horns of the dilemma.
3
Understand why both are false
Razi argues first that no accessible Imam exists empirically, so that scenario is false. Second, an inaccessible Imam cannot achieve its purpose, so that scenario is also pointless. Conclude that the obligation claim is false.
Focus on the logical flow: if neither horn holds, the original statement collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بيان المقام الثاني . وهو أنه لا يجب على الله نصب الإمام - فالخلاف فيه مع الإسماعيلية والإثنا عشرية على ما شرحناه فنقول : الدليل على أنه غير واجب أنه لو جب على الله نصب الإمام لكان الواجب إما نصب إمام يتمكن المكلف من الرجوع إليه والانتفاع به في دينه ودنياه، أو الواجب نصب إمام سواء كان كذلك أو لم يكن، والقسمان ،باطلان فالقول بالوجوب باطل . إنما قلنا : إن القسم الأول باطل لأنه غير موجود ولو كان ذلك واجبًا على الله تعالى لفعله، ولكنه ما فعله، فإن الواحد منا إذا احتاج إلى هذا الإمام في أن يستفيد منه علما أو دينا أو يجلب بواسطته إلى نفسه منفعة أو يدفع عنها مضرة فلو أتى بأي حيلة كانت لم يجد منه البتة أثرًا ولا خبرًا، والعلم بذلك ضروري. وإنما قلنا : إن القسم الثاني باطل لأن المقصود من نصب هذا الإمام إما منفعة دينية أو دنيوية لا محالة والانتفاع به يعتمد إمكان الوصول إليه، ولما تعذر إمكان الوصول إليه تعذر ذلك الانتفاع به ، وإذا تعذر الانتفاع به لم يكن في نصبه فائدة أصلا، فكان القول بوجوب نصبه عبئًا.
And the clarification of the second station: that it is not obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam - the disagreement is with the Ismailis and the Twelvers as we have explained. So we say: The proof that it is not obligatory is that if it were obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam, then the obligatory would be either the appointment of an Imam whom the responsible person can refer to and benefit from in his religion and worldly affairs, or the appointment of an Imam regardless of whether that is the case or not. Both divisions are false, so the claim of obligation is false. We say: The first division is false because it does not exist. Had it been obligatory upon God, He would have done it, but He did not do it. For if one of us needs this Imam to obtain from him knowledge or religion, or to bring benefit to himself or repel harm through him, then even if he uses any trick, he finds no trace or news of him at all. And knowledge of that is necessary. We say: The second division is false because the purpose of appointing this Imam is necessarily either religious or worldly benefit. Benefiting from him depends on the possibility of reaching him. Since the possibility of reaching him is impossible, that benefit is impossible. When benefiting from him is impossible, there is no benefit in his appointment at all. So the claim that his appointment is obligatory is a burden.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the appointment of an Imam is not obligatory upon God. Razi frames it as a disjunctive syllogism. If it were obligatory, the Imam must be either (1) an Imam who is accessible and beneficial to the individual in religion and world, or (2) an Imam regardless of whether he is accessible or beneficial. Both are false. (1) is false because such an Imam does not exist empirically; if it were obligatory, God would have created it, but we see no accessible beneficial Imam. (2) is false because the very purpose of an Imam is benefit; if benefit is impossible due to inaccessibility, the appointment has no point. Hence the obligation claim is baseless.

Key Terms
وجوب
إمام
قسم
باطل
مكلف
نصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi denies that an Imam could be beneficial in principle.
Correction: Razi does not deny the theoretical benefit of an Imam; he denies its existence and thus its obligation.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible divisions of an obligatory imam according to Razi?
A) One who guides to God and one who rules justly
B) One who is accessible and beneficial, and one regardless of that
C) One from the Prophet's family and one from Quraysh
D) One who is hidden and one who is apparent

Key Takeaway
Razi uses a division argument to show that the obligation of an Imam is rationally untenable because neither possible scenario holds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever find yourself waiting for a perfect guide or leader to solve your problems or show you the way? How does Razi's argument challenge that passivity and call you to take responsibility for your own religious and worldly affairs?

If you truly believed that no divinely appointed Imam is accessible today, what would change about how you seek knowledge and guidance? Would you rely more on scripture, reason, or community, and why?
?
Razi says that an inaccessible Imam provides no benefit. Are there people or sources of knowledge in your life that you treat as inaccessible—perhaps because you feel unworthy or distant? How might you actively seek to benefit from them rather than waiting for them to come to you?

Refuting the Justification for the Imam's Occultation
Objector claims Imam guides; answer shows occultation breaks the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Ismaili justification for the Imam's occultation by showing that it would deprive an innocent person of benefit, rendering the claim unfounded.

Core Idea
Occultation cannot be excused if even one innocent person cannot benefit.

Imam Razi addresses a common argument that the Imam's presence offers great benefits, and his occultation is due to oppressors' fault. He responds by focusing on a specific innocent individual who cannot reach the Imam, showing that God would then be obliged to make the Imam appear. Since this does not happen, the claim of a hidden imam is unfounded.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's two claims: the Imam guides to God (for Ismailis) or is a grace for duties, and that his occultation is due to oppressors' threat.
2
Identify the answer's core move
Razi considers a specific person who has done nothing to make the Imam fear him yet cannot reach the Imam, concluding that God would command the Imam to appear.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since that does not occur, the entire report of a hidden imam is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إن في نصبه أعظم الفوائد والمنافع ، وهو أن يكون هاديًا إلى معرفة الله تعالى على قول الإسماعيلية أو أن يكون لطفا في أداء الواجبات العقلية إلا أن الاختفاء والاستتار فالذنب منهم ؛ حيث الظلمة خوفوه تخويفا احتاج معه أحوجوه إلى الاختفاء. فالجواب : أن هذا المكلف المعين إذا لم يفعل فعلا البتة يوجب أن يصير الإمام خائفًا منه، ثم إنه بقي بحال لا يمكنه الوصول إلى هذا الإمام بشيء من الطرق، فقد صار محرومًا عن هذا الانتفاع لا بأمر صدر منه، فكان يجب على الله تعالى أن يأمر الإمام بأن يظهر نفسه لهذا المحتاج ولما لم يوجد شيء من علمنا أنه لا أصل لهذا الحديث.
If it is said: In his appointment are the greatest benefits, namely that he be a guide to knowledge of God according to the Ismailis, or that he be a grace (lutf) in performing rational duties, except that concealment and occultation - the fault is from them, since the oppressors threatened him a threat that he needed with it they compelled him to concealment. The answer: That this particular responsible person, if he does not do any action at all that would cause the Imam to become fearful of him, and then he remains in a state where he cannot reach this Imam by any means, then he has been deprived of this benefit not due to anything he did. So it would be incumbent upon God to command the Imam to show himself to this needy person. Since nothing of that exists, we know that this report has no basis.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that the imam provides great benefits: guidance to God (for Ismailis) or grace for rational duties. If he is in occultation, that is the fault of oppressors who intimidated him. The answer refutes this by considering a specific responsible person who has done nothing to make the Imam fear him, yet cannot reach the Imam. Since this deprivation occurs from no act of his own, God would be obliged to command the Imam to appear to this needy person. Because this does not happen, the claim that there is a hidden imam is baseless.

Key Terms
هادي
لطف
اختفاء
مكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The occultation of the Imam is excused because oppressors caused it, so no one is deprived of benefit.
Correction: Razi shows that even if oppressors caused the occultation, the existence of a specific innocent person who cannot reach the Imam means God would have to command the Imam to appear to him, which does not happen, thereby refuting the claim.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the claim that the Imam's occultation is excused by the fault of oppressors?
A) Because oppressors are not responsible
B) Because a specific innocent person cannot reach the Imam, and God should make the Imam appear to him
C) Because the Imam is not actually in occultation
D) Because the benefits of the Imam are not dependent on his presence

Key Takeaway
The argument for a hidden imam collapses because it fails to account for the innocent individual who is deprived of guidance through no fault of his own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have felt that guidance is unavailable to you due to circumstances beyond your control. This lesson argues that God would command the Imam to appear for even one innocent person who cannot reach him. Have you been using external obstacles as an excuse to avoid actively seeking the truth? What step will you take today to personally seek guidance without waiting for a hidden figure?
?
The objector blames the oppressors for the Imam's occultation, but the answer shifts the focus to the individual's responsibility. Where in your spiritual life do you shift blame to others (society, leaders, environment) for your own failure to benefit from divine guidance? How will this lesson change your accountability?
?
If you were that mukallaf who could not reach the Imam through no fault of your own, would you still believe that God has not abandoned you? This lesson says God would act to bring the Imam to you. Does your belief in God's justice and care align with this? What does that demand of your trust in God's plan for your guidance?

Definition of Lutf and the Imam as Lutf
Al-Murtada defines lutf and asserts the imam is a lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of lutf (grace) in kalam and why Al-Sharif al-Murtada argues that the appointment of an Imam is a lutf.

Core Idea
Lutf is whatever brings a person closer to obedience without compulsion.

Al-Sharif al-Murtada opens his argument by defining a key concept: lutf (grace). He explains that lutf is something that God knows will make a responsible person more likely to obey and avoid sin, provided it does not force them. This definition is critical because it sets the terms for the debate on whether God must appoint an Imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Focus on how al-Murtada lays out his thesis and defines lutf.
Notice the careful condition that lutf must not reach compulsion (ilja').
2
Understand the definition of lutf
Al-Murtada defines lutf as something that makes obedience more likely without forcing it. Identify the key elements: God's knowledge, the mukallaf's state, and the condition of no compulsion.
This definition is crucial for the entire argument.
3
See why the imam is a lutf
Al-Murtada argues that an imam acts as an overriding authority (qahir) that deters sin and commands obedience, thereby making people's state more obedient. Reflect on why this qualifies as lutf.
He claims the truth of this is known by empirical observation (istiqra' al-'adat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الشريف المرتضى على أنه يجب على الله نصب الإمام بأن قال : نصب الإمام لطف، واللطف على الله واجب، فيلزم أن يكون نصب الإمام واجبًا على الله تعالى. واعلم أن مرادنا من اللطف الأمر الذي علم الله تعالى من حال المكلف أنه وجد ذلك الأمر كان حاله إلى قبول الطاعات والاحتراز عن المعاصي أقرب مما إذا لم يوجد ذلك الأمر، بشرط أن لا ينتهي إلى حد الإلجاء. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنما قلنا إن نصب الإمام لطف لأن الخلق إذا كان لهم قاهر يمنعهم عن المعاصي، ويأمرهم بالطاعات كان حالهم في القرب من الطاعات والبعد عن المعاصي أكمل مما إذا لم يكن لهم مثل هذا الرئيس، والعلم بصحة ما ذكرناه بعد استقراء العادات ضروري. وإنما قلنا : إن اللطف واجب على الله تعالى لوجهين : الأول : أن من اتخذ ضيافة الإنسان وعلم أن ذلك الإنسان لا يحضر في الضيافة إلا إذا ذهب إليه المضيف بنفسه، والتمس منه الحضور فإن صدق فيما قال أنه يريد حضور ذلك الإنسان في ضيافته وجب أن يذهب إليه بنفسه ويلتمس منه الحضور، وإن لم يذهب إليه ولم يلتمس منه الحضور مع علمه بأنه إن لم يفعل ذلك لم يحضر علمنا أنه ما كان يريد حضور ذلك الإنسان في ضيافته، فكذا ههنا أنه تعالى لو أراد من العبد فعل الطاعات والاجتناب عن المحظورات وعلم انه لا يقدم العبد على ذلك الفعل إلا إذا نصب الله تعالى له إمامًا وجب أن تكون تلك الإرادة مستلزمة لإرادة نصب الإمام فإن لم يرد هذا امتنع كونه مريدا لتلك الطاعات. الثاني: أن فعل اللطف إزالة لعذر المكلف، فوجب أن يكون واجبًا قياسًا على التمكين. والجواب عنه من وجوه كثيرة ذكرناها في نهاية العقول وفي كتاب المحصول في الأصول" ونذكر ههنا نكتا كافية في هذا الباب :
Al-Sharif al-Murtada argued that it is obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam, saying: The appointment of an Imam is a grace (lutf), and grace is obligatory upon God, so it follows that the appointment of an Imam is obligatory upon God. Know that our meaning of lutf is that matter which God knows that if it exists, the responsible person's state is closer to accepting obedience and refraining from sins than if that matter did not exist, provided it does not reach the point of compulsion (ilja'). If you understand this, we say: We said that the appointment of an Imam is a lutf because if people have an overriding authority (qahir) that prevents them from sins and commands them to obedience, their state in closeness to obedience and distance from sins is more complete than if they did not have such a leader. Knowledge of the correctness of what we mentioned after empirical survey (istiqra' al-'adat) is necessary. We said that lutf is obligatory upon God for two reasons: First: If a person prepares a hospitality and knows that that person will not attend the hospitality unless the host goes to him himself and asks him to attend, then if he is truthful in saying that he wants that person to attend his hospitality, he must go to him himself and ask him to attend. If he does not go to him and does not ask him to attend, knowing that if he does not do that he will not attend, we know that he did not want that person to attend his hospitality. Similarly here: If God wants from the servant to perform acts of obedience and avoid prohibitions, and He knows that the servant will not perform that act unless God appoints an Imam for him, then that desire must necessitate the desire to appoint the Imam. If He does not will this, it is impossible that He wills those acts of obedience. Second: That performing the lutf removes the excuse of the responsible person, so it must be obligatory by analogy with enabling (tamkin). The answer to it is through many aspects that we have mentioned in Nihayat al-'Uqul and in the book al-Mahsul fi al-Usul, and we will mention here sufficient points in this chapter.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents the lutf argument for the obligation of an imam from al-Sharif al-Murtada, followed by Razi's presentation of the argument and a brief refutation. It is a combined objection and answer. Al-Murtada argues: imam is a lutf; lutf is obligatory upon God; therefore imam is obligatory. Razi defines lutf as something that makes a responsible person more inclined to obedience without coercion. He then explains why imam is a lutf: an authoritative leader makes people more obedient. Then two reasons why lutf is obligatory: (1) If God truly wants obedience, He must provide the necessary means, like a host who truly wants a guest must personally invite him. (2) Lutf removes excuses, analogous to tamkin (enabling). Razi then states that the answer has been given in his other works.

Key Terms
لطف
إلجاء
قاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lutf means forcing someone to obey.
Correction: Al-Murtada explicitly states that lutf must not reach the level of compulsion (ilja'), it only makes obedience more likely.

Checkpoint
According to al-Murtada, what condition must lutf not reach?
A) Compulsion (ilja')
B) Obligation (wujub)
C) Ignorance (jahl)
D) Disobedience (ma'siya)

Key Takeaway
Al-Murtada defines lutf as a non-coercive means that makes obedience more likely and claims that an Imam is such a lutf, supported by empirical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the role of an Imam as a 'qahir' (overriding authority) that deters you from sin. In your daily life, do you seek out or resist such authority? What is one specific area where you need to submit to divine guidance through a legitimate leader, and what concrete step will you take this week to do so?
?
Al-Murtada says that it is empirically obvious that having an authority makes people more obedient. Look at your own life: when you are accountable to someone (a teacher, parent, or spiritual guide), do you act differently? Does this recognition move you to thank God for the Imam as a grace (lutf)? Write one sincere prayer asking God to help you truly value this grace.
?
The lutf is provided without compulsion—you can still choose to disobey. Does this knowledge make you more grateful or more negligent? Tonight, recall a moment today when you were aware of divine guidance but chose otherwise. What does that say about your heart? Commit to one change in your response to divine guidance tomorrow.

Two Proofs that Lutf is Obligatory
Al-Murtada presents two reasons why lutf is obligatory upon God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments Al-Murtada uses to prove that lutf is obligatory upon God, and how they are grounded in analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
If God truly wants obedience, He must provide the means that make it more likely.

Having defined lutf and shown that the imam is a lutf, Al-Murtada now needs to prove that any lutf is obligatory upon God. He offers two proofs: one using the analogy of a host and guest, and another by analogy with tamkin (enabling). These proofs aim to show that God's will for obedience must translate into action.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs
Read the passage carefully. Al-Murtada presents two proofs for the obligation of lutf. Identify each proof and its structure.
The passage ends with Razi's note that the answer will come later.
2
Understand the first proof: host-guest analogy
The first proof draws an analogy: a host who truly wants a guest to attend must personally invite him if that is necessary. Similarly, if God truly wants obedience, He must provide the lutf (imam) if it is necessary.
This shows the connection between will and action.
3
Understand the second proof: analogy with tamkin
The second proof argues that lutf removes the excuse of the mukallaf, similar to tamkin (enabling), which is considered obligatory. Thus lutf is also obligatory.
Tamkin is the idea that God provides the ability to act.
4
Note Razi's response
Razi states that he has answered these proofs in his other works and will provide a summary in this chapter. This shows the ongoing debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting the Hidden Imam: A Rational Argument
Al-Razi answers that the hidden Imam should appear to the needy, and the hadith has no basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's rational argument against the hidden Imam and its reliance on divine justice.

Core Idea
A hidden Imam contradicts God's justice by depriving the innocent of guidance.

Al-Razi now tackles a key objection: how can an Imam be hidden if people need his guidance? He presents a crisp argument showing that a truly hidden Imam would be unjust, and thus the hadith claiming his concealment cannot be authentic. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: condition, conclusion, empirical check.
The word 'ذلك' at the end may be a separator or remnant; it does not affect the argument.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Al-Razi lays out a scenario: a specific responsible individual (mukallaf) does no act to make the Imam fear him, and then cannot reach the Imam by any means. Understand why this leads to a problem.
3
Understand the divine obligation
From that predicament, Al-Razi deduces that God must command the Imam to show himself to this needy person. Ask yourself: why is that logically necessary?
This connects to God's justice and the purpose of imamate.
4
Grasp the empirical refutation
Since we see no such appearance, Al-Razi concludes the hadith about the hidden Imam has no basis. Notice how he uses observable reality to reject a theological claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون فالجواب : أن هذا المكلف المعين إذا لم يفعل فعلا البتة يوجب أن يصير الإمام خائفًا منه، ثم إنه بقي بحال لا يمكنه الوصول إلى هذا الإمام بشيء من الطرق، فقد صار محرومًا عن هذا الانتفاع لا بأمر صدر منه، فكان يجب على الله تعالى أن يأمر الإمام بأن يظهر نفسه لهذا المحتاج ولما لم يوجد شيء من علمنا أنه لا أصل لهذا الحديث. ذلك
The thirty-ninth question. The answer: that this specific responsible individual if he does not do an action at all that would cause the Imam to become fearful of him, then he remains in a state wherein he cannot reach this Imam by any means, so he has been deprived of this benefit not by any act on his part, so it would be incumbent upon God Almighty to command the Imam to show himself to this needy one. And since nothing has been found, from our knowledge it has no basis in this hadith. That.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a response to a previous objection concerning the hidden Imam. Al-Razi argues that if a specific individual (mukallaf) does not perform any action that would make the Imam fear him, and then remains unable to reach the Imam by any means, he is deprived of benefit through no fault of his own. Therefore, God should command the Imam to reveal himself to that needy person. Al-Razi then concludes that since we find no such occurrence, we know the hadith (claiming the Imam is hidden) has no foundation. The passage thus refutes the notion of an absent Imam by highlighting God's justice and the obligation to make guidance accessible.

Key Terms
مكلف
إمام
انتفاع
حديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage argues that an Imam is not needed at all or that the Imam cannot be hidden under any circumstances.
Correction: Al-Razi does not deny the possibility of a hidden Imam per se; he refutes a specific hadith that claims the Imam is hidden, by showing that such concealment would be unjust if individuals are deprived of benefit through no fault of their own.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi in this passage, what must God do if a responsible individual cannot reach the Imam through no fault of his own?
A) God must punish the individual for his ignorance.
B) God must command the Imam to show himself to that needy person.
C) God must make the individual fear the Imam.
D) God must reveal the hadith to be authentic.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses a conditional argument and empirical observation to show that a hadith claiming the Imam is hidden cannot be true, because it would contradict divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were standing before God and had to justify your reliance on a teaching that you knew had a weak basis, what would you say? Are there any beliefs you hold that, like the hidden Imam hadith, might crumble under rational scrutiny?
?
Al-Razi uses empirical observation (the Imam does not appear) to dismantle a claim. Think of a belief you have about religion that you have never tested against reality. Are you willing to examine it honestly, even if it means letting go of something comfortable?

Al-Murtada's Proof for the Imam through Lutf
Al-Razi presents al-Murtada's argument that appointing an Imam is a lutf and thus obligatory on God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Murtada's argument that appointing an Imam is a lutf and thus obligatory on God, including the definition of lutf and the two supporting proofs.

Core Idea
The Imam is a divine grace (lutf) necessary for obedience—without compulsion.

Al-Razi presents the argument of al-Sharif al-Murtada, a prominent Shia theologian, for the necessity of the Imam. This argument rests on the concept of lutf (grace), a key term in Mu'tazili and Shia theology. Understanding this argument requires grasping both the definition of lutf and the analogies used to prove its obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Read the Arabic passage once and note its overall structure: a claim, a definition, an application, and two proofs.
The passage is a complete argument for the necessity of the Imam.
2
Understand the definition of lutf
Focus on the definition of lutf: it is something that brings a responsible individual closer to obedience without reaching compulsion. Note the condition 'لا ينتهي إلى حد الإلجاء'.
This distinguishes lutf from coercion.
3
Examine the first proof: the banquet analogy
Consider the analogy of a host who truly wants a guest to attend a banquet: he must go and invite him personally. Similarly, if God truly wants obedience, He must provide the lutf of an Imam.
The analogy relies on the sincerity of the will.
4
Examine the second proof: removal of excuse
The second proof argues that lutf removes the excuse of the responsible individual, making it obligatory by analogy to tamkin (empowerment), which is agreed to be obligatory.
Tamkin is the act of giving ability without excuse.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about how this argument fits into the broader debate on Imamah. Notice that it uses rational principles (lutf, removal of excuse) to establish a religious obligation.
This is a standard kalam argument structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الشريف المرتضى على أنه يجب على الله نصب الإمام بأن قال : نصب الإمام لطف، واللطف على الله واجب، فيلزم أن يكون نصب الإمام واجبًا على الله تعالى. واعلم أن مرادنا من اللطف الأمر الذي علم الله تعالى من حال المكلف أنه وجد ذلك الأمر كان حاله إلى قبول الطاعات والاحتراز عن المعاصي أقرب مما إذا لم يوجد ذلك الأمر، بشرط أن لا ينتهي إلى حد الإلجاء. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنما قلنا إن نصب الإمام لطف لأن الخلق إذا كان لهم قاهر يمنعهم عن المعاصي، ويأمرهم بالطاعات كان حالهم في القرب من الطاعات والبعد عن المعاصي أكمل مما إذا لم يكن لهم مثل هذا الرئيس، والعلم بصحة ما ذكرناه بعد استقراء العادات ضروري. وإنما قلنا : إن اللطف واجب على الله تعالى لوجهين : الأول: أن من اتخذ ضيافة الإنسان وعلم أن ذلك الإنسان لا يحضر في الضيافة إلا إذا ذهب إليه المضيف بنفسه، والتمس منه الحضور فإن صدق فيما قال أنه يريد حضور ذلك الإنسان في ضيافته وجب أن يذهب إليه بنفسه ويلتمس منه الحضور، وإن لم يذهب إليه ولم يلتمس منه الحضور مع علمه بأنه إن لم يفعل ذلك لم يحضر علمنا أنه ما كان يريد حضور ذلك الإنسان في ضيافته، فكذا ههنا أنه تعالى لو أراد من العبد فعل الطاعات والاجتناب عن المحظورات وعلم انه لا يقدم العبد على ذلك الفعل إلا إذا نصب الله تعالى له إمامًا وجب أن تكون تلك الإرادة مستلزمة لإرادة نصب الإمام فإن لم يرد هذا امتنع كونه مريدا لتلك الطاعات. الثاني: أن فعل اللطف إزالة لعذر المكلف، فوجب أن يكون واجبًا قياسًا على التمكين.
Al-Sharif al-Murtada argued that it is incumbent upon God to appoint the Imam by saying: Appointing the Imam is a lutf (grace), and lutf is obligatory upon God, so it follows that appointing the Imam is obligatory upon God Almighty. Know that our meaning of lutf is the matter which God Almighty knows from the state of the responsible individual that if that matter existed, his state would be closer to accepting acts of obedience and refraining from sins than if that matter did not exist, on condition that it does not reach the point of compulsion. If you understand this, we say: We only said that appointing the Imam is a lutf because if the creation has a coercer who prevents them from sins and commands them to obedience, their state in closeness to obedience and distance from sins is more complete than if they did not have such a leader, and knowledge of the correctness of what we have mentioned after examining customs is necessary. And we only said that lutf is obligatory upon God Almighty for two reasons: First: He who undertakes a banquet for a person and knows that that person will not attend the banquet unless the host goes to him himself and requests his presence, if he is truthful in his statement that he wants that person to attend his banquet, it is necessary for him to go to him himself and request his presence. And if he does not go to him and does not request his presence despite knowing that if he does not do so he will not attend, we know that he did not want that person to attend his banquet. Likewise here, if God Most High wants from a servant the performance of acts of obedience and avoidance of prohibitions, and knows that the servant will not undertake that action unless God appoints an Imam for him, then that will must entail the will to appoint the Imam. If He does not will this, it is impossible for Him to be willing for those acts of obedience. Second: That the act of lutf removes the excuse of the responsible individual, so it must be obligatory by analogy to tamkin (empowerment).
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of al-Sharif al-Murtada for the necessity of the Imam. It is a definition and proof structure. First, al-Murtada states his premise: appointing an Imam is a lutf, and lutf is obligatory on God, so the appointment is obligatory. Al-Razi then clarifies the definition of lutf: it is something that God knows will make the servant closer to obedience without compulsion. Then he applies it: if people have a coercive leader, they are more prone to obedience. This is known by custom. Then two proofs for the obligation of lutf: the banquet analogy and the removal of excuse analogy. In the first, if a host truly wants a guest, he will go fetch him. Similarly, if God truly wants obedience, He must provide the lutf of an Imam. The second proof compares lutf to tamkin (empowerment), which is agreed to be obligatory as it removes excuses.

Key Terms
لطف
إمام
مكلف
التمكين
إلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think lutf is a form of coercion or compulsion.
Correction: Lutf is explicitly defined as not reaching the limit of compulsion (ilja'). It is a grace that facilitates obedience without forcing the individual.

Checkpoint
According to the definition given, what condition must lutf not reach?
A) The level of obligation
B) The limit of compulsion (ilja')
C) The level of empowerment
D) The limit of necessity

Key Takeaway
Al-Murtada argues that the Imam is a divine grace (lutf) that facilitates obedience, and since God desires obedience, He must provide this grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever sincerely wanted to obey God but found yourself making excuses instead of seeking means like knowledge or good company? What does al-Murtada's banquet analogy say about the sincerity of your desire for obedience?
?
If God must provide lutf for your obedience, then what are you doing with the lutfs He has already sent—like a just leader, a clear Quran, or a wake-up call? Are you treating them as compulsion to resist or as grace to embrace?
?
Al-Murtada argues that lutf removes your excuse before God. What excuses are you still holding onto that this teaching exposes as invalid? Will you let go of them today and take responsibility for your own obedience?

Al-Razi's First Objection: The Appointed Imam is Not a Lutf
Al-Razi's first refutation: the Imam you posit cannot be shown to be a lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first objection to the lutf argument, which exposes a dilemma in the Imamiyya position.

Core Idea
A hidden Imam cannot be a lutf — therefore the argument fails.

Al-Razi now turns to refuting the Imamiyya's argument that appointing an Imam is a lutf (divine grace). He denies this premise directly and constructs a clever dilemma: the only kind of Imam that could genuinely be a lutf is not claimed to exist by the opponents, while the hidden Imam they do claim cannot be a lutf. This passage shows his logical method of exposing inconsistencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure of the objection.
2
Understand the definition of lutf
Al-Razi clarifies what a lutf would require: a coercive, governing Imam whose reward and punishment are effective. This is key to the dilemma.
The Imamiyya's lutf argument originally used this definition.
3
Identify the dilemma
Al-Razi points out that the Imam who fits the lutf description is not claimed to exist by the opponents, while the hidden Imam they claim does not fit the description.
This is a classic 'you cannot have it both ways' argument.
4
See how the argument collapses
Because the opponents cannot have an Imam that is both a lutf and hidden, their proof that appointing the Imam is a lutf fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: لا نسلم أن نصب الإمام لطف. وبيانه هو أن اللطف الذي قررتموه إنما يحصل من نصب إمام قاهر سائس يرجى ثوابه ويخشى عقابه، وأنتم لا تقولون بوجوب نصب مثل هذا الإمام، أما أن الإمام الذي لا له في الدنيا أثر ولا خبر فلا نسلم أنه لطف البتة، فإذن الإمام الذي يمكن بيان كونه لطفا لا توجبون وجوده والذي توجبون وجوده لا يمكن بيان كونه لطفا، فسقط الاستدلال.
First: We do not concede that appointing the Imam is a lutf. Its explanation: The lutf which you have established is only obtained from appointing a coercive, governing Imam whose reward is hoped for and whose punishment is feared, and you do not say that appointing such an Imam is obligatory. As for the Imam who has neither trace nor news in this world, we do not concede that he is a lutf at all. Therefore, the Imam for whom it could be demonstrated that he is a lutf you do not make his existence obligatory, and the one whose existence you make obligatory cannot be demonstrated to be a lutf, so the argument collapses.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is the first objection in al-Razi's refutation of the lutf argument. Al-Razi denies the premise that appointing the Imam is a lutf. He explains that a true lutf would require a powerful, governing Imam who can reward and punish, but the opponents (Imamiyya) do not claim such an Imam exists in the world. Their Imam is hidden and has no trace or news. Such a hidden Imam cannot be a lutf because he cannot exert influence. Thus, there is a dilemma: the Imam that could be a lutf is not required, and the one required is not a lutf, so the proof fails.

Key Terms
لطف
إمام قاهر
سائس
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Al-Razi is denying the need for an Imam altogether.
Correction: He is only denying that the hidden Imam can be considered a lutf, not rejecting the need for an Imam in principle.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what kind of Imam would actually be a lutf?
A) An Imam who is hidden and inaccessible
B) An Imam who is coercive, governing, and whose reward and punishment are effective
C) An Imam who is only spiritual
D) An Imam who does not exist

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first objection exposes the inconsistency in the Imamiyya's lutf argument by showing that the only Imam who could be a lutf is not required to exist, while the one they require cannot be a lutf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that a hidden Imam cannot be a lutf because he has no trace or news. Is there any belief you cling to that, when examined honestly, has no real impact on your life or actions? Will you have the courage to let it go if it lacks effect?
?
Al-Razi points out that the required Imam cannot be proven to be a lutf. Are you holding onto a theological position simply because it is inherited from your community, not because it stands up to scrutiny? What would it cost you to truly test it?
?
The lutf argument fails because the Imam has no worldly effect. Your faith in the unseen—does it produce real change in your intentions and behavior, or is it merely theoretical? What concrete evidence in your life demonstrates that your beliefs are actually a 'lutf' for you?
