Second Objection: Parity of Reasoning on Obligation
Al-Razi's second objection: parity argument against Imam's necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's second objection to the necessity of the Imam, using a parity argument that if the Imam is required for completeness, then many other things would also be required.

Core Idea
If the Imam is necessary for obedience, then infallible judges, armies, and deputies are equally necessary.

Imam al-Razi continues challenging his opponents' argument that God must appoint an Imam because His existence makes obedience more complete. He now uses a powerful parity argument: if completeness justifies obligation, then many other things—like infallible judges, armies, and deputies—would also be obligatory. But his opponents do not claim that God must appoint such figures, so something must be wrong with the original reasoning. Al-Razi then considers two possible explanations for why these others are not required and shows that both could apply equally to the Imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that al-Razi presents an analogy and then two options for why the analogy does not lead to obligation for the other figures.
The passage is dense; reading it twice will help.
2
Identify the parallel cases
Identify that al-Razi lists infallible judges (قضاة معصومون), armies (عساكر معصومة), and deputies (نواب معصومين) as parallel to the Imam. See that he says these also make obedience more complete.
These terms are key: قضاة معصومون, عساكر معصومة, نواب معصومون.
3
Understand the two possible reasons
Al-Razi gives two reasons why infallible judges etc. are not obligatory: 1) only basic empowerment (تمكين) is required, not its perfection; 2) even if they are lutf, they might involve a hidden harm (مفسدة). Notice that he then applies these same reasons to the Imam.
These reasons are the crux of the objection.
4
Grasp the force of the objection
Realize that by parity, the opponent's argument cannot justify the necessity of the Imam; either they must accept many obligatory things, or they must admit that the argument from completeness is insufficient. This undercuts the opponent's position.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانية : كما أن كون الخلق أقرب إلى الطاعة وأبعد عن المعصية أتم وأكمل عند وجود الإمام منه عند عدمه، فكذلك هذه الأحوال أكمل عند وجود القضاة المعصومين والعساكر المعصومة والنواب المعصومين، وأنتم لا توجبون شيئًا من ذلك على الله تعالى، فعدم وجوب هذه الأشياء إما أن يقال: إنه لأجل أن الواجب تحصيل أصل التمكين، وأما تحصيل تكميل التمكين فغير واجب - أو يقال : كون القضاة والعساكر من هذه الوجوه - وإن كان لطفا - إلا أنه ربما اشتمل على وجه خفي من المفسدة لا نعرفه، فلا جرم لا يجب على الله نصب القضاة المعصومين والعساكر المعصومين، وعلى التقديرين فلم لا يجوز مثله في هذه المسألة ؟
Second: Just as the creation being closer to obedience and farther from sin is more complete and perfect when the Imam exists than when he does not, similarly these states are more complete when there are infallible judges, infallible armies, and infallible deputies, and you do not make any of that obligatory upon God Almighty. Then the non-obligation of these things is either said: because what is obligatory is to obtain the basis of empowerment (tamkin), while obtaining the completion of empowerment is not obligatory; or said: that judges and armies being of these aspects—even if they are lutf—perhaps they involve a hidden aspect of corruption (mafsada) that we do not know, so indeed it is not incumbent upon God to appoint infallible judges and infallible armies. On both suppositions, why is the like not permissible in this issue?
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is the second objection. Al-Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the completeness of obedience due to an Imam proves its obligation, then the same would apply to infallible judges, armies, and deputies, which would also complete obedience. But the opponents do not require these from God. The reason for not requiring them is either that only basic empowerment (tamkin) is required, not its perfection, or that these might involve a hidden mafsada. Either explanation can also apply to the Imam, so the argument from completeness is insufficient.

Key Terms
قضاة معصومون
عساكر معصومة
نواب معصومون
تمكين
مفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is denying that the Imam is a lutf at all.
Correction: Al-Razi does not deny that the Imam is a lutf; he challenges the necessity of that lutf by showing that other lutfs are not obligatory.

Checkpoint
What two possible reasons does al-Razi give for why infallible judges and armies are not obligatory, even though they are a lutf?
A) They are too numerous and expensive.
B) Only the basis of tamkin is required, or they may involve a hidden mafsada.
C) They are not actually a lutf because they are not needed.
D) They exist only in the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's parity reasoning shows that if the Imam's completeness were a proof of necessity, then many other things would also be necessary, so the argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about what God must do for you—perhaps that He must grant you a specific blessing or remove a certain trial. Al-Razi shows that what seems good (like an infallible leader) may not become obligatory because of hidden reasons. Is it possible that some of your demands on God are actually based on a partial view of what is best? Will you trust His wisdom even when He does not give what you expected?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between basic empowerment (tamkin) and its perfection. In your own spiritual life, do you often focus on the lack of perfect conditions rather than thanking God for the basic ability to obey? How can you shift your gratitude from demanding more to appreciating what is already given?
?
Consider a time you were convinced that a certain outcome was necessary for your faith. Al-Razi's argument challenges the assumption that more aids to obedience always entail obligation. Can you honestly examine your heart and admit that you might have insisted on something God never promised? What would it look like to surrender that demand today?

The Third Objection: A Lutf Must Be Free from All Corruption
Al-Razi presents a conditional requirement for obligatory lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's third objection that a lutf (divine grace) is only obligatory if it is free from all aspects of corruption, and why mere benefit does not suffice.

Core Idea
Benefit alone does not make something a lutf—it must be pure of all harm.

Al-Razi continues his critique by focusing on the nature of lutf. The pro-Imam argument claimed that appointing an Imam is beneficial and therefore a lutf. But Al-Razi points out a critical flaw: benefit can coexist with corruption. For something to be a lutf obligatory upon God, it must be entirely free from corruption. The opponents have not shown that the Imam is free from all corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the general principle to the specific case.
The phrase 'بد معه' is a shortened form of 'لا بد معه' meaning 'it is necessary with it'.
2
Identify the key condition
Find the sentence that states the condition for a lutf to be obligatory. It says it must be 'خاليا عن جميع جهات المفسدة' (devoid of all aspects of corruption).
This is the core point of the objection.
3
Understand the logical gap
Al-Razi argues that the opponents only showed benefit, not freedom from corruption. Without proving that the Imam is free from all mafsada, the claim of lutf is not established.
This shows the need for a comprehensive evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالثة : أن كون الشيء مشتملا على المصلحة من بعض الوجوه لا يمنع على المفسدة من وجه آخر، والشيء لا يكون لطفا واجبًا على الله تعالى إلا إذا كان خاليا عن جميع جهات المفسدة، فإذن لا يتم القول بأن نصب الإمام لطف بمجرد ما ذكرتم، بد معه من بيان كونه خاليا عن جميع المفسدة، وأنتم ما بينتم ذلك، فإذن لم يثبت أن نصب الإمام لطف.
Third: That a thing comprising benefit in some respects does not prevent it from having corruption in another respect, and a thing is not a lutf obligatory upon God Almighty unless it is devoid of all aspects of corruption. Therefore, the statement that appointing the Imam is a lutf is not established merely by what you mentioned; there must be with it a demonstration that it is devoid of all corruption, and you have not demonstrated that. So, it is not established that appointing the Imam is a lutf.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is the third objection. Al-Razi points out that something can have benefit (maslaha) in some aspects but also corruption (mafsada) in others. A lutf is only obligatory if it is free from all mafsada. The opponents have only shown that the Imam has some benefit, not that he is free from all possible mafsada. Therefore, they have not proven that his appointment is a lutf. This objection highlights the need for a comprehensive evaluation of the Imam's impact.

Key Terms
مصلحة
مفسدة
لطف
خالٍ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any beneficial thing is a lutf obligatory upon God.
Correction: A lutf is obligatory only if it is free from all aspects of corruption, not merely because it has some benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what must be demonstrated for something to be a lutf obligatory upon God?
A) That it has some benefit
B) That it is free from all aspects of corruption
C) That it is desired by the people
D) That it is possible to achieve

Key Takeaway
A lutf is obligatory only if it is entirely free from corruption; mere benefit is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What hidden corruption might you be ignoring in something you currently believe is purely beneficial for your faith? How can you apply this lesson's standard of requiring complete purity before assuming God's obligation?
?
Think of a time you expected God to provide something because it seemed good, but it didn’t happen. Could it be that He saw a corruption you missed? How does this passage change your trust in His wisdom?
?
In your own judgments of people or situations, do you ever focus only on the benefits while overlooking harms? What would it cost you to adopt the thorough evaluation this lesson demands—examining everything for hidden corruption?

The Basis of Obligation: Preponderant Opinion (Zann) in Determining Lutf
Al-Razi responds that the Mu'tazila's acceptance of knowledge as lutf is based on preponderant opinion, not certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that preponderant opinion (zann) suffices for humans to judge something as a lutf and thus obligatory.

Core Idea
For humans, preponderant opinion (zann) stands in place of certainty in determining obligation.

This passage presents al-Razi's response to a Mu'tazili objection. The Mu'tazila argue that if the possibility of corruption (mafsada) prevents something from being considered a lutf, then the same would apply to knowledge of God. Al-Razi counters by clarifying that human obligation is based on preponderant opinion (zann) when one sees an aspect of goodness and no known evil. This distinction preserves the obligation to acquire ma'rifa while rejecting the hidden Imam's lutf status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: objection, then response with a distinction.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili objection
Find why the Mu'tazila think al-Razi's earlier argument applies equally to knowledge of God. They claim that if the possibility of corruption negates lutf, then ma'rifa cannot be lutf either.
3
Analyze al-Razi's reply
Al-Razi distinguishes between certainty and preponderant opinion. For humans, zann suffices for obligation when there is an aspect of goodness and no known evil. This shows why ma'rifa is obligatory while the hidden Imam is not.
The key phrase: 'والظن في حقنا قائم مقام العلم'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إنا في هذا المقام إنما نتكلم مع المعتزلة، وهم يسلمون لنا أن المعرفة إنما يجب تحصيلها علينا لكونها لطفا في أداء الواجبات العقلية، فإن كان مجرد تجویز اشتمال الشيء على المفسدة يمنع من الحكم بكونه لطفا وجب أيضًا أن لا يحكموا يكون المعرفة لطفا ؛ لأن هذا الاحتمال قائم فيها. لأنا نقول : الفرق ظاهر، وذلك لأن المعرفة لطف يجب علينا تحصيلها، ونحن إذا عرفنا في الشيء كونه مشتملا على جهة من جهات الحسن، ولم نعرف فيه جهة من جهات القبح غلب على ظننا كونه لطفا، والظن في حقنا قائم قائم مقام العلم في كونه سببًا لوجوب الفعل علينا، فلا جرم كفى هذا القدر في أنه يجب علينا تحصيل معرفة الله تعالى.
If it is said: We in this discussion are speaking with the Mu'tazila, and they concede to us that knowledge (ma'rifa) is only obligatory for us to acquire because it is a lutf in performing the rational duties. If mere possibility that something involves corruption prevents judging that it is a lutf, then they should also not judge that knowledge is a lutf, because that possibility exists in it. We say: The difference is clear. That is because knowledge is a lutf whose acquisition is obligatory upon us, and when we know of a thing that it comprises an aspect of goodness and we do not know in it an aspect of evil, our preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) is that it is a lutf. And opinion (zann) for us stands in the place of knowledge in being a cause for the obligation of the act upon us. Therefore, this much suffices for the obligation upon us to acquire knowledge of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is al-Razi's response to a potential counter-argument from the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila might say that if the possibility of mafsada prevents something from being a lutf, then the same applies to knowledge of God (ma'rifa), which they consider a lutf. Al-Razi replies that for us (humans), we act on preponderant opinion (zann). When we see an aspect of goodness in something and no known evil, we deem it a lutf. Our obligation is based on this opinion, not certainty. Therefore, knowledge of God is obligatory for us because we have no known mafsada in it. This does not apply to the hidden Imam, for which we have strong indications of mafsada. Thus the analogy fails.

Key Terms
معرفة
لطف
ظن
حسن
قبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi denies that humans can be obligated based on opinion; he demands certainty.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that for humans, preponderant opinion (zann) stands in place of knowledge as a cause for obligation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the basis for humans to judge something as a lutf and therefore obligatory upon themselves?
A) Certain knowledge that it is free from all corruption
B) Preponderant opinion (zann) after seeing its goodness and no known evil
C) The consensus of scholars
D) Direct divine command

Key Takeaway
Preponderant opinion (zann) based on an aspect of goodness and no known evil suffices for humans to judge something as a lutf and therefore obligatory upon them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious obligation you act upon daily. Is your certainty about its necessity based on clear proof, or on a preponderant opinion that it is good and has no known harm? How comfortable are you with that basis?
?
In your own life, when have you avoided an act of obedience because you feared it might lead to some harm, even though you saw its goodness? How does Al-Razi's argument challenge you to rely on preponderant opinion rather than demand certainty?
?
If preponderant opinion (zann) is enough for obligation, what does that say about your excuses for neglecting duties like seeking knowledge of God? Where have you hidden behind the demand for certainty to avoid taking action?

Fifth Objection: Lutf in Shar'iyyat vs. 'Aqliyyat and the Problem of Internal Motives
Razi argues that an imam cannot be a lutf for rational duties because it cannot affect internal motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth objection against the Twelver claim that appointing an imam is a lutf, and why he argues that the imam cannot affect the internal motives required for rational obligations.

Core Idea
Even if an imam is a lutf, he cannot change what is inside your heart.

In this objection, Imam Razi challenges the Twelver argument that appointing an imam is a divine grace (lutf) that facilitates obedience. He presents a dilemma: either the imam is a lutf for legal duties or for rational duties. Razi systematically dismantles both possibilities, showing that the imam's influence on internal motives is neither necessary nor provable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Razi forces the opponent to choose between two domains: legal (shar'iyyat) or rational ('aqliyyat). Pay attention to how this frames the entire objection.
The dilemma is exhaustive: the imam's lutf must apply to one of these two categories.
2
Analyze the legal branch
If the imam is a lutf for legal duties, then since legal duties can be absent in certain times, the imam can also be absent. This undermines the necessity of an imam.
Razi uses the opponent's own principle of lutf against them.
3
Examine the rational branch
If the imam is a lutf for rational duties, two sub-possibilities arise: either the imam helps bring actions into existence regardless of motive (useless), or he helps motivate the person to act for the right reason.
Razi quickly dismisses the first sub-possibility as irrelevant.
4
Grasp the psychological argument
Razi argues that external inducements like fear or praise may backfire, causing aversion to good acts and desire for forbidden things. Thus, the imam's influence on motives is unprovable and likely counterproductive.
This is a key insight: internal motives are not easily manipulated by external authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة: أنكم إما أن تدعوا بأن نصب الإمام لطف في الشرعيات أو في العقليات، فإن كان الأول بطل قولكم؛ لأن خلو الزمان عن التكاليف الشرعية جائز، فالإمام الذي هو لطف فيه أولى أن يجوز خلو الزمان عنه، وإن كان الثاني فنقول: إما أن تدعوا أن الإمام لطف في إدخال تلك الواجبات العقلية في الوجود سواء كان إدخالها في الوجود لأجل وجود وجوبها أو لهذا الوجه، وإما أن تقولوا بأن الإمام لطف في أن يدخلها المكلف في الوجود لأجل وجه وجوبها، والأول باطل؛ لأن إدخالها في الوجود لا لأجل وجه وجوبها لا عبرة به البتة، فلم يبق إلا أن تدعوا أن الإمام لطف في أن يدخل المكلف الواجبات العقلية في الوجود لأجل وجه وجوبها. وهذا مما لا يمكنكم أن تذكروا في تقريره شيئا، وذلك لأن إدخال الفعل في الوجود لأجل وجه وجوبه عبارة عن كيفية من كيفيات الدواعي القائمة بالقلوب، ومن أين يمكن إثبات أن نصب الإمام أبدا له أثر في هذه الكيفيات؟ بل لو قيل: إن الأمر بالعكس لكان أولى؛ لأن الممنوع متبوع، فإذا صار الإنسان محمودا على فعل من الأفعال بالتخويف صار متنفرا عنه، وإذا صار ممنوعا عن الشيء صار راغبا فيه. فثبت أن هذه التمويهات التي يذكرها هؤلاء الإثنا عشرية تميهات محضة، وأنه متى بحث عن محل الخلاف على التعيين لا يمكنهم أن يذكروا في تقرير مذهبهم بعد التلخيص خيالا عن حجة.
Fifth: You either claim that appointing an imam is a lutf in legal matters (shar'iyyat) or in rational matters ('aqliyyat). If the first, your statement is invalid because the time being free from legal obligations is permissible, so the imam who is a lutf in them is more fitting that time be free from him. If the second, we say: Either you claim that the imam is a lutf in bringing those rational obligations (al-wajibat al-'aqliyya) into existence, whether that bringing into existence is for the sake of the existence of their obligation or for this aspect; or you say that the imam is a lutf in that the obligated person brings them into existence for the sake of the aspect of their obligation. The first is false because bringing them into existence not for the sake of the aspect of their obligation is of no account at all. So nothing remains except that you claim that the imam is a lutf in that the obligated person brings the rational obligations into existence for the sake of the aspect of their obligation. This is something for which you cannot mention anything in its establishment, because bringing an action into existence for the sake of the aspect of its obligation is a description of the types of motives (dawa'i) present in hearts. How can it be proven that appointing an imam ever has an effect on these types of motives? Rather, if it were said that the opposite is more likely, because what is forbidden is followed, thus if a person is praised for an action by intimidation, he becomes averse to it; and if he is prevented from something, he becomes desirous of it. So it is established that these sophistries which these Twelvers mention are pure sophistries, and that when the locus of disagreement is examined specifically, they cannot mention, after summarization, even an illusion of a proof for their doctrine.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This is the fifth and longest objection. Razi presents a dilemma: the opponents must claim that the imam is a lutf either in legal (shar'iyya) or rational ('aqliyya) duties. If legal, then since legal obligations can be absent in certain times, the imam being a lutf for them can also be absent, so no obligation. If rational, then the opponents must specify whether the imam helps in bringing the actions into existence regardless of motive, or specifically in motivating the person to perform them for the sake of the obligation. The first is useless because actions not done for the right motive are worthless. The second is unprovable because the imam's influence on internal motives (دواعي القلوب) cannot be demonstrated. Moreover, Razi suggests the opposite may be true: fear-based motivation can backfire, making people averse to good acts and desirous of forbidden things. Razi concludes that all these arguments are mere sophistries (تمويهات), and the Twelvers cannot produce a real proof for their doctrine.

Key Terms
شرعيات (legal matters)
عقليات (rational matters)
دواعي (motives)
تمويهات (sophistries)
إثنا عشرية (Twelvers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that an external authority figure like an imam can directly instill good motives and that fear-based commands always work.
Correction: Razi argues that external inducements may actually produce aversion, not motivation, and that internal motives are not directly influenced by appointing an imam.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's fifth objection regarding rational obligations?
A) The imam is unnecessary for legal obligations.
B) The imam cannot affect the internal motives needed for performing actions for their own sake.
C) The imam should be hidden.
D) Rational obligations are not dependent on the imam.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fifth objection shows that the claim of imamate as lutf fails because internal motives are beyond the imam's direct influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever check your inner motive? Are you doing it purely for the sake of Allah's command, or are you influenced by the fear of people's judgment or the comfort of routine? How can you cultivate a motive that seeks only the aspect of obligation itself, as Razi describes?
?
Razi argues that external pressure—like intimidation or praise—can actually make you averse to what you are commanded. Think of a time when you felt resentful toward an Islamic obligation because of how it was enforced or expected. How has that affected your relationship with that act? What can you do to reconnect with the intrinsic motivation for that duty?
?
This passage shows that no human authority, not even an imam, can change what is inside your heart. If you are relying on external figures—scholars, leaders, or peers—to fix your spiritual state, what does that say about your own responsibility? What step will you take today to turn your heart towards Allah directly, without intermediaries?

Refuting the Obligation to Appoint an Imam
Razi opens the thirty-ninth issue by refuting the Shia claim that God must appoint an imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument against the Shia claim that appointing an imam is obligatory upon God, and the distinction between an act being obligatory on God and an act being a grace that God freely does.

Core Idea
Obligation on God requires proof of absence of corruption; mere grace does not imply obligation.

In this passage, Imam Razi directly addresses a core sectarian dispute: the Shia doctrine that God must appoint an imam for the community. Razi challenges the logical basis of this claim, arguing that such an obligation cannot be asserted without establishing that the imam is free from all corruption in God's knowledge. He then clarifies that even if a scriptural proof existed, it would only show that God chose to do an act of grace, not that He was obligated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Razi states that to assert an obligation on God, two things are needed: that the act is free from corruption in God's knowledge, and that the act is obligatory. Note that he challenges the opponent to prove either.
2
Understand the hypothetical scriptural proof
Razi then considers the scenario where a scriptural text shows that God appointed an imam. He explains that this would only prove that the act is free from ugliness and is a grace, not that it is obligatory.
3
Grasp the conclusion and the distinction
The key takeaway is the distinction between an act being wajib (obligatory) on God and an act being a lutf (grace) that God freely does. This distinction is central to Razi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
أما أنتم فإنما توجبون نصب الإمام على الله تعالى ولا يمكن الجزم بوجوبه على الله إلا إذا ثبت أنه في علم الله تعالى خالٍ عن جميع جهات المفسدة، فأين أحد البابين بل لو ورد دليل سمعي على أنه تعالى فعل ذلك استدللنا بأن الله تعالى فعله على خلوه عن جميع جهات القبح، وعلى كونه لطفا في نفسه لا على أنه واجب على الله تعالى، فظهر الفرق بين البابين
The thirty-ninth issue: As for you (Shia), you make the appointment of the imam obligatory upon God, but it cannot be conclusively affirmed as obligatory upon God unless it is established that in God's knowledge it is free from all aspects of corruption. So where is (the proof for) either of the two doors? Rather, even if a scriptural proof were adduced that He (God) did that, we would infer that God did it because it is free from all aspects of ugliness and because it is a grace (lutf) in itself, not because it is obligatory upon God. Thus the difference between the two doors is clear.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage opens the thirty-ninth issue by directly addressing the Shia claim that appointing an imam is obligatory upon God. Razi argues that such an obligation cannot be asserted unless it is certain that the imam is free from all aspects of corruption in God's knowledge. He challenges the opponent to produce proof for either of the two premises (that the imam is free from corruption or that such an appointment is obligatory). Razi then says that even if a scriptural proof existed that God did appoint an imam, the inference would be that God did so because it is free from ugliness and is a grace in itself, not because it is a duty upon God. This establishes the key distinction between an act being obligatory upon God and an act being a grace that God freely does.

Key Terms
نصب الإمام
لطف
واجب على الله
جهات المفسدة
قبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Razi rejects the concept of lutf entirely.
Correction: Razi does not reject lutf; he only denies that it is obligatory upon God. Lutf remains a valid theological concept.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what must be established before one can claim that appointing an imam is obligatory upon God?
A) That the imam is a descendant of the Prophet.
B) That the imam is free from all aspects of corruption in God's knowledge.
C) That a scriptural text explicitly commands it.
D) That the imam is the most knowledgeable person.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that appointing an imam cannot be proven obligatory on God; even if God did appoint an imam, it would be a free act of grace, not a duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever assume that God owes you a specific answer? What would it feel like to release that expectation and see every good thing in your life as pure grace, not a debt God had to pay? Commit today to surrender one specific expectation you hold about what God must do for you.
?
Razi distinguishes between what God is obligated to do and what He freely gives as grace. Is there an area of your life where you have felt entitled to God's favor—perhaps because of your worship, charity, or knowledge? Confess that entitlement and ask God to give you a heart that receives everything as lutf.
?
This passage challenges a theological claim about the imamate, but it also exposes a deeper heart issue: the human tendency to put God in a box of our own making. Where in your life have you tried to dictate to God what He should do? How can you actively trust that whatever He does is free from ugliness and is a grace, even when you don't understand it?

Razi's Refutation of the Rarity Objection
Razi refutes the rarity objection by arguing any moment could be the rare moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that an objection based on rarity fails to establish the obligation of appointing an imam, and how this counters the Shia position.

Core Idea
A rare possibility cannot be dismissed if it could be actual at any moment.

In this fourth point, Razi presents a powerful logical refutation against the Shia claim that appointing an imam is always obligatory. He argues that if there is even a rare possibility that a leader causes increased discord, then one can never be certain that appointing a leader is beneficial in any given time. This passage shows how Razi uses the concept of probability to undermine an unconditional obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: Razi makes a claim, anticipates an objection, and then responds. The argument is concise but logically dense.
Pay attention to the key terms: نادر (rare) and عبرة (taken into account).
2
Understand the Shia objection
Identify the objection that Razi anticipates: the scenario of a leader causing discord is rare and rare occurrences are not considered valid evidence. This objection aims to dismiss Razi's earlier point.
3
Analyse Razi's counter-argument
Razi's response is twofold. He grants rarity but argues that every moment could be that rare moment. Therefore, we can never be certain that in any given time it is obligatory to appoint an imam. This destroys the Shia claim of a categorical obligation.
Razi uses the concept of possibility to show that obligation cannot be proven with certainty.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this argument fits into the broader debate. Razi is not denying that a leader might be beneficial; he is attacking the notion that it is always obligatory upon God. The argument shows that rational certainty about divine obligations is hard to achieve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : لا يبعد وجود زمان متى نصب لأهل ذلك الزمان رئيس سائس استنكفوا عن طاعته، فيصير ذلك الرئيس في ذلك الزمان سبيا لازدياد الفتنة. لا يقال : هذا وإن كان محتملاً إلا أنه نادر، والنادر لا عبرة به. لأنا نقول : هب أنه نادر إلا أنه لا زمان إلا ويحتمل أن يكون ذلك الزمان زمانا لذلك النادر، وبتقدير أن يكون كذلك لم يكن نصب الرئيس فيه واجبا، وحينئذ لا يمكنكم أن تقطعوا في شيء من الأزمنة بأنه يجب فيه نصب الإمام الرئيس على الله تعالى
The fourth (point): It is not far-fetched that there exists a time when if a ruling leader is appointed for the people of that time, they would disdain obedience to him, so that leader would become a cause for increased discord. It should not be said: 'This is possible but it is rare, and the rare is not taken into account.' For we say: Granted that it is rare, but there is no time except that it is possible that that time is the time of that rare (occurrence). And if it is thus, then appointing the leader is not obligatory at that time. Therefore you cannot be certain of any time that appointing the imam-leader is obligatory upon God.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is the fourth sub-argument in Razi's refutation. He raises a scenario: it is not unlikely that when a leader is appointed to a people, they become rebellious and the leader causes increased discord. He anticipates an objection that such a scenario is rare and rare occurrences are ignored. Razi responds that even if it is rare, every moment has the potential to be that rare moment, so one can never be certain that in any given time it is obligatory to appoint an imam. This undermines the Shia claim that appointing an imam is always obligatory.

Key Terms
نادر
عبرة
نصب الرئيس
فتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rare is never considered in Islamic reasoning and can be safely ignored.
Correction: Razi shows that when dealing with claims of certainty, even rare possibilities must be considered because they could actually occur at any time. Rareness does not eliminate the possibility.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the objection that a leader causing discord is rare?
A) Because rare events sometimes occur and cannot be ignored.
B) Because any given time could be the time of that rare event, so certainty of obligation is impossible.
C) Because the objection is based on conjecture, not evidence.
D) Because the Shia themselves admit the possibility.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that a rare but possible harm can never be ruled out, thus the obligation to appoint an imam cannot be established with certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a sin you have comfortably dismissed as rare for you, assuming it will never happen? Razi warns that any moment could be that rare moment—what would it cost you to be unprepared? What can you do today to guard against that rare but devastating fall?
?
Razi's argument shows that a rare possibility can invalidate a certainty. Where in your life have you been certain that you would not be tested with a particular trial, only to be caught off guard? How can you live with the awareness that the trial could come at any moment?
?
The rarity objection is comforting because it allows us to ignore potential harms. But Razi insists we cannot. Think of a relationship or duty you have avoided because a rare harm seemed unlikely. Are you using 'it's rare' as an excuse? What would it mean to take that possibility seriously and still act?

Refuting the Imam as Lutf (Grace)
Razi refutes the classification of imam as lutf in either sharia or rational obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical refutation of the Shia claim that the imam is a lutf in either shar'i or rational matters.

Core Idea
The imam's appointment cannot be proven to affect inner motives, so the lutf argument fails.

Imam Razi continues his critique of Twelver Shi'ite theology by examining the claim that the imam is a 'lutf' (grace) from Allah. He uses a logical division to show that whether one considers the imam's role in religious obligations or rational obligations, the argument collapses. This passage demonstrates the precision of kalam reasoning in dismantling unsupported claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: Razi presents two main alternatives and refutes each.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main branches
Razi first asks whether the imam is a lutf for shar'i matters or rational matters. Trace how he refutes the first branch.
The refutation of the first branch is brief: since shar'i obligations can be absent, the imam can also be absent.
3
Examine the rational branch sub-arguments
For rational matters, Razi splits into two possibilities: (a) lutf in bringing obligations into existence regardless of motive, and (b) lutf in helping the obligated person act for the correct motive. See why (a) is rejected and (b) is impossible to prove.
4
Understand the final conclusion
Razi concludes that the Shia arguments are pure delusions because they cannot prove that the imam affects inner motives. Note the psychological point about praise/blame backfiring.
This is a key insight: external inducements can be counterproductive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : أنكم إما أن تدعوا بأن نصب الإمام لطف في الشرعيات أو في العقليات، فإن كان الأول بطل قولكم ؛ لأن خلو الزمان عن التكاليف الشرعية جائز، فالإمام الذي هو لطف فيه أولى أن يجوز خلو الزمان عنه، وإن كان الثاني فنقول: إما أن تدعوا أن الإمام لطف في إدخال تلك الواجبات العقلية في الوجود سواء كان إدخالها في الوجود لأجل وجود وجوبها أو لهذا الوجه وإما أن تقولوا بأن الإمام لطف في أن يدخلها المكلف في الوجود لأجل وجه وجوبها، والأول باطل ؛ لأن إدخالها في الوجود لا لأجل وجه وجوبها لا عبرة به البتة، فلم يبق إلا أن تدعوا أن الإمام لطف في أن يدخل المكلف الواجبات العقلية في الوجود، لأجل وجه وجوبها. وهذا مما لا يمكنكم أن تذكروا في تقريره شيئًا ، وذلك لأن إدخال الفعل في الوجود لأجل وجه وجوبه عبارة عن كيفية من كيفيات الدواعي القائمة بالقلوب، ومن أين يمكن إثبات أن نصب الإمام أبدا له أثر في هذه الكيفيات ؟ بل لو قيل: إن الأمر بالعكس لكان أولى؛ لأن الممنوع متبوع، فإذا صار الإنسان محمودًا على فعل من الأفعال بالتخويف صار متنفرًا عنه، وإذا صار ممنوعا عن الشيء صار راغبا فيه. فثبت أن هذه التمويهات التي يذكرها هؤلاء الإثنا عشرية تميهات محضة، وأنه متى بحث عن محل الخلاف على التعيين لا يمكنهم أن يذكروا في تقرير مذهبهم بعد التلخيص خيالا عن حجة.
The fifth (point): You (Shia) claim that the appointment of the imam is a grace (lutf) either in shar'i matters or in rational matters. If it is the first, your statement is invalid because the absence of shar'i obligations from a time is permissible, so the imam who is a grace for them is all the more likely to be permissible to be absent from a time. If it is the second, we say: Either you claim that the imam is a grace in bringing those rational obligations into existence, whether they are brought into existence because of the existence of their obligatoriness or for this reason, or you say that the imam is a grace for the obligated person to bring them into existence for the sake of the aspect of their obligatoriness. The first is false because bringing them into existence not for the sake of the aspect of their obligatoriness is of no account at all. So only the last remains: that you claim that the imam is a grace for the obligated person to bring rational obligations into existence for the sake of the aspect of their obligatoriness. But this is something for which you cannot present any (proof) in its establishment, because bringing an act into existence for the sake of the aspect of its obligatoriness is a kind of quality of the motives (dawā'ī) residing in hearts. How can it be established that appointing an imam has any effect on these qualities? Rather, if it were said that the opposite is more likely, because a person refrains from what he is praised for out of intimidation, he becomes averse to it, and when he is prevented from something, he becomes desirous of it. Thus it is established that these delusions which these Twelvers mention are pure delusions, and that when the point of disagreement is precisely examined, they cannot, after summarizing, mention even a figment of an argument for their doctrine.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is the fifth sub-argument. Razi uses a logical division to refute the Shia claim that the imam is a lutf (grace). First, he considers whether the imam is a lutf for shar'i matters or rational matters. If for shar'i matters, then since it is permissible for a time to be devoid of shar'i obligations, it is even more permissible to be devoid of the imam who is a lutf for them. So the claim fails. If for rational matters, then he further divides into two possibilities: (1) the imam is a lutf in bringing those obligations into existence regardless of the motive, or (2) the imam is a lutf in helping the obligated person to bring them into existence for the correct motive. The first is invalid because actions done without the correct motive are worthless. The second is what remains, but Razi argues that it is impossible to prove that the imam affects the inner motives (dawā'ī) of the heart. In fact, Razi suggests the opposite is likely: external inducements like praise or blame can backfire, making a person averse to the praised action and desirous of the forbidden. Therefore the Shia arguments are pure delusions, and when the issue is scrutinized, they have no real argument.

Key Terms
لطف
شرعيات
عقليات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi denies the possibility of any lutf altogether.
Correction: Razi does not deny lutf; he only denies that the imam's appointment can be proven to affect the inner motives necessary for righteous action.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the claim that the imam is a lutf for rational obligations in the sense of helping the obligated person act for the correct motive?
A) Because rational obligations do not exist.
B) Because the effect of the imam on inner motives cannot be proven.
C) Because the imam is not obligated to help others.
D) Because the correct motive is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the Shia claim of the imam as lutf is a delusion because it cannot be proven that the imam influences the inner motives necessary for righteous action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when someone's praise or criticism made you less inclined to do what is right. How does Razi's argument that coercion can backfire resonate with that experience? What does this teach you about where your motivation for good deeds should truly come from?
?
Razi says that the imam's effect on inner motives cannot be proven. Consider your own heart: what external factors do you rely on to motivate your faith? Will you still obey Allah when those factors are absent?
?
If the imam is not a lutf, then what is your ultimate source of grace for righteous action? Does your daily practice reflect reliance on that source or on worldly authorities and incentives?

Razi's Dilemma: Lutf and the Preventer
Razi argues that if God knows a preventer exists, the lutf is futile; if no preventer, the effect must occur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that if God knows a preventer exists, lutf is futile, and if no preventer, the effect must occur, and how this undermines the opponent's claim that the imam is lutf.

Core Idea
If a preventer exists, lutf is futile; if not, the effect must occur—either way, the imam is not lutf.

This passage presents Razi's sixth argument against the Shi'i claim that the imam is a 'lutf' (divine grace) that necessitates obedience. Razi considers two scenarios based on God's knowledge of a preventer. The argument shows that if a preventer exists, the lutf is pointless, and if no preventer, the lutf must cause the act, which contradicts reality. This logical dismantling reinforces the Sunni position on the non-necessity of the imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice the two conditional scenarios introduced by 'إما أن'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Identify the two cases God knows: either a preventer exists or it does not. Mark the transition between them.
The first scenario begins with 'فإن كان الأول', the second with 'وإن كان الثاني'.
3
Analyze the logical consequences
For each scenario, understand why Razi concludes that lutf is either not obligatory (first) or that the effect must occur (second).
The first scenario argues futility; the second argues necessity.
4
Connect to the imam argument
Razi applies the dilemma to the claim that the imam is lutf. If the imam were lutf, corruption would be removed. Since it is not, the claim fails.
This step links back to the objective of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة : أن عند حصول هذا اللطف المقرب إما أن يعلن الله تعالى بأنه حصل ما يمنع عن الفعل أو يعلم أنه لم يحصل هذا المانع البتة، فإن كان الأول فلا نسلم انه في العقول فعل مثل هذا اللطف ؛ لأنه إذا كان بتقدير وجوده لا يحصل الفعل كما أن بتقدير عدمه أيضًا لا يحصل لم يكن فعل هذا اللطف واجبًا، بل الفعل يوجب الامتناع منه ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في فعله البتة، وإن كان الثاني وهو أن الله تعالى علم أن هذا اللطف جانب الفعل وعلم أنه لم يحصل معه ما يمنع من الفعل، فمثل هذا يقتضي وجوب حصول الأثر - على ما بيناه في مسألة خلق الأفعال. فلو كان نصب الإمام لطفا بهذا التفسير لزالت المفاسد، وحصلت المصالح لا محالة، ولما لم يكن كذلك وجب أن لا يكون نصب الإمام لطفا.
The sixth (point): When this proximate grace (al-luṭf al-muqarrib) occurs, either God knows that something has occurred that prevents the act, or He knows that this preventer has not occurred at all. If the first, we do not accept that in reason this kind of lutf is performed; because if, assuming its existence, the act does not occur, just as assuming its absence it also does not occur, then performing this lutf is not obligatory; rather, the act (of performing lutf) necessitates refraining from it, because there is no benefit in performing it at all. If the second, that God knows that this lutf encourages the act and knows that no preventer has occurred with it, then such a scenario necessitates the occurrence of the effect (athar) – as we have explained in the issue of the creation of acts. So if appointing the imam were a lutf according to this interpretation, then corruptions would inevitably be removed and benefits would occur. Since that is not the case, it must be that appointing the imam is not a lutf.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is the sixth sub-argument. Razi considers two possibilities regarding God's knowledge when a lutf occurs. First: God knows that a preventer to the act exists. In that case, the lutf is ineffective because the act does not occur whether the lutf exists or not. So performing such a lutf is pointless and not obligatory. Second: God knows there is no preventer, and the lutf encourages the act. In that case, the effect (the act) must occur necessarily, as per Razi's doctrine on the creation of acts. If the imam were such a lutf, then all corruptions would be removed and benefits achieved. But this is not the case in reality, so the imam cannot be a lutf in this sense.

Key Terms
لطف مقرب
مانع
أثر
خلق الأفعال
مفاسد
مصالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi contradicts himself by acknowledging that lutf can have an effect in the second scenario, but he is only analyzing consequences within the opponent's framework.
Correction: Razi does not affirm that lutf necessarily causes acts; he is drawing out the logical implications of the opponent's own definition. If the opponent claims lutf encourages and no preventer exists, then in Razi's own system, the act must occur. This is used to show the opponent's position leads to an impossible outcome.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the second scenario where God knows no preventer exists?
A) The lutf is unnecessary.
B) The lutf must necessarily cause the act, so if the imam were lutf, corruption would end, but it does not.
C) The lutf is still optional.
D) The lutf is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi's logical dilemma shows that the imam cannot be considered a lutf because the expected outcomes (removal of corruption) do not occur.

God's Knowledge Makes Lutf Redundant
Razi presents a logical dilemma on lutf and God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical dilemma showing that the imam cannot be an obligatory lutf because God's foreknowledge renders it unnecessary or ineffective.

Core Idea
If God knows it will happen, lutf is redundant; if He knows it won't, lutf is futile.

Imam Razi continues his critique of the Shia doctrine that appointing an imam is a lutf (grace) obligatory upon God. In this seventh point, he presents a devastating logical dilemma based on God's foreknowledge. If God knows an action will occur, it is necessitated and needs no lutf; if He knows it won't, it is impossible and no lutf can cause it. Let's unpack this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: if God knows X, then Y.
The argument is a simple but powerful dichotomy.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either God knows the action will occur, or He knows it will not occur. See how each leads to a different conclusion about the lutf.
3
Understand the conclusion
In both cases, the lutf is either unnecessary or ineffective. Therefore, the claim that the imam is a necessary lutf is false.
This refutes the Shia position that appointing an imam is obligatory upon God.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how God's foreknowledge affects the necessity of means. This argument does not deny causality, but shows that a lutf cannot be obligatory if God's knowledge already determines the outcome.
Avoid the misconception that this is a fatalist denial of means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابعة : هذا الذي يجعل نصب الإمام لطفا فيه إن كان الله تعالى عالما بوقوعه كان واجب الوقوع فلا حاجة به إلى هذا اللطف وإن كان عالما بعدم وقوعه كان ممتنع الوقوع، فلم يكن للطف فيه أثر البتة.
The seventh (point): This (action) for which the appointment of the imam is made a lutf – if God is knowing of its occurrence, then it is necessarily occurring, so there is no need for this lutf; and if He is knowing of its non-occurrence, then it is impossible to occur, so the lutf has no effect at all.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is the seventh sub-argument, a concise logical dilemma. Razi argues that the action for which the imam is supposed to be a lutf is either known by God to occur or not to occur. If God knows it will occur, then it is necessitated and does not need the lutf. If God knows it will not occur, then it is impossible, and the lutf cannot make it happen. Either way, the lutf is redundant or ineffective. Therefore, the imam cannot be a necessary lutf.

Key Terms
عالم
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that means (asbāb) are necessary for actions.
Correction: Razi does not deny causality; he only shows that given God's foreknowledge, the specific claim that the imam is a necessary lutf becomes impossible. The argument is specific to the lutf theory.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, if God knows an action will occur, then that action is:
A) Contingent and needs lutf
B) Necessitated and does not need lutf
C) Impossible and cannot occur
D) Possible but lutf is optional

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma shows that God's foreknowledge makes the notion of an obligatory lutf logically impossible: either lutf is unnecessary or ineffective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on certain people, circumstances, or rituals as if they are necessary for your obedience to God, forgetting that God's knowledge has already decreed the outcome? What would it look like for you to trust fully in God's decree and act without that crutch?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you attribute it to some external factor (a teacher, a book, a community) rather than to God's knowledge that made it certain? How can you shift your gratitude entirely to God, knowing that He alone is the cause of all that occurs?
?
Razi's argument shows that if God knows something will happen, nothing else is needed for it to occur. Is there an area of your life where you secretly think you need something more than God's knowledge—like a specific outcome from a person or event—to be safe or successful? Confront that area and ask yourself: Is my trust in God's foreknowledge complete, or do I still seek assurance from other things?

Lutf Is Not Obligatory Upon God
Razi concedes the imam may be a lutf but denies it is obligatory upon God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi concedes the imam may be a lutf but denies there is any obligation upon God, and why this aligns with his broader theological principle that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Core Idea
Razi separates the concept of lutf from divine obligation.

In this final passage on the imamate, Razi makes a strategic concession. He accepts, for the sake of argument, that the imam might be considered a grace (lutf). But he immediately blocks the theological conclusion that such a grace is obligatory upon God. This short paragraph encapsulates his core principle that nothing is binding on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a concession followed by a rebuttal.
2
Identify the concession and rebuttal
Find where Razi says 'we grant' (سلمنا) and where he says 'we do not grant' (لا نسلم). Understand what is being conceded and what is rejected.
The concession is hypothetical, not a full acceptance.
3
Connect to the earlier principle
Razi references an earlier point that 'nothing at all is obligatory upon God.' Recall why this principle is central to his theology.
This principle denies the Shia claim that lutf is wājib.
4
Reflect on the concept of lutf
Consider why the Shia consider lutf important and how Razi's response undermines their argument without rejecting the concept entirely.
Lutf is a means to an end; Razi denies the obligation on God to provide it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن نصب الإمام لطف، لكن لا نسلم أن اللطف واجب، وقد قدمنا ما يدل على أنه لا يجب على الله شيء أصلا ورأسا. فهذا هو الكلام المختصر في هذا الباب.
We grant that the appointment of the imam is a grace (lutf), but we do not grant that the grace is obligatory. And we have already presented what indicates that nothing at all is obligatory upon God. So this is the brief discussion on this topic.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a final concession and rebuttal. Razi hypothetically accepts that the appointment of an imam is a lutf, but he firmly denies that such a lutf is obligatory upon God. He then references his earlier established principle that nothing whatsoever is obligatory upon God (denying the concept of wājib ‘alā Allāh). This serves to summarize his position and conclude the discussion of this issue.

Key Terms
لطف
واجب
لا يجب على الله شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia concept of lutf is inherently obligatory upon God.
Correction: Razi separates the concept of lutf from obligation; even if something is a grace, it does not mean God is required to provide it.

Checkpoint
What does Razi concede and then reject in this final passage?
A) He concedes that an imam is needed, but rejects that it is a lutf.
B) He concedes that the imam is a lutf, but rejects that lutf is obligatory upon God.
C) He concedes that lutf is obligatory, but rejects that the imam is a lutf.
D) He concedes nothing and rejects everything.

Key Takeaway
Razi maintains that even if the imam is a grace, there is no obligation upon God to provide it, upholding his core principle that nothing is obligatory for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or ask God for something, do you feel He is obliged to answer? How does Razi's assertion that nothing is obligatory upon God challenge that feeling, and what will you change in your attitude toward du'a starting today?
?
Think of a time you felt God 'owed' you something—a reward for your good deed, or a favor for your sacrifice. In light of this lesson, is that expectation justified? Will you release that expectation and instead worship with pure gratitude?
?
If God is not obliged to give you even a single grace (lutf), what does that mean for your sense of entitlement? How will you remind yourself daily that every blessing is a pure gift, not a debt?

Refutation of the Lutf Argument: The Sixth Point
Razi presents the sixth argument analyzing the logical conditions of lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical analysis of how divine knowledge of preventers undermines the claim that the Imam's appointment is a grace.

Core Idea
If a grace doesn't cause the action, it's futile; if it does, it necessitates the action.

Razi continues his refutation of the lutf argument by dissecting the very concept of grace. He shows that divine foreknowledge of preventers renders the grace either ineffective or redundant. This logical move challenges the necessity of the Imam as a divine grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
Razi presents two scenarios: either God knows a preventer exists or He knows no preventer exists. Trace each branch.
3
Understand the consequence of each branch
In the first branch, the grace is futile and not obligatory. In the second, the grace necessitates the action, which would require the Imam to achieve perfect benefits.
4
Apply to the Imam case
Since in reality evils are not removed by the Imam's appointment, the second branch fails. Therefore, the appointment cannot be a grace in the required sense.
5
Connect to broader kalam debate
This argument is part of Razi's critique of the lutf proof. Reflect on how it fits into Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة : أن عند حصول هذا اللطف المقرب إما أن يعلن الله تعالى بأنه حصل ما يجب يمنع عن الفعل أو يعلم أنه لم يحصل هذا المانع البتة، فإن كان الأول فلا نسلم انه في العقول فعل مثل هذا اللطف ؛ لأنه إذا كان بتقدير وجوده لا يحصل الفعل كما أن يرجع بتقدير عدمه أيضًا لا يحصل لم يكن فعل هذا اللطف واجبًا، بل الفعل يوجب الامتناع منه ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في فعله البتة، وإن كان الثاني وهو أن الله تعالى علم أن هذا اللطف جانب الفعل وعلم أنه لم يحصل معه ما يمنع من الفعل، فمثل هذا يقتضي وجوب حصول الأثر - على ما بيناه في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
فلو كان نصب الإمام لطفا بهذا التفسير لزالت المفاسد، وحصلت المصالح لا محالة، ولما لم يكن كذلك وجب أن لا يكون نصب الإمام لطفا. السابعة : هذا الذي يجعل نصب الإمام لطفا فيه إن كان الله تعالى عالما بوقوعه كان واجب الوقوع فلا حاجة به إلى هذا اللطف وإن كان عالما بعدم وقوعه كان ممتنع الوقوع، فلم يكن للطف فيه أثر البتة.
سلمنا أن نصب الإمام لطفا، لكن لا نسلم أن اللطف واجب، وقد قدمنا ما يدل على أنه لا يجب على الله شيء أصلا ورأسا.
فهذا هو الكلام المختصر في هذا الباب.
The sixth: That when this proximate grace occurs, either God knows that what must prevent the action has occurred, or He knows that this preventer has not occurred at all. If the first, then we do not concede that in the intellects [there is] an action like this grace; because if by the estimation of its existence the action does not occur, just as by the estimation of its non-existence it also does not occur, then this grace is not obligatory; rather, the action necessitates refraining from it because there is no benefit in it at all. And if the second, which is that God knows that this grace accompanies the action and knows that there is no preventer with it, then such a thing necessitates the occurrence of the effect - according to what we explained in the issue of the creation of actions.
If the appointment of the Imam were a grace in this interpretation, then evils would necessarily be removed and benefits would be attained; but since it is not so, it is necessary that the appointment of the Imam is not a grace. The seventh: That which makes the appointment of the Imam a grace: if God knows its occurrence, then it is necessarily occurring, so there is no need for this grace; and if He knows its non-occurrence, then it is impossible, so there is no effect for the grace in it at all.
We concede that the appointment of the Imam is a grace, but we do not concede that the grace is obligatory. And we have already presented what indicates that nothing is obligatory upon God at all, absolutely.
This is the concise discourse on this chapter.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that appointing an Imam is a required divine grace (lutf). The author presents two points (السادسة and السابعة) and then a concession. First, he analyzes the logic of grace: if God knows that a preventer exists, then the grace is ineffective; if He knows no preventer exists, then the grace necessitates the action, implying that the Imam's grace would guarantee benefits, but since it does not, it is not a true grace. Second, if the Imam were a grace, either God knows it will occur (so it is necessary and needs no grace) or He knows it will not occur (so impossible, and grace is irrelevant). Finally, he concedes the Imam is a grace but denies that grace is obligatory on God.

Key Terms
لطف (lutf)
إمام (Imam)
وجوب (obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lutf argument shows that God is obligated to appoint an Imam as a grace.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that the concept of grace either becomes futile or would necessitate benefits that are not observed, thus the argument fails to establish obligation.

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of Razi's sixth argument, what is the status of the grace if God knows a preventer exists?
A) It is obligatory.
B) It is beneficial.
C) It is futile.
D) It is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth argument shows that the concept of lutf is self-defeating when applied to the Imam, because it either requires benefits that do not occur or renders the grace pointless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that a grace that does not produce the intended action is pointless. When you fail to obey, do you secretly hope God will provide some special push to make it easy, or do you take full responsibility for your choice? This passage confronts you: no external grace can force righteousness. Will you stop waiting for circumstances and start acting?
?
If you truly believed that God’s grace necessitates obedience, then sin would be impossible. But you sin daily. Does this lesson not shake your assumptions about what God owes you? Can you still claim that God must send guides and signs to make belief inevitable, when His own creation shows that even with clear signs, people choose disbelief?
?
Razi’s argument leaves no room for blaming God’s lack of grace for your shortcomings. The preventer is within you, not outside. What is one excuse you have used to avoid a duty? How will you abandon that excuse today and rely solely on your will to obey?

Refutation of the Lutf Argument: The Seventh Point and Concession
Razi presents the seventh argument and concedes the point but rejects obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that the knowledge of occurrence or non-occurrence of the Imam's appointment nullifies the need for lutf, and his concession that even if it were lutf, it is not obligatory on God.

Core Idea
If God already knows what will happen, grace is either unnecessary or impossible.

Razi presents a sharp logical point: if the Imam's appointment is a grace, then either God knows it will occur or He knows it will not. If He knows it will, it is necessary and does not need grace; if He knows it will not, it is impossible and grace has no effect. Then Razi makes a key concession, granting the appointment is a grace but denying that grace is obligatory on God, aligning with his broader Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic for the seventh point
Read the Arabic passage for the seventh argument and the concession. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Razi presents two possibilities: God knows the Imam's appointment will occur, or He knows it will not. Each has a different implication for the lutf.
3
See how each case undermines lutf
If God knows it will occur, it is already necessary, so no grace is needed. If He knows it will not occur, it is impossible, so grace cannot cause it.
4
Recognize the concession
Razi concedes that the appointment is a grace but uses his Ash'ari principle that nothing is obligatory on God to reject the conclusion of the lutf argument.
5
Reflect on the overall stance
This passage shows Razi's strategy: first refuting the logic of lutf, then denying any obligation on God. This is a key Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة : أن عند حصول هذا اللطف المقرب إما أن يعلن الله تعالى بأنه حصل ما يجب يمنع عن الفعل أو يعلم أنه لم يحصل هذا المانع البتة، فإن كان الأول فلا نسلم انه في العقول فعل مثل هذا اللطف ؛ لأنه إذا كان بتقدير وجوده لا يحصل الفعل كما أن يرجع بتقدير عدمه أيضًا لا يحصل لم يكن فعل هذا اللطف واجبًا، بل الفعل يوجب الامتناع منه ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في فعله البتة، وإن كان الثاني وهو أن الله تعالى علم أن هذا اللطف جانب الفعل وعلم أنه لم يحصل معه ما يمنع من الفعل، فمثل هذا يقتضي وجوب حصول الأثر - على ما بيناه في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
فلو كان نصب الإمام لطفا بهذا التفسير لزالت المفاسد، وحصلت المصالح لا محالة، ولما لم يكن كذلك وجب أن لا يكون نصب الإمام لطفا. السابعة : هذا الذي يجعل نصب الإمام لطفا فيه إن كان الله تعالى عالما بوقوعه كان واجب الوقوع فلا حاجة به إلى هذا اللطف وإن كان عالما بعدم وقوعه كان ممتنع الوقوع، فلم يكن للطف فيه أثر البتة.
سلمنا أن نصب الإمام لطفا، لكن لا نسلم أن اللطف واجب، وقد قدمنا ما يدل على أنه لا يجب على الله شيء أصلا ورأسا.
فهذا هو الكلام المختصر في هذا الباب.
The sixth: That when this proximate grace occurs, either God knows that what must prevent the action has occurred, or He knows that this preventer has not occurred at all. If the first, then we do not concede that in the intellects [there is] an action like this grace; because if by the estimation of its existence the action does not occur, just as by the estimation of its non-existence it also does not occur, then this grace is not obligatory; rather, the action necessitates refraining from it because there is no benefit in it at all. And if the second, which is that God knows that this grace accompanies the action and knows that there is no preventer with it, then such a thing necessitates the occurrence of the effect - according to what we explained in the issue of the creation of actions.
If the appointment of the Imam were a grace in this interpretation, then evils would necessarily be removed and benefits would be attained; but since it is not so, it is necessary that the appointment of the Imam is not a grace. The seventh: That which makes the appointment of the Imam a grace: if God knows its occurrence, then it is necessarily occurring, so there is no need for this grace; and if He knows its non-occurrence, then it is impossible, so there is no effect for the grace in it at all.
We concede that the appointment of the Imam is a grace, but we do not concede that the grace is obligatory. And we have already presented what indicates that nothing is obligatory upon God at all, absolutely.
This is the concise discourse on this chapter.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that appointing an Imam is a required divine grace (lutf). The author presents two points (السادسة and السابعة) and then a concession. First, he analyzes the logic of grace: if God knows that a preventer exists, then the grace is ineffective; if He knows no preventer exists, then the grace necessitates the action, implying that the Imam's grace would guarantee benefits, but since it does not, it is not a true grace. Second, if the Imam were a grace, either God knows it will occur (so it is necessary and needs no grace) or He knows it will not occur (so impossible, and grace is irrelevant). Finally, he concedes the Imam is a grace but denies that grace is obligatory on God.

Key Terms
لطف (lutf)
إمام (Imam)
وجوب (obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lutf argument shows God must appoint an Imam.
Correction: Razi shows that God's knowledge makes grace redundant, and even if it were a grace, it is not obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's seventh argument, if God knows that the Imam's appointment will occur, what is the implication?
A) The appointment is impossible.
B) The appointment is already necessary, so no need for grace.
C) The grace is essential.
D) The appointment is a mistake.

Key Takeaway
Razi's seventh argument and concession conclusively undermine the lutf proof for the obligation of the Imam, reinforcing the Ash'ari position that nothing is obligatory upon God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If nothing is obligatory upon God, how do you reconcile the feeling that God owes you something—health, provision, or guidance? Have you been living as though you deserve His blessings, or with humble gratitude for His free gifts?
?
Razi shows that God's knowledge makes events necessary or impossible. Does this truth disturb your sense of free will, or does it bring you peace to know that everything is under His control? How does this affect your trust in His decree for your life?
?
Conceding the point but denying obligation: Razi grants the opposition's premise but still rejects the conclusion. Are there areas in your faith where you hold onto the 'letter' of an argument but miss its 'spirit'? Are you willing to concede a point for the sake of truth, even if it seems to weaken your position?

Proof from Consensus that the Imam Need Not Be Infallible
Razi states positions on infallibility and gives Ash'ari proof from ijma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof from consensus that infallibility is not a necessary condition for the Imam.

Core Idea
Consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate proves infallibility is not a condition.

This passage presents a key theological debate about the qualifications of the Imam. Razi first lists the schools that differ on whether infallibility is required, then offers a concise argument based on the unanimous acceptance of Abu Bakr's caliphate. The proof is built on two premises: the validity of Abu Bakr's imamate and the consensus that he was not infallible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that Razi presents two opposing views on whether the Imam must be infallible. Then read the translation carefully.
The Arabic uses 'لا يجب' (not necessary) versus 'يجب' (necessary).
2
Identify which schools hold each view
The passage lists the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Zaydis, and Khawarij as rejecting the necessity of infallibility. The Isma'ilis and Ithna 'Asharis affirm it. Pay attention to the groups mentioned.
3
Examine the proof structure
Razi then provides a two-premise proof: (1) We will prove the validity of Abu Bakr's imamate, and (2) the ummah agreed that he was not necessarily infallible. The conclusion is that infallibility is not a condition. Notice the careful phrasing 'I do not say that he was not infallible'—this avoids claiming Abu Bakr erred.
The proof relies on consensus (ijma'), a major source of authority in Sunni Islam.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would the opponents object to this proof? How does the consensus of the companions serve as a decisive argument? Consider that this debate has implications for the legitimacy of the early caliphs.
This argument is central to the Sunni position on leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: في أنه لا يجب ان يكون الإمام معصوما
قال أصحابنا والمعتزلة والزيدية والخوارج لا يجب أن يكون الإمام معصوما.
وقالت الإسماعيلية والإثنا عشرية : يجب.
لنا: أنا سنقيم الدلالة إن شاء الله تعالى على صحة إمامة أبي بكر الصديق وأجمعت الأمة على أنه ما كان واجب العصمة لا أقول إنه ما كان معصوما، وحينئذ يحصل لنا من مجموع هاتين المقدمتين أن الإمام ليس من شرطه أن يكون واجب العصمة.
The second chapter: That it is not necessary that the Imam be infallible.
Our companions, the Mu'tazila, the Zaydiyya, and the Khawarij said: It is not necessary that the Imam be infallible.
The Isma'iliyya and the Ithna 'Ashariyya said: It is necessary.
[Our proof:] We will establish the evidence, God willing, for the validity of the imamate of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, and the ummah has agreed that he was not necessarily infallible - I do not say that he was not infallible - and then from these two premises we obtain that it is not a condition of the Imam that he be necessarily infallible.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a statement of doctrinal positions and a proof. Razi first lists the schools: Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Zaydis, and Khawarij hold that infallibility is not a condition for the Imam; Isma'ilis and Ithna 'Asharis hold it is necessary. Then he presents the Ash'ari proof: The ummah unanimously accepted the imamate of Abu Bakr, who was not necessarily infallible (though he was virtuous). This shows that infallibility is not a condition. The proof rests on the consensus (ijma') of the companions.

Key Terms
عصمة (infallibility)
إمام (Imam)
إجماع (consensus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which groups hold that the Imam does not need to be infallible?
A) Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Zaydis, Khawarij
B) Ash'aris, Isma'ilis, Ithna 'Asharis
C) Mu'tazila, Isma'ilis, Zaydis
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position uses the historical consensus on Abu Bakr to show that infallibility is not a prerequisite for the Imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that a leader must be perfect before you follow them? How does the consensus on Abu Bakr challenge your expectation of flawlessness in those you obey?
?
If the ummah agreed on Abu Bakr's leadership despite his fallibility, what does that say about the role of community agreement in your own religious decisions? Are you willing to submit to a consensus even if it contradicts your personal desire for a sinless guide?
?
Razi's proof relies on actual historical practice. Are you willing to let history and consensus shape your theology, or do you insist on abstract ideals? What is at stake for you in this question?
