Refuting the Imamiyya's Argument for Infallibility
Razi presents the second Imamiyya argument and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the Imamiyya's analogy-based argument for the Imam's infallibility by applying the same regress/circle reasoning.

Core Idea
If error is impossible for the Imam, the argument collapses.

This lesson examines the Imamiyya's second argument for the Imam's infallibility, which draws an analogy with the Creator's necessary existence. Razi refutes it by pointing out that the same reasoning would require an infinite chain of Imams. We'll see how he turns their own logic against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two distinct arguments: the first (shubha) and the second (analogy).
The first argument is not refuted here; focus on the second.
2
Understand the second argument
The Imamiyya argue that just as the world's contingency requires a necessary Creator, the possibility of disobedience in humans requires an infallible Imam to make obedience preponderant.
They see the Imam as a 'preponderator' (مرجح) for moral action.
3
Identify Razi's refutation
Look for 'قلنا' where Razi responds. He says that the possibility of disobedience cannot exist in the Imam; if it did, he would need another Imam, leading to regress or circle.
This mirrors the argument for God's necessary existence.
4
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Does Razi's refutation also apply to the first argument? Think about how regress is avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإثنا عشرية فقد احتجوا على وجوب عصمة الإمام بوجوه :
الشبهة الأولى: أنا إنما أحوجنا الخلق إلى الإمام لجواز الخطأ عليهم، فلو حصل في الإمام هذا المعنى لافتقر هو أيضًا إلى إمام آخر ، فيلزم التسلسل أو الدور، وهما محالان، فوجب أن لا يتحقق فيه جواز الخطأ.
واعلم أنهم احتجوا فقالوا إن دليلنا في وجوب وجود الإمام وفي وجوب عصمته تعينه، وهو عين الدليل الذي يعرف به وجود الصانع ووجوب وجوده. وذلك لأنا نقول : العالم يجوز وجوده ويجوز عدمه، فافتقر رجحان وجوده على عدمه إلى مرجح فكان قولنا : الخلق يجوز عليهم الطاعة ويجوز عليهم المعصية، فافتقر رجحان الطاعة على المعصية إلى المرجح وهو الإمام، ثم قلنا : الجواز الذي هو علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر غير حاصل في الصانع، وإلا افتقر إلى مؤثر آخر، فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل، فكذا ههنا.
قلنا : جواز الإقدام على المعصية غير حاصل في الإمام، وإلا لافتقر إلى آخر، فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل وهما محالان فثبت أن مسألة إثبات الإمامة بعينها
As for the Ithna 'Ashariyya, they have argued for the necessity of the Imam's infallibility by [several] arguments:
The first doubt: We only need a created being for the Imam because of the possibility of error upon them. So if this meaning occurs in the Imam, he too would need another Imam, leading to either regress or circle, both of which are impossible. Therefore, it is necessary that the possibility of error does not occur in him.
Know that they argued, saying: Our proof for the necessity of the existence of the Imam and for the necessity of his infallibility is the same, and it is precisely the proof by which the existence of the Creator and His necessary existence are known. That is because we say: The world is possible in its existence and possible in its non-existence, so its existence being preponderant over its non-existence needed a preponderator. Similarly, we say: The creation has the possibility of obedience and the possibility of disobedience, so the preponderance of obedience over disobedience needed a preponderator, which is the Imam. Then we said: The possibility which is the cause of the need for an effecting agent is not present in the Creator; otherwise He would need another effecting agent, leading to either circle or regress. So too here.
We said: The possibility of proceeding to disobedience is not present in the Imam; otherwise he would need another, leading to either circle or regress, both impossible. Thus it is established that the issue of establishing the imamate is precisely...
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents the Imamiyya's arguments for infallibility and a refutation of the second. First, they argue that since the function of the Imam is to prevent error, the Imam himself must be free from error to avoid regress. This is the first doubt. Then they present a more sophisticated analogy: just as the world's contingency requires a necessary cause, so the creation's possibility of obedience/disobedience requires an infallible Imam. Razi refutes the second by pointing out that the possibility of disobedience is not present in the Imam, otherwise he would need another Imam, leading to regress. Thus the Imamiyya's own reasoning defeats their claim. Note: The first doubt is not explicitly refuted here.

Key Terms
عصمة (infallibility)
تسلسل (regress)
دور (circle)
مرجح (preponderator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first argument (shubha) is compelling and unanswered, implying weakness in Razi's position.
Correction: Razi's refutation of the second argument applies analogously to the first: the same regress logic shows that the Imam cannot have the possibility of error.

Checkpoint
Razi refutes which of the Imamiyya's arguments explicitly?
A) The first doubt about regress
B) The second analogy with the Creator
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Imamiyya's analogy by showing that the same logical requirement would make the Imam need another Imam, so the Imam must be without possibility of error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you ever treat any human leader, scholar, or guide as if they were infallible? How would admitting their fallibility change your relationship to them?
?
The Imamiyya argue that without an infallible guide, people will inevitably fall into error. When you lack a perfect human example, what steps can you take to ensure you remain on the right path?
?
Reflect on the logical regress: if every guide needs a guide, where does it end? Have you ever been trapped in seeking endless advice, never trusting your own judgment? What would it mean for you to accept that all humans, including yourself, are fallible, and that only God is the ultimate guide?

The Imam Need Not Be Infallible: Razi's Proof
Razi states the two positions and gives his proof from Abu Bakr's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that infallibility is not a necessary condition for the Imam, based on the consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate.

Core Idea
If the first Imam wasn't required to be infallible, neither are his successors.

Imam Razi addresses a sectarian dispute about whether the Imam must be infallible. He presents the two opposing views and then offers a concise proof from the example of Abu Bakr. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic original and its translation to get the flow of Razi's argument.
Note how he first states the disagreement.
2
Identify the two premises
Razi's proof has two premises: first, that Abu Bakr's imamate is valid; second, that he was not necessarily infallible. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The combination of these premises shows that infallibility is not a condition for the imamate. Reflect on why this argument is effective.
This is a simple yet powerful argument from consensus and actual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحابنا والمعتزلة والزيدية والخوارج لا يجب أن يكون الإمام معصوما.
وقالت الإسماعيلية والإثنا عشرية : يجب.
لنا: أنا سنقيم الدلالة إن شاء الله تعالى على صحة إمامة أبي بكر لا وأجمعت الأمة على أنه ما كان واجب العصمة لا أقول إنه ما كان معصوما، وحينئذ يحصل لنا من مجموع هاتين المقدمتين أن الإمام ليس من شرطه أن يكون واجب العصمة.
Our companions, the Mu'tazila, the Zaydiyya, and the Khawarij said: The Imam does not have to be infallible. The Ismailiyya and the Ithna-ashariyya said: He must be. For us: We will establish the proof, God willing, of the correctness of the imamate of Abu Bakr – no, and the ummah agreed that he was not necessarily infallible, I do not say that he was not infallible – and then from the combination of these two premises we obtain that the Imam does not have the condition that he be necessarily infallible.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's own argument for why the Imam does not need to be infallible. First, he reports the disagreement: the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Zaydis, and Kharijites deny the necessity of infallibility, while the Ismailis and Twelver Imamis affirm it. Then he gives his proof, which consists of two premises: (1) He will prove the imamate of Abu Bakr, (2) The ummah agreed that Abu Bakr was not necessarily infallible (though Razi clarifies that he is not denying that Abu Bakr was infallible in practice; rather, he means that infallibility was not a required attribute for him). From these two premises, he concludes that infallibility is not a condition (shart) for the Imam. The argument is simple: if the first Imam (Abu Bakr) was validly Imam without being necessarily infallible, then infallibility cannot be a necessary condition for the imamate as such.

Key Terms
عصمة
إمام
شرط
أصحابنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi argued that Abu Bakr was not infallible.
Correction: Razi actually clarifies that he does not say Abu Bakr was not infallible; he only denies that infallibility was a necessary condition for his imamate.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why is infallibility not a necessary condition for the Imam?
A) Because infallibility is impossible for any human.
B) Because the consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate shows he was Imam without being necessarily infallible.
C) Because the Quran explicitly says the Imam need not be infallible.
D) Because the Ismailis and Twelvers agree that infallibility is not required.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof from the consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate establishes that infallibility is not a necessary condition for the Imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If, as Razi argues, infallibility is not a condition for legitimate leadership, then whom in your life have you dismissed because of their flaws—perhaps a parent, a teacher, or a leader—that you should reconsider following? What would it cost you to accept them as imperfect yet rightful guides?
?
Razi builds his argument on the consensus of the ummah. But in your heart, do you truly submit to the collective wisdom of the community, or do you secretly believe that your own standards—like sinlessness—are more reliable? How does that pride affect your relationship with Muslims who disagree with you?
?
Imagine if the early Muslims had demanded infallibility from Abu Bakr before accepting him. The entire history of Islam would have changed. Now, think of a decision you are facing where you are waiting for a 'perfect' option. Are you willing to act with the imperfect but collectively endorsed path, as the sahaba did? If not, what does that reveal about your trust in Allah's plan for His community?

Razi's Refutation of Collective Infallibility
Razi refutes the argument that the entire ummah must be infallible as transmitter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the argument that the entire Muslim community must be infallible as the transmitter of the Sharia, and why he concludes that infallibility must belong to specific individuals.

Core Idea
Infallibility of the entire community is neither rationally proven nor airtight; it leads to circular reasoning.

Razi presents a sophisticated refutation of a common argument used to prove the infallibility of the entire ummah and the authority of consensus. The argument initially seems plausible: the Sharia must be preserved and transmitted, so the transmitter must be infallible. But who is that transmitter? Razi systematically dismantles the claim that it is the community as a whole, exposing a fatal circularity. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow of Razi's argument.
Notice that Razi begins by stating the objection before refuting it.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two candidates for the infallible transmitter: the entire community (as proponents of ijmāʿ claim) or specific individuals. Note how Razi sets up this dichotomy.
3
Follow the refutation of the first possibility
Razi gives two arguments against the community: first, its infallibility is not rationally known (counterexample: Christians), second, even if known through transmitted proofs, it leads to circularity. Trace each step.
The circularity argument is subtle: to prove ijmāʿ, you need to know the community is infallible, but that knowledge itself requires ijmāʿ.
4
Understand the conclusion and dismissal of tawatur
After refuting the first possibility, Razi concludes that specific individuals must be infallible. He then briefly dismisses the idea that tawatur alone preserves the Sharia, because tawatur only confirms what is transmitted, not that nothing is omitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية في وجوب العصمة: أنه ثبت بالتواتر عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن الشريعة التي جاء بها، فإنه أوجبها على جميع المكلفين إلى يوم القيامة، ولما أوجبها على جميع المكلفين وجب وصولها إلى جميع المكلفين، وإلا لكان ذلك تكليفا بما لا يطاق. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: هذه الشرائع لا بد لها من حافظ يحفظها عن التغير، ثم ناقل ينقلها إلينا، وذلك الناقل لا بد وأن يكون واجب العصمة، إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لم يكن نقله مفيدًا للعلم، وذلك الناقل الذي هو واجب العصمة إما أن يكون مجموع الأمة - على ما ذهب إليه من يقول: الإجماع حجة ـ أو بعض آحاد الأمة، والأول باطل؛ لأن وجوب عصمة كل الأمة غير ثابت بالعقل، لأنا نرى النصارى على كثرتهم مجمعين على الأباطيل، فإذن وجوب عصمة مجموع الأمة لا يعرف إلا بالدلائل السمعية وكل دليل نقلي فإنه لا يبعد تطرق التخصيص والنسخ إليه، فإذن كل دليل يدل على أن الإجماع حجة فإنه لا يتم كونه دليلا إلا إذا عرفنا أنه لم يوجد له ناسخ ولا مخصص وإنما يحصل لنا العلم بهذا العدم لو عرفنا أنه لو حصل هذا الناسخ أو هذا المخصص لوجب أن يصل إلينا، وإنما نعرف أنه لو كان لوصل لنا لو علمنا أنه لا يجوز على الأمة أن يخلوا بنقل بعض الشرائع، وإنما العلم نعرف أنه لا يجوز فيهم هذا الإخلال إذا عرفنا كونهم موصوفين بوجوب العصمة، فثبت أن العلم بأنه لم يحصل الإخلال بنقل الشريعة متى استفدناه من الإجماع لزم الدور، والدور باطل، فكان هذا القسم باطلا. وإذا بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن المتكفل بنقل الشريعة وحفظها عن التغيير والتبديل أشخاص معينون موصوفون بوجوب العصمة، وذلك هو المطلوب. لا يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الشريعة تبقى محفوظة بنقل أهل التواتر؟ لأنا نقول: نقل أهل التواتر يدل على أن ما نقلوه صحيح لكن لا يدل على أن الذي لم ينقلوه لم يوجد فأين أحد البابين من الآخر؟ وهذه الشبهة كانوا يرتبونها على وجوه سخيفة، ونحن رتبناها لهم على هذا الوجه.
The second sophistical argument concerning the obligation of infallibility: It is established by tawatur (mass transmission) from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that the Sharia which he brought, he made it obligatory upon all those morally responsible until the Day of Judgment. And when he made it obligatory upon all those morally responsible, its reaching all those morally responsible became necessary, otherwise that would be an obligation beyond capacity. When this is established, we say: These laws necessarily have a preserver who preserves them from change, then a transmitter who transmits them to us. That transmitter must necessarily be infallible (wājib al-ʿiṣma), because if he were not such, his transmission would not yield knowledge. That transmitter who is infallible is either the totality of the community (umma) — as held by those who say consensus (ijmāʿ) is a proof — or some individuals of the community. The first is false, because the obligation of infallibility of the entire community is not established by reason; for we see the Christians, despite their multitude, united upon falsehoods. Therefore, the obligation of infallibility of the totality of the community is known only through transmitted proofs. Every transmitted proof is subject to specification and abrogation. Thus every proof indicating that consensus is a proof is not complete as a proof unless we know that no abrogator or specifier exists for it. We only gain knowledge of this non-existence if we know that had this abrogator or specifier occurred, it would necessarily have reached us. And we only know that it would have reached us if we know that it is not permissible for the community to neglect transmitting some of the laws. But we know that this neglect is not permissible in them only if we know that they are described as being under the obligation of infallibility. So it is established that knowledge that no neglect in transmitting the Sharia occurred, when we derive it from consensus, leads to circularity. Circularity is false. So this division is false. And when this division is false, it is established that the one who undertakes the transmission of the Sharia and its preservation from change and alteration is specific individuals described as being under the obligation of infallibility. That is what is sought. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the Sharia remains preserved through the transmission of the people of tawatur? For we say: The transmission of the people of tawatur indicates that what they transmitted is correct, but it does not indicate that what they did not transmit did not exist. So what connection is there between the two aspects? This sophistical argument was arranged by them in foolish ways, and we arranged it for them in this manner.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa-jawāb) concerning the necessity of infallibility (ʿiṣma) for the transmitter of the Sharia. The objection begins by stating that the Sharia is obligatory for all until Judgment Day, so it must reach all; otherwise it would be an impossible obligation. Therefore, the Sharia needs an infallible preserver and transmitter. The question is: who is this infallible transmitter? Two possibilities: the entire community (as proponents of ijmāʿ argue) or specific individuals. Razi refutes the first: the infallibility of the entire community is not rationally proven, as seen by Christians united on falsehoods. Even if one argues from transmitted proofs (samʿiyyāt), these proofs are subject to abrogation and specification. To know that no abrogator or specifier exists, we must know that if one existed, it would have reached us. But we can only know that if we know the community cannot neglect transmission. However, that knowledge itself requires their infallibility, leading to circularity (dawr). Hence the first possibility fails, proving that specific individuals (the Imams) must be infallible. Finally, Razi dismisses the alternative that tawatur alone preserves the Sharia, because tawatur only confirms what is transmitted but does not prove that nothing was omitted. Razi notes that this argument was originally presented in weak forms, but he has strengthened it.

Key Terms
عصمة
تواتر
إجماع
دور
واجب العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi is arguing for the infallibility of the entire community.
Correction: Razi explicitly refutes the infallibility of the community and argues that specific individuals (the Imams) must be infallible.

Checkpoint
What problem does Razi identify in the argument that the entire ummah is the infallible transmitter?
A) The infallibility of the ummah is not rationally known.
B) The proof for consensus requires prior knowledge that the ummah does not neglect transmission.
C) Both A and B.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that the infallible transmitter of the Sharia must be specific individuals, not the entire community, because the latter leads to circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often have you unquestioningly accepted religious knowledge based on the consensus of those around you, without critically examining the chain of transmission or the possibility of omission? Will you now investigate the foundations of your beliefs as Razi did?
?
Razi argues that the preservation of the Sharia depends on specific infallible individuals. If you hold a different view, how do you reconcile your belief with the logical circularity he exposes? Are you willing to face the implications for your own tradition?
?
When you recite a hadith or rely on a teaching, do you ever consider that there might have been omissions or changes over time? How would knowing that the Sharia is fully preserved only through infallible transmitters change your trust in what you have learned?

The Third Shubah: Necessity of the Imam's Infallibility
Razi argues that the imam must be infallible because following a sinful imam would entail commanding sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's linguistic and logical argument for why the imam must be infallible.

Core Idea
An imam who can sin cannot be an imam.

This lesson presents Razi's third argument for imam's infallibility. He starts with the very meaning of 'imam' as one who is followed, then uses a dilemma to show that allowing sin on the imam leads to either commanding sin or undermining his leadership. This elegant argument ties the imam's infallibility directly to the function of being followed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic definition of imam
Razi begins by defining 'imam' linguistically as one who is followed and taken as a leader, just as a garment is named for being worn. This definition sets the foundation for the argument.
The analogy with clothing emphasizes that the imam's identity is relational to being followed.
2
Examine the dilemma when the imam sins
Razi poses a dilemma: if the imam sins, either he is followed in that sin or he is not followed. Both options lead to unacceptable consequences.
The dilemma covers all possibilities, so no escape.
3
First branch: following leads to commanding sin
If the imam is followed in his sin, then through the imam's example, Allah would be commanding sin, which is impossible for Allah.
This branch is impossible, so it cannot happen.
4
Second branch: not following negates imamate
If the imam is not followed in his sin, then the people follow their own judgment of good and evil, not the imam. Thus the imam ceases to be an imam in reality.
This branch also destroys the imam's status.
5
Conclude the necessity of infallibility
Since both branches lead to absurdities, it is impossible for the imam to sin. Therefore the imam must be infallible (ma'sum).
The argument does not rely on scriptural proof but on the logical function of imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: الإمام في اللغة عبارة عن الشخص الذي يؤم به ويقتدى به كالرداء، فإنه اسم لما يرتدى به واللحاف اسم لما يلتحف به، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو جوزنا الذنب على الإمام فحال إقدامه على ذلك الذنب إما أن يقتدى به أو لا يقتدى به، فإن كان الأول كان الله قد أمر بالذنب، وإنه غير جائز، وإن كان الثاني خرج الإمام عن كونه إمامًا لأن المأموم إذا رأى ما علم حسنه فعله، وإذا رأى ما علم قبحه لم يفعله، وحينئذ لا يكون متبعا له ولا مقتديا به، بل يكون متبعا للدليل، وذلك يقدح في كونه إمامًا، فثبت أن الخطأ على الإمام غير جائز.
The third sophistical argument: The imam, in language, is an expression for the person who is followed and taken as a leader, like the cloak (ridā’), for it is a name for that which is worn; and the blanket (liḥāf) is a name for that which is wrapped. When this is established, we say: If we permit sin upon the imam, then in the case of his committing that sin, either he is followed in it or he is not followed. If the first [i.e., he is followed], then Allah would have commanded sin, and that is impermissible. And if the second [i.e., he is not followed], then the imam departs from being an imam because the follower, when he sees what he knows to be good, does it, and when he sees what he knows to be evil, does not do it; then he is not a follower of him nor a leader, but rather a follower of the evidence. That impugns his being an imam. So it is established that error is impermissible for the imam.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument (shubha) for the necessity of the imam's infallibility. Razi begins with a definition: the term 'imam' linguistically refers to one who is followed (yu'ammu bihi wa yuqtadā bihi), just as a garment (ridā') is named for being worn. From this, he presents a dilemma: if we allow sin on the imam, then when he commits a sin, either people follow him in that sin or they do not. If they follow, then Allah would be commanding sin through the imam, which is impossible. If they do not follow, then the imam is no longer actually an imam, because the follower acts based on his own knowledge of good and evil, not following the imam. Thus the imam ceases to be a leader. Therefore, the imam must be infallible (ma'sūm) and cannot commit error (khaṭa').

Key Terms
إمام
عصمة
اقتداء
ذنب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to major sins, not minor errors.
Correction: Razi's reasoning applies to any sin or error (khaṭa'), as the dilemma holds for any act that would be considered a disobedience.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's third shubah, what happens if the imam sins and people do not follow him?
A) The imam must repent.
B) The imam ceases to be an imam because he is not being followed.
C) The people are excused for not following.
D) The imam's sin is forgiven.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third shubah establishes that the imam must be infallible because any sin would either command sin or negate his imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you follow a leader who you know is committing a sin, do you still consider them your leader? How does Razi's argument force you to choose between following the evidence and following the person? What would you actually do?
?
When you look at the people you follow—whether scholars, influencers, or community leaders—are you prepared to depart from following them if they lead you toward something evil? What sin would you be willing to overlook just to maintain your allegiance?
?
The argument says that if you do not follow a sinful imam, you become a follower of evidence. Are you someone who follows evidence even when it costs you a relationship or a status? What is one area of your life where you are afraid to follow the truth because it might mean leaving behind a leader you admire?

Third Objection on Infallibility and Its Refutation
Third objection to Imam's infallibility and its refutation via deputy analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the Imam's infallibility and its refutation through the analogy of following deputies.

Core Idea
The linguistic meaning of 'imam' does not force absolute following.

This passage presents a linguistic argument against the possibility of a fallible Imam, followed by a refutation that points to the deputies of the Imam who are followed despite being fallible. You'll see how the objection relies on a false dichotomy and how the answer exposes it by drawing a parallel to established practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the flow of the objection and answer.
Pay attention to the linguistic definition given at the start.
2
Analyze the objection's structure
Notice the dichotomy: if sin is permitted, either the Imam is followed (implying God commands sin) or not followed (negating his imamate). Both are deemed impossible, so the objector concludes the Imam must be infallible.
The objection hinges on the assumption that following is absolute.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer points out that the Imam's deputies (judges, scholars, witnesses) are followed by consensus despite being fallible. This shows that the objection's dichotomy is false, because one can follow a leader in some matters without endorsing all actions.
The analogy breaks the absolute following assumption.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect on how this argument and counterargument illustrate the complexity of reasoning about infallibility (ʿiṣma) in Islamic theology.
This lesson establishes that linguistic arguments alone are insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : الإمام في اللغة عبارة عن الشخص الذي يؤم به ويقتدى به كالرداء، فإنه اسم لما يرتدى به واللحاف اسم لما يلتحف به، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو جوزنا الذنب على الإمام فحال إقدامه على ذلك الذنب إما أن يقتدى به أو لا يقتدى به، فإن كان الأول كان الله قد أمر بالذنب، وإنه غير جائز، و إن كان الثاني خرج الإمام عن كونه إمامًا لأن المأموم إذا رأى ما علم حسنه فعله، وإذا رأى ما علم قبحه لم يفعله، وحينئذ لا يكون متبعا له ولا مقتديا به، بل يكون متبعا للدليل، وذلك يقدح في كونه إمامًا، فثبت أن الخطأ على الإمام غير جائز.
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لا نزاع في أنه يجب على كل واحد من آحاد الرعية أن يقتدي بنواب الإمام من القضاة والعلماء والشهود، مع أنهم بالاتفاق ليسوا معصومين وكل ما يقولونه فيهم فهو جوابنا عن الإمام الأعظم.
The third objection: Imam in language is the person by whom one leads and is followed (yu'ammu bihi wa yuqtada bihi), like the garment (al-rida'), for it is a name for what is worn as a cloak, and the coverlet (al-lihaf) is a name for what is used as a blanket. If this is established, we say: If we permit sin for the Imam, then when he commits that sin, either he is followed or he is not followed. If the first, then God would have commanded sin, and that is not permissible. If the second, then the Imam would cease to be an Imam, because the follower (al-ma'mum) when he sees what he knows to be good does it, and when he sees what he knows to be evil does not do it, and in that case he is not following him nor taking him as a model, but rather following the evidence, and that detracts from his being an Imam. Thus it is established that error is not permissible for the Imam.
The answer to the third objection: There is no dispute that it is obligatory for every individual of the subjects to follow the deputies of the Imam, such as the judges, scholars, and witnesses, although they are by consensus not infallible. And whatever they say about them is our answer regarding the supreme Imam.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the possibility of the Imam being non-infallible, followed by its refutation. The objection is linguistic: the word 'imam' implies being followed, so if he sins, either he is followed (which would mean God commands sin) or not followed (which negates his imamate). The answer refutes by pointing out that the Imam's deputies (judges, scholars) are followed despite not being infallible, so the same logic applies to the Imam. The objection's dichotomy is shown to be false because following is not absolute; one can follow in some matters and not in others.

Key Terms
إمام
اقتداء
عصمة
خطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection conclusively proves the Imam's infallibility.
Correction: The objection is refuted by showing that deputies are followed despite being fallible, so the dichotomy is false.

Checkpoint
What is the core assumption in the third objection regarding the linguistic meaning of 'imam'?
A) Imam means leader in worship only
B) Imam is one who is followed
C) Imam is always infallible
D) Imam must be a scholar

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because its assumption that following is absolute is contradicted by the practice of following deputies despite their fallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow a scholar or leader, do you ever examine whether their actions align with the evidence, or do you follow them blindly? How does this lesson's distinction between following in guidance and imitating every action challenge you to reconsider your own obedience to those in authority?
?
The objector feared that not following the Imam in sin would undermine his status. But the answer shows that following is not absolute. Where in your life are you tempted to demand absolute loyalty from those you lead or follow? Will you allow yourself to be corrected when you err, or do you expect others to follow even your mistakes?

This lesson uses the analogy of deputies who are followed despite being fallible. Think of a time you followed someone who later made a mistake. Did that mistake cause you to reject everything they taught? Reflect: are you able to separate the truth from the person, and still follow the evidence?

The Fourth Objection on Imam's Infallibility: Three Impossibilities
Presents the fourth objection with three divisions and its refutation by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the Imam's infallibility, its three-part structure, and why the objector concludes the Imam must be sinless.

Core Idea
If the Imam could sin, either the subjects prevent him or not—both lead to absurdities, so he must be sinless.

This lesson examines the fourth objection advanced by proponents of the Imam's infallibility. The objection argues that if the Imam were not infallible, then when he commits a major sin, the duty to prevent him creates three impossible scenarios, thus proving his infallibility. We'll explore each division and the beginning of the refutation that uses an analogous argument against the objector.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: the objection sets up a trilemma and then argues each horn leads to impossibility.
The refutation is incomplete in the source text.
2
Identify the three divisions
The objection splits the duty to prevent the Imam into three cases: obligatory on the entire community, on each individual, or not at all. Mark where each division is introduced.
The first two divisions are argued to be impossible; the third is argued to be invalid.
3
Analyze the arguments for impossibility
For each division, note the reasons given: first division impossible due to lack of consensus and fear; second division impossible due to weakness and circularity; third division leads to contradiction with the purpose of the Imamate.
4
Examine the start of the refutation
The answer begins by turning the same reasoning against the objector: the Imam's deputies are not infallible, yet the objector accepts them. Read the beginning and consider how the argument might be completed.
The text cuts off after 'because the Imam...' so the full refutation is not available.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : لو جاز الذنب على الإمام فبتقدير إقدامه على سفك الدماء واستباحة الفروج وأنواع الظلم إما أن يجب على الرعية منعه عن هذه الأفعال أو لا يجب، فإن وجب فإما أن يجب ذلك على مجموع الأمة أو على آحاد الأمة. أما القسم الأول : وهو وجوب ذلك على مجموع الأمة فلا جائز أن يقال : يجب على مجموع الأمة منعه عن تلك الأفعال لوجهين : الأول: أن إطباق جميع الرعايا الموجودين في الشرق والغرب على الفعل الواحد ممتنع وحينئذ لا يحصل منع الإمام عن تلك الأعمال المنكرة. الثاني : أنا نرى الملك العظيم إذا أقدم على فعل قبيح فكل واحد من آحاد الرعايا يخاف من إظهار الإنكار عليه ؛ لأنه يخاف أن يصير غيره موافقا لذلك الملك في ذلك الفعل القبيح، وحينئذ يأخذون هذا الواحد الذي أظهر الإنكار ويقتلونه، وإذا كان هذا الخوف حاصلا لكل واحد من آحاد الرعية امتنع اجتماعهم على منع ذلك الملك عن ذلك الفعل القبيح. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن يجب على كل أحد من آحاد الرعية إظهار الإنكار على الملك الكبير فهذا أيضا بعيد من وجهين : الأول: أن كل واحد من آحاد الرعية لا يقوى على مقاومة أمير صغير فكيف على معاداة ملك الدنيا. الثاني : أن المقصود من نصب الإمام أن يؤدب كل واحد من آحاد الرعية، فلو كلفنا كل واحد من آحاد الرعية أن يؤدب الإمام لزم الدور، فإن هذا إنما يتزجر عن معصيته بسبب ذلك، وذلك ينزجر بسبب هذا ومعلوم أن الدور باطل. وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: إن عند إقدام الإمام على المعاصي والفواحش لا يجوز منعه عنها البتة - فهو أيضًا باطل؛ لأن الدلائل الدالة على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر عامة، فتتناول الإمام وغيره، وأيضا فعلى هذا الطريق يصير نصب الإمام سببًا لتكثير الفواحش ومعلوم أن المقصود منه تقليلها، وهذا يفضي إلى التناقض فثبت أن الإمام لو لم يكن واجب العصمة لأفضى إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة، فوجب القول بكونه باطلا.
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : المعارضة أيضا بنواب الإمام، وذلك أن الإمام المعصوم إذا فوض الإمارة والوزارة إلى بعض الناس فذلك المفوض إليه غير معصوم، وبتقدير صدور الذنب عنه فالمانع له إما الإمام الأكبر وحده أو مع غيره. والأول: وهو أن المانع له هو الإمام الأكبر وحده، فهو باطل؛ لأن الإمام
The fourth objection: If sin is permitted for the Imam, then supposing he commits bloodshed, violation of private parts, and types of injustice, either it is obligatory for the subjects to prevent him from these acts or not. If it is obligatory, then either it is obligatory on the entire community or on the individuals of the community. As for the first division: that it is obligatory on the entire community – it is not permissible to say that it is obligatory on the entire community to prevent him from those acts for two reasons: first, that the consensus of all subjects in the east and west on a single act is impossible, and then prevention of the Imam from those reprehensible acts does not occur. Second, we see that if a great king commits an evil act, every individual of the subjects fears to show disapproval against him, because he fears that others might agree with that king in that evil act, and then they would take this one who showed disapproval and kill him. Since this fear exists for every individual of the subjects, their gathering to prevent that king from that evil act is impossible. As for the second division: that it is obligatory on each individual of the subjects to show disapproval against the great king – this too is unlikely for two reasons: first, each individual of the subjects is not strong enough to resist a minor prince, so how can he oppose the king of the world? Second, the purpose of appointing an Imam is to discipline each individual of the subjects, so if we charge each individual of the subjects to discipline the Imam, circularity (al-dawr) would result, for this one is deterred from his sin by that one, and that one is deterred by this one; and it is known that circularity is invalid. As for the third division: that it is said that when the Imam commits sins and abominations, it is not at all permissible to prevent him – this too is invalid, because the proofs indicating the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil are general, encompassing the Imam and others. Also, in this way, appointing an Imam would become a cause for increasing abominations, whereas it is known that its purpose is to decrease them, and this leads to contradiction. Thus it is established that if the Imam were not necessarily infallible, it would lead to these invalid divisions, so it is necessary to say he is infallible.
The answer to the fourth objection: The counterargument (al-mu'arada) is also by the deputies of the Imam. That is, if the infallible Imam delegates leadership and ministry to some people, then that delegate is not infallible. Supposing sin occurs from him, then the one who prevents him is either the supreme Imam alone or with others. The first: that the preventer is the supreme Imam alone is invalid, because the Imam...
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against the Imam being non-infallible, followed by the beginning of its refutation (cut off). The objection argues that if the Imam can sin, then when he does evil, either the subjects must prevent him or not, and both options lead to impossibilities. It divides the duty to prevent into three cases: obligation on the entire community (impossible due to lack of consensus), obligation on each individual (impossible due to weakness and circularity), and no obligation at all (invalid because it contradicts the general command of forbidding evil and would increase evil). Thus the Imam must be infallible. The answer begins by countering with the same logic applied to the Imam's deputies (who are non-infallible but are still followed and corrected by the Imam), implying that the objection's reasoning would also apply to them, but they are accepted. The answer is incomplete in the text.

Key Terms
عصمة
أمر بالمعروف
نهي عن المنكر
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is a definitive proof for infallibility, but the answer shows it can be turned against the objector by analogy with deputies.
Correction: The refutation uses the same logical move (al-mu'arada) to show that the objector's own position leads to similar issues with non-infallible deputies, thus the objection is not decisive.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, why is it impossible for the entire community to prevent the Imam from evil?
A) Because the Imam is too powerful
B) Because consensus on a single act is impossible
C) Because the subjects are weak
D) Because it would cause circularity

Key Takeaway
The objection uses a trilemma to argue for the Imam's infallibility, but the refutation uses the same logical move against the proponent's own position.

Fifth Objection: The Covenant Does Not Reach Wrongdoers
Objection using Quran 2:124 to argue for Imam's infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth objection uses Quran 2:124 to argue that the Imam must be infallible.

Core Idea
Only the sinless can receive the covenant of imamate.

The fifth objection turns to a direct scriptural proof: God's statement to Ibrahim that His covenant does not reach the wrongdoers. The objection links wrongdoing to sin and argues that any sinner, whether openly or secretly, is excluded from imamate. This passage shows how a single verse can be used to establish a major theological claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection cites Quran 2:124 and then explains its implication.
The verse is from the story of Ibrahim's test.
2
Identify the key elements
Find the verse, the interpretation of 'covenant' as imamate, the equation of 'wrongdoer' with 'sinner', and the conclusion.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: verse says wrongdoers are excluded; all sinners are wrongdoers; therefore all sinners are excluded; so the Imam must be sinless (infallible).
The objection uses a second verse (35:32) to support that sinners are wrongdoers.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important for the doctrine of infallibility. What would the opponent need to counter?
The objection assumes 'zalim' includes all sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى لإبراهيم : قَالَ وَمِن ذُرِّيَّتِي قَالَ لَا يَنَالُ عَهْدِى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٢٤] دلت الآية على أن عهد الإمامة لا يصل إلى من كان ظالما، وكل من كان مذنبًا، فإنه ظالم قال تعالى : فَمِنْهُمْ ظَالِمُ لِنَفْسِهِ [فاطر: ۳۲] فصارت الآية نصا في أن كل من كان مذنبا سواء كان ذنبه ظاهرا أو باطنا فإنه لا يكون إماماً، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت أن الإمام لا بد وأن يكون معصوما.
The fifth objection: Adherence to His statement to Ibrahim: 'He said: And of my progeny? He said: My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers.' (Al-Baqarah 2:124). The verse indicates that the covenant of imamate does not reach one who is a wrongdoer, and everyone who is a sinner is a wrongdoer. He said: 'Among them are wrongdoers to themselves' (Fatir 32). So the verse becomes a clear text that everyone who is a sinner, whether their sin is apparent or hidden, cannot be an Imam. If that is so, it is established that the Imam must be infallible.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which is a scriptural argument for the Imam's infallibility. The objection cites Quran 2:124, where God tells Ibrahim that His covenant (interpreted as imamate) does not reach the wrongdoers (al-zalimun). Then it argues that every sinner is a wrongdoer, citing Quran 35:32. Therefore, any sinner, whether openly or secretly, cannot be an Imam. The conclusion is that the Imam must be infallible (ma'sum). This is a straightforward proof text for those who argue for infallibility.

Key Terms
عهد
ظالم
عصمة
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume the verse refers only to major sins or specific wrongdoers like polytheists.
Correction: The objection argues that 'zalim' includes all sin, citing Quran 35:32 which calls sinners 'wrongdoers to themselves.'

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is used to support that every sinner is a wrongdoer?
A) Al-Baqarah 2:124
B) Fatir 35:32
C) Al-Ma'ida 5:3
D) Al-An'am 6:82

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection argues that since the covenant of imamate is denied to wrongdoers and all sinners are wrongdoers, the Imam must be infallible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection demands that a leader be sinless. But you are not a leader of the ummah. Yet you are responsible for your own covenant with Allah. Are you living as if your sins truly matter to that covenant, or do you presume upon His mercy without accountability?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and He applies the standard of this verse to you: 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers.' What one specific sin do you need to repent of today to restore your covenant with Him?

Refuting Objections to the Imam's Infallibility
Refutation of two objections against Imam's infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the first two objections that challenge the necessity of an infallible Imam.

Core Idea
Accessibility is required for an infallible transmitter to preserve Sharia.

In this passage, Imam Razi responds to two objections raised against the doctrine of the Imam's infallibility. He explains that the first objection is built on a premise already addressed, and the second fails because an inaccessible infallible transmitter cannot safeguard the Sharia.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise argument style of Imam Razi.
The passage contains two separate answers.
2
Analyze the first answer
The answer to the first objection states that it depends on an earlier issue already refuted. Understand that this is a criticism of circular reasoning.
The objection is dismissed by referring to a previous discussion.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer introduces a condition: an infallible transmitter must be physically accessible to preserve Sharia. Without accessibility, the argument collapses.
This highlights the necessity of a living, accessible Imam.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Observe how Razi uses a conditional: preservation only if accessible. Identify the core premise and its implication.
This reasoning defends the need for an Imam who can be consulted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أنها مبنية على المسألة الأولى، وهي أن الخلق لما كان الخطأ عليهم جائزا احتاجوا إلى نصب الإمام، وقد تقدم الجواب عن كلامهم فيه. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الشريعة إنما تبقى محفوظة بنقل الناقل المعصوم لو كان ذلك الناقل المعصوم بحيث يرى ويمكن الوصول إليه والرجوع إلى قوله فأما إذا لم يكن كذلك لم تصر الشريعة محفوظة بنقله، فسقطت هذه الشبهة.
The answer to the first objection: It is built on the first issue, namely that since error was possible for the people, they needed the appointment of an Imam. And the answer to their statement has already been given. The answer to the second objection: The Sharia is only preserved by the transmission of an infallible transmitter if that infallible transmitter is such that he can be seen, reached, and his statement referred to. But if that is not the case, then the Sharia is not preserved by his transmission. Therefore this objection collapses.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage provides brief refutations to the first two objections (which are not given in this window). The answer to the first objection states that it depends on an earlier issue (the need for an Imam due to fallibility) and that the answer has already been given, implying it is circular or already refuted. The answer to the second objection argues that the preservation of Sharia through an infallible transmitter requires that transmitter to be physically accessible for consultation. If no such accessible infallible person exists, then the argument fails. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms

نصب الإمام
شريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that as long as there is an infallible person somewhere, the Sharia is preserved regardless of accessibility.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that the infallible transmitter must be accessible for consultation; otherwise his existence does not preserve the Sharia.

Checkpoint

B) He must be seen and accessible
C) He must be a scholar
D) He must be sinless

Key Takeaway
An infallible Imam's preservation of Sharia requires his physical accessibility to the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the truth, but do you have a living guide whose words you can actually seek? Or are you relying on an absent authority to preserve Islam for you? Will you make the effort to find and follow the accessible infallible teacher?
?
The preservation of Sharia depends on accessible transmission. Ask yourself: Is your Islam preserved through a reliable, living connection? Or have you settled for a religion that exists only in books, without a teacher to correct your errors?

Razi says an inaccessible infallible transmitter cannot preserve Sharia. Where is your access to God's preserved message today? Are you satisfied with a mere claim of infallibility, or do you demand accessibility? What will you do to ensure you are not deceived?

Proof of Imam's Infallibility from the Third Division
Imam Razi refutes the third division and concludes that the imam must be infallible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the imam must be infallible by refuting the possibility that the imam's sins cannot be prevented.

Core Idea
The imam must be infallible or his appointment becomes self-defeating.

In this passage, Imam Razi completes his proof of the imam's infallibility by eliminating the third of three possible scenarios. He demonstrates that if the imam could sin and no one could forbid him, the very purpose of his appointment would be contradicted. Let's see how he reasons through this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فثبت' (thus it is established).
2
Identify the third division being refuted
The third division is the claim that when the imam commits sins, it is not permissible to prevent him at all.
This is the position the author argues against.
3
Understand the two reasons for its falsehood
The author gives two reasons: (1) the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil is general and includes the imam, and (2) allowing the imam to sin unchecked would multiply evil, contradicting the purpose of his appointment.
Each reason independently refutes the third division.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since all three divisions are false, the only remaining possibility is that the imam must be infallible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن معصيته بسبب ذلك، وذلك ينزجر بسبب هذا ومعلوم أن الدور باطل. وأما القسم الثالث: وهو أن يقال: إن عند إقدام الإمام على المعاصي والفواحش لا يجوز منعه عنها البتة - فهو أيضًا باطل؛ لأن الدلائل الدالة على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر عامة، فتتناول الإمام وغيره، وأيضا فعلى هذا الطريق يصير نصب الإمام سببًا لتكثير الفواحش ومعلوم أن المقصود منه تقليلها، وهذا يفضي إلى التناقض فثبت أن الإمام لو لم يكن واجب العصمة لأفضى إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة، فوجب القول بكونه باطلا.
From his sin because of that, and that [person] is deterred by this [act], and it is known that circular reasoning is invalid. And as for the third division: that it is said that when the Imam commits sins and immoral acts, it is not permissible to prevent him from them at all – this is also false; because the proofs indicating the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil are general, so they include the Imam and others. Also, in this way, the appointment of the Imam becomes a cause for the multiplication of immoral acts, and it is known that the purpose of it [his appointment] is to reduce them, and this leads to contradiction. Thus it is established that if the Imam were not necessarily infallible (wajib al-ismah), it would lead to these false divisions; therefore it is necessary to say that he [must be] infallible.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage concludes an argument consisting of three divisions. The author presents the third division and refutes it, then draws the overall conclusion. He shows that if the Imam were not infallible, one of three false outcomes would occur; the third leads to contradiction because the obligation of forbidding evil applies to all, and allowing the Imam to sin unchecked would defeat the purpose of his office. Therefore, the Imam must be infallible.

Key Terms
عصمة (ismah)
الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر (commanding good and forbidding evil)
الإمام (imam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation of forbidding evil does not apply to the leader.
Correction: Imam Razi asserts that the obligation is general and includes the imam, as supported by general proofs.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi conclude from the argument about the three divisions?
A) The Imam must be infallible.
B) The Imam may sometimes be prevented from sin.
C) The Imam's sins are permissible.
D) The obligation of commanding good does not apply to the Imam.

Key Takeaway
The imam's infallibility is necessary to prevent logical contradiction and uphold the purpose of his office.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any sins you commit privately that you think are 'small' or 'excusable' because of your position or role? How does this teaching about the imam's accountability challenge that excuse?
?
When was the last time you stayed silent about a wrong done by someone in authority over you? What would it cost you to speak up, and what would it cost you not to, according to this passage?
?
If the imam must be infallible to prevent evil from increasing, what does that say about your own responsibility to avoid being a source of evil for those under your care? Will you commit to one specific change today?

Fifth Objection: The Verse of Ibrahim and Its Answer
Imam Razi presents the fifth objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection to the necessity of the Imam's infallibility based on Qur'an 2:124 and the nuanced answer that distinguishes between being engaged in sin and absolute infallibility.

Core Idea
The verse only forbids ongoing sin, not all sin, so it does not prove absolute infallibility.

This lesson focuses on the fifth objection raised against the necessity of the Imam's infallibility, which appeals to the famous verse where Allah tells Ibrahim that His covenant does not reach the wrongdoers. While the objection seems strong, Imam Razi provides a precise answer that limits the verse's implication. We will examine the objection and its refutation in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth objection
Read the Arabic text of the fifth objection carefully. Notice how it uses the verse as a proof that the Imam must be infallible since every sinner is a wrongdoer.
The objection builds on the idea that 'wrongdoer' includes any sinner.
2
Analyze the objection's logic
Identify the chain: the imamate is a covenant → covenant does not reach wrongdoers → sinners are wrongdoers → therefore sinners cannot be imams → imam must be infallible.
3
Read the answer to the fifth objection
Read the answer provided by Imam Razi. He distinguishes between being 'engaged in sin' and being 'necessarily infallible'.
The answer is brief but decisive.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Reflect on the difference between a condition that prohibits active sin and a condition that requires absolute protection from sin. The verse only establishes the former.
This is a key subtlety in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى لإبراهيم : قَالَ وَمِن ذُرِّيَّتِي قَالَ لَا يَنَالُ عَهْدِى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٢٤] دلت الآية على أن عهد الإمامة لا يصل إلى من كان ظالما، وكل من كان مذنبًا، فإنه ظالم قال تعالى : فَمِنْهُمْ ظَالِمُ لِنَفْسِهِ [فاطر: ۳۲] فصارت الآية نصا في أن كل من كان مذنبا سواء كان ذنبه ظاهرا أو باطنا فإنه لا يكون إماماً، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت أن الإمام لا بد وأن يكون معصوما. والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أنها مبنية على المسألة الأولى، وهي أن الخلق لما كان الخطأ عليهم جائزا احتاجوا إلى نصب الإمام، وقد تقدم الجواب عن كلامهم فيه. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الشريعة إنما تبقى محفوظة بنقل الناقل المعصوم لو كان ذلك الناقل المعصوم بحيث يرى ويمكن الوصول إليه والرجوع إلى قوله فأما إذا لم يكن كذلك لم تصر الشريعة محفوظة بنقله، فسقطت هذه الشبهة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لا نزاع في أنه يجب على كل واحد من آحاد الرعية أن يقتدي بنواب الإمام من القضاة والعلماء والشهود، مع أنهم بالاتفاق ليسوا معصومين وكل ما يقولونه فيهم فهو جوابنا عن الإمام الأعظم. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : المعارضة أيضا بنواب الإمام، وذلك أن الإمام المعصوم إذا فوض الإمارة والوزارة إلى بعض الناس فذلك المفوض إليه غير معصوم، وبتقدير صدور الذنب عنه فالمانع له إما الإمام الأكبر وحده أو مع غيره. والأول: وهو أن المانع له هو الإمام الأكبر وحده، فهو باطل؛ لأن الإمام الأكبر وحده لا يقدر على دفع ذلك الأمير مع عساكره الكثيرة، ألا ترى أن عليا عليه السلام لم يقدر على دفع معاوية عن الأمر مع ما كان معه من العساكر الكثيرة، فضلا عن أن يقدر عليه وحده. والثاني: - وهو أن الدافع لذلك الأمر هو الإمام الأكبر مع عساكره ـ فهذا أيضًا باطل لأن عساكره ليسوا معصومين فربما لا يمتثلون أمر الإمام المعصوم. فثبت أن تفويض الإمارة والوزارة إلى غير المعصوم سبب لازدياد الشر والفتنة، وكل ما تقولونه ههنا فنحن أيضا نقوله في الإمام. والجواب عن الشبهة الخامسة : أن الآية دالة على أن شرط الإمام أن لا يكون مشتغلا بالذنب فأما أن يكون واجب العصمة، فلا دلالة عليه.
The fifth objection: Adherence to His statement – exalted is He – to Ibrahim: 'He said, “And of my offspring.” He said, “My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers.”' [al-Baqarah 2:124] The verse indicates that the covenant of the imamate does not reach one who is a wrongdoer, and everyone who is a sinner is a wrongdoer. He – exalted is He – said: 'Among them are wrongdoers to themselves' [Fatir 35:32]. Thus the verse becomes a clear text that everyone who is a sinner, whether his sin is apparent or hidden, cannot be an Imam, and if so, it is established that the Imam must be infallible. And the answer to the first objection: It is based on the first issue, that when error is possible for people, they need the appointment of an Imam, and the answer to their speech about it has preceded. And the answer to the second objection: The Sharia is only preserved through the transmission of an infallible transmitter, if that infallible transmitter is such that he can be seen and access to him and recourse to his statement is possible. But if it is not so, the Sharia is not preserved through his transmission, so this objection falls. And the answer to the third objection: There is no dispute that it is obligatory upon every individual of the subjects to follow the deputies of the Imam, such as judges, scholars, and witnesses, even though they are by consensus not infallible, and whatever they say about them is our answer concerning the supreme Imam. And the answer to the fourth objection: It is also a rebuttal by the deputies of the Imam, in that when the infallible Imam delegates command and ministry to some people, that delegate is not infallible, and if sin occurs from him, then the one preventing him is either the greatest Imam alone or with others. The first – that the preventer is the greatest Imam alone – is false because the greatest Imam alone cannot repel that prince with his many armies. Do you not see that Ali, peace be upon him, could not repel Muawiyah from the command with what he had of many armies, let alone that he could do it alone? And the second – that the repeller of that matter is the greatest Imam with his armies – is also false because his armies are not infallible, so perhaps they do not obey the command of the infallible Imam. Thus it is established that delegating command and ministry to a non-infallible is a cause for increase of evil and sedition, and whatever you say here we also say concerning the Imam. And the answer to the fifth objection: The verse indicates that the condition of the Imam is that he not be engaged in sin; but as for being necessarily infallible (wajib al-ismah), there is no indication of it.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection (shubhah) to the necessity of the Imam's infallibility, then provides answers to all five objections in order. The objection uses the Qur'anic verse where Ibrahim asks for his descendants to be included in the covenant, and Allah responds that the covenant does not reach the wrongdoers. The objector argues that since the imamate is a covenant, it cannot go to a wrongdoer, and since every sinner is a wrongdoer, the Imam must be infallible. The author then answers each objection: (1) The first objection is based on an earlier issue that has already been answered. (2) The second objection argues that preservation of Sharia requires an infallible transmitter, but this only works if that transmitter is accessible; if not, it is irrelevant. (3) The third objection raises the point that the Imam's deputies (judges, scholars, witnesses) are not infallible yet people follow them; the author says this same argument applies to the Imam. (4) The fourth objection involves delegation of authority to non-infallible deputies; the author argues that if the Imam tries to prevent their sins alone he cannot, and if with his armies they may not obey, leading to more evil; but this same argument can be turned against the Imam's own infallibility. (5) The answer to the fifth objection is that the verse only shows that the Imam should not be engaged in sin, not that he must be absolutely infallible. Thus, none of the objections prove the necessity of 'ismah.

Key Terms
ظالم (wrongdoer)
عهد الإمامة (covenant of imamate)
عصمة (ismah)
شبهة (objection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' proves that the Imam must be absolutely infallible.
Correction: The verse only requires that the Imam not be engaged in sin; it does not demand absolute protection from sin.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what does the verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' actually prove about the Imam?
A) The Imam must be absolutely infallible.
B) The Imam must not be engaged in sin.
C) The Imam must be from the progeny of Ibrahim.
D) The Imam must be sinless from birth.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection is answered by distinguishing between the condition of not being engaged in sin and the requirement of absolute infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the objector's argument, did you feel it was compelling? Why? Have you ever assumed a verse means something more absolute than it actually does? Reflect on a time you misapplied a Qur'anic principle to judge others. What can you do to avoid this?
?
The answer draws a fine distinction between 'not being engaged in sin' and 'absolute infallibility.' In your own life, do you hold others to a standard of absolute perfection, or do you allow for ongoing struggle? How does this lesson change how you view religious leaders?
?
If you were to counsel someone who uses this verse to claim that leaders must be sinless, what would you say based on this passage? How does that challenge your own assumptions about leadership?

Answers to Objections One Through Four
Imam Razi answers the first four objections against the necessity of the Imam's infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi systematically refutes the first four objections to the necessity of the Imam's infallibility by appealing to precedents and logical parallels.

Core Idea
The arguments for the Imam's infallibility are refuted by pointing out that the same logic would apply to non-infallible deputies.

Before tackling the fifth objection, Imam Razi provides answers to the first four objections in a concise manner. These answers share a common theme: they either rely on earlier responses, or they turn the argument back on the objector by showing that the same reasoning would undermine other accepted practices. Let's examine each answer closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the four answers
Read the Arabic text containing answers to objections 1-4. Notice how each answer is structured: first objection, then a rebuttal.
The answers are brief and assume knowledge of earlier discussions.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first answer refers back to an earlier discussion about the necessity of an Imam. It says the objection is based on that issue which has already been answered.
3
Analyze the second answer
The second answer argues that even if an infallible transmitter is needed, that transmitter must be accessible for it to be useful. If not, the objection fails.
4
Analyze the third and fourth answers
Both answers use the example of the Imam's deputies (judges, scholars, witnesses, governors) who are not infallible yet are followed. The same logic would apply to them, so the objection proves too much.
The fourth answer adds a detailed argument about delegation and the inability to prevent sin, but the core is similar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : التمسك بقوله تعالى لإبراهيم : قَالَ وَمِن ذُرِّيَّتِي قَالَ لَا يَنَالُ عَهْدِى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [البقرة : ١٢٤] دلت الآية على أن عهد الإمامة لا يصل إلى من كان ظالما، وكل من كان مذنبًا، فإنه ظالم قال تعالى : فَمِنْهُمْ ظَالِمُ لِنَفْسِهِ [فاطر: ۳۲] فصارت الآية نصا في أن كل من كان مذنبا سواء كان ذنبه ظاهرا أو باطنا فإنه لا يكون إماماً، وإذا كان كذلك ثبت أن الإمام لا بد وأن يكون معصوما. والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : أنها مبنية على المسألة الأولى، وهي أن الخلق لما كان الخطأ عليهم جائزا احتاجوا إلى نصب الإمام، وقد تقدم الجواب عن كلامهم فيه. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن الشريعة إنما تبقى محفوظة بنقل الناقل المعصوم لو كان ذلك الناقل المعصوم بحيث يرى ويمكن الوصول إليه والرجوع إلى قوله فأما إذا لم يكن كذلك لم تصر الشريعة محفوظة بنقله، فسقطت هذه الشبهة. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لا نزاع في أنه يجب على كل واحد من آحاد الرعية أن يقتدي بنواب الإمام من القضاة والعلماء والشهود، مع أنهم بالاتفاق ليسوا معصومين وكل ما يقولونه فيهم فهو جوابنا عن الإمام الأعظم. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : المعارضة أيضا بنواب الإمام، وذلك أن الإمام المعصوم إذا فوض الإمارة والوزارة إلى بعض الناس فذلك المفوض إليه غير معصوم، وبتقدير صدور الذنب عنه فالمانع له إما الإمام الأكبر وحده أو مع غيره. والأول: وهو أن المانع له هو الإمام الأكبر وحده، فهو باطل؛ لأن الإمام الأكبر وحده لا يقدر على دفع ذلك الأمير مع عساكره الكثيرة، ألا ترى أن عليا عليه السلام لم يقدر على دفع معاوية عن الأمر مع ما كان معه من العساكر الكثيرة، فضلا عن أن يقدر عليه وحده. والثاني: - وهو أن الدافع لذلك الأمر هو الإمام الأكبر مع عساكره ـ فهذا أيضًا باطل لأن عساكره ليسوا معصومين فربما لا يمتثلون أمر الإمام المعصوم. فثبت أن تفويض الإمارة والوزارة إلى غير المعصوم سبب لازدياد الشر والفتنة، وكل ما تقولونه ههنا فنحن أيضا نقوله في الإمام. والجواب عن الشبهة الخامسة : أن الآية دالة على أن شرط الإمام أن لا يكون مشتغلا بالذنب فأما أن يكون واجب العصمة، فلا دلالة عليه.
The fifth objection: Adherence to His statement – exalted is He – to Ibrahim: 'He said, “And of my offspring.” He said, “My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers.”' [al-Baqarah 2:124] The verse indicates that the covenant of the imamate does not reach one who is a wrongdoer, and everyone who is a sinner is a wrongdoer. He – exalted is He – said: 'Among them are wrongdoers to themselves' [Fatir 35:32]. Thus the verse becomes a clear text that everyone who is a sinner, whether his sin is apparent or hidden, cannot be an Imam, and if so, it is established that the Imam must be infallible. And the answer to the first objection: It is based on the first issue, that when error is possible for people, they need the appointment of an Imam, and the answer to their speech about it has preceded. And the answer to the second objection: The Sharia is only preserved through the transmission of an infallible transmitter, if that infallible transmitter is such that he can be seen and access to him and recourse to his statement is possible. But if it is not so, the Sharia is not preserved through his transmission, so this objection falls. And the answer to the third objection: There is no dispute that it is obligatory upon every individual of the subjects to follow the deputies of the Imam, such as judges, scholars, and witnesses, even though they are by consensus not infallible, and whatever they say about them is our answer concerning the supreme Imam. And the answer to the fourth objection: It is also a rebuttal by the deputies of the Imam, in that when the infallible Imam delegates command and ministry to some people, that delegate is not infallible, and if sin occurs from him, then the one preventing him is either the greatest Imam alone or with others. The first – that the preventer is the greatest Imam alone – is false because the greatest Imam alone cannot repel that prince with his many armies. Do you not see that Ali, peace be upon him, could not repel Muawiyah from the command with what he had of many armies, let alone that he could do it alone? And the second – that the repeller of that matter is the greatest Imam with his armies – is also false because his armies are not infallible, so perhaps they do not obey the command of the infallible Imam. Thus it is established that delegating command and ministry to a non-infallible is a cause for increase of evil and sedition, and whatever you say here we also say concerning the Imam. And the answer to the fifth objection: The verse indicates that the condition of the Imam is that he not be engaged in sin; but as for being necessarily infallible (wajib al-ismah), there is no indication of it.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection (shubhah) to the necessity of the Imam's infallibility, then provides answers to all five objections in order. The objection uses the Qur'anic verse where Ibrahim asks for his descendants to be included in the covenant, and Allah responds that the covenant does not reach the wrongdoers. The objector argues that since the imamate is a covenant, it cannot go to a wrongdoer, and since every sinner is a wrongdoer, the Imam must be infallible. The author then answers each objection: (1) The first objection is based on an earlier issue that has already been answered. (2) The second objection argues that preservation of Sharia requires an infallible transmitter, but this only works if that transmitter is accessible; if not, it is irrelevant. (3) The third objection raises the point that the Imam's deputies (judges, scholars, witnesses) are not infallible yet people follow them; the author says this same argument applies to the Imam. (4) The fourth objection involves delegation of authority to non-infallible deputies; the author argues that if the Imam tries to prevent their sins alone he cannot, and if with his armies they may not obey, leading to more evil; but this same argument can be turned against the Imam's own infallibility. (5) The answer to the fifth objection is that the verse only shows that the Imam should not be engaged in sin, not that he must be absolutely infallible. Thus, none of the objections prove the necessity of 'ismah.

Key Terms
الناقل المعصوم (infallible transmitter)
نواب الإمام (deputies of the Imam)
المعارضة (rebuttal)
شبهة (objection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi answer the third objection regarding the need for an infallible Imam?
A) He says the objection is based on a false premise.
B) He argues that the Imam's deputies are non-infallible yet people follow them, so the same is true for the Imam.
C) He claims the verse is abrogated.
D) He denies that the Imam needs to be infallible.

Key Takeaway
The answers to the first four objections collectively demonstrate that the arguments for the Imam's infallibility are either already resolved or self-defeating when applied consistently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a religious leader, do you subconsciously expect them to be infallible? What happens to your trust when they fail? How does this lesson challenge you to adjust that expectation?
?
Think of a time you relied on someone in authority who you knew was imperfect. Did that diminish your respect for their position? How can you follow imperfect leaders while still holding them accountable, as the deputies in this passage are followed despite not being infallible?
?
The passage uses the historical example of Ali ibn Abi Talib being unable to stop Muawiyah even with an army. How does this reality of human limitation affect your view of leadership and change? What does it demand from you in terms of patience and striving for justice, knowing that even the most righteous leaders have limits?

The Different Views on Establishing Imamate
Razi summarizes the positions of the sects on how imamate is established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions among early Muslim sects regarding how a person becomes an imam, beyond divine designation.

Core Idea
Imamate can be established through designation or, according to some, through other means like bay’a.

This passage from Imam al-Razi sets the stage for a detailed discussion on the causes of imamate. He notes that while all agree on divine designation (nass), there is sharp disagreement on whether other methods exist. This lesson maps out the three main positions that will be debated: the Zaydi, the Ash'ari/Mu'tazili, and the Twelver Imami.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the unanimous point
Begin by reading the Arabic and translation. Notice the first sentence where al-Razi states the agreement on nass. This is the starting point for all later disagreements.
The term 'nass' means explicit designation by God or His Prophet.
2
Identify the three divergent positions
Find the three groups mentioned: Zaydis, Ash'aris & Mu'tazila, and Twelvers. Note their distinctive conditions for imamate (Fatimid qualification, bay'a, and rejection of non-designative causes).
The Zaydi position includes conditions of knowledge, asceticism, and armed uprising.
3
Understand the proof from Abu Bakr's imamate
Al-Razi presents his proof: Abu Bakr's imamate was established by bay'a, not nass, so bay'a must be a valid cause. This is the Ash'ari/Mu'tazili argument.
The text has a lacuna after 'Abi Bakr' – likely 'al-Siddiq'.
4
Recognize the Twelver objection
The passage ends by noting that Twelvers argue against bay'a being a cause. This sets up the next section where their arguments are detailed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: فيما يصير الإمام به إمامًا اتفقت الأمة على أن النص من الله تعالى ورسوله على شخص بالإمامة سبب مستقل لصيرورته إماماً ، لكنهم اختلفوا في أنه هل يصير إماما بطريق آخر سوى هذا التنصيص أم لا ؟ فقالت الزيدية : إن الفاطمي إذا كان عالما زاهدًا وخرج بالسيف ودعا إلى نفسه بالإمامة صار إمامًا، وقال أصحابنا والمعتزلة : عقد البيعة سبب لحصول الإمامة والإثنا عشرية أنكروا ذلك. لنا : أنا سنقيم الدلالة على صحة إمامة أبي بكر [؟] وإمامته لم تنعقد إلا بالبيعة، وهذا يقتضي أن البيعة طريق لحصول الإمامة، أما الإثنا عشرية فقد احتجوا على أن البيعة لا يمكن أن تكون سببًا لحصول الإمامة بوجوه :
The third chapter: On what makes a person an imam. The ummah agreed that explicit designation (nass) from God Most High and His Messenger upon a person for the imamate is an independent cause for his becoming an imam, but they disagreed about whether he becomes an imam through another way besides this designation. The Zaydis said: 'Indeed the Fatimid, if he is knowledgeable and ascetic, and goes out with the sword and calls to himself for the imamate, becomes an imam.' And our companions (Asharis) and the Mu'tazila said: 'The contract of allegiance (bay'a) is a cause for the attainment of the imamate.' And the Twelvers denied that. Our proof: We will establish the proof of the validity of the imamate of Abu Bakr [?] and his imamate was not contracted except by bay'a, and this necessitates that bay'a is a way for the attainment of the imamate. As for the Twelvers, they argued that bay'a cannot be a cause for the attainment of the imamate with [several] aspects:
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage introduces the third chapter on what makes someone an imam. It states the agreement of the ummah that explicit designation (nass) from God and His Messenger is a complete cause for imamate, but disagreement exists on whether there are other means. The Zaydis hold that a Fatimid who is knowledgeable and ascetic, and rises with the sword calling to himself for imamate, becomes imam. The Ash'aris (our companions) and Mu'tazila hold that the contract of bay'a (pledge of allegiance) is a cause for imamate, while the Twelvers deny this. The author then declares that he will prove the validity of Abu Bakr's imamate, which was only established by bay'a, showing that bay'a is a valid path. He then notes that the Twelvers have raised objections against bay'a being a cause, and he will present them.
