Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree on how an imam is appointed; there is no disagreement.
Correction: While all agree on nass, the passage shows major disagreements: Zaydis allow armed uprising, Ash'aris/Mu'tazila allow bay'a, and Twelvers reject both.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris (our companions) and Mu'tazila, what is the cause for imamate?
A) Divine designation only
B) The contract of bay'a
C) Rising with the sword
D) Being a Fatimid

Key Takeaway
The main three positions on the cause of imamate are divine designation (nass), the Zaydi uprising, and the bay'a contract, with Twelvers rejecting all but nass.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Zaydis require the imam to 'go out with the sword.' Are you willing to actively fight for what you believe is the correct leadership? Or do you rely on others to act? What does your answer reveal about your commitment to justice?
?
When you consider the leaders of the Muslim community today, do you instinctively look for a divine sign (nass) or do you trust the community's choice (bay'a)? How does that preference shape your loyalty and critique of current rulers?
?
The Twelvers reject any imamate not established by nass. Imagine you were born into that belief — would you be content to wait for the hidden imam, or would you feel compelled to act? What does your patience or urgency say about your trust in God's plan?

First Twelver Objection and Its Refutation
Razi presents the first Twelver objection against bay'a and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Twelver objection against bay'a and how Razi refutes it using the analogy of witness and judge.

Core Idea
Bay'a empowers like a witness empowers a judge.

The Twelvers argue that electors have no authority, so they cannot confer authority. Razi refutes this by comparing the electors to witnesses in court: a witness has no direct authority but his testimony empowers the judge. Similarly, the act of bay'a empowers the imam by divine permission.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first doubt raised by the Twelvers. Notice their premise: those giving bay'a have no capability to dispose of even small matters. Then they ask how such people could empower an imam over the whole world.
This is a logical objection based on the principle that one cannot give what one does not have.
2
Examine Razi's refutation
Read Razi's answer. He draws an analogy from the legal system: a witness has no authority over the defendant, yet the judge uses the witness's testimony to rule. Similarly, the electors' bay'a is a means by which Allah empowers the imam.
Notice the key terms: 'shahid' (witness) and 'qadi' (judge).
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how the analogy works: the witness does not transfer his own authority (which he lacks), but his words enable the judge to act. This shows that bay'a does not require the electors to have authority; it is simply a mechanism for selection.
Reflect on how this refutes the objection without claiming that the electors have independent authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : أن هؤلاء الذين يبايعون الإمام لا قدرة لهم البتة على التصرف في أقل الأمور، وعلى أقل الأشخاص، ومن لا قدرة له على التصرف في أقل الأمور وعلى أقل الأشخاص كيف يعقل أن تكون له قدرة على إقدار الغير على التصرف في جميع أهل الشرق والغرب ؟ والجواب عن الأول : أنه منقوض بما أن الشاهد لا قدرة له على التصرف في المدعى عليه ثم إن القاضي بقوله يصير متمكنا من التصرف فيه، فكذا ههنا.
The first doubt: That these people who give allegiance (bay'a) to the imam have absolutely no capability to dispose of the smallest matters or over the least persons. And one who has no capability to dispose of the smallest matters or over the least persons, how can it be rationally conceived that he would have the capability to empower another to dispose over all the people of the East and the West? The answer to the first: It is refuted by the fact that the witness has no capability to dispose over the defendant, yet the judge, by his statement, becomes capable of disposing over him. Similarly here.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (shubha) of the Twelvers against the validity of bay'a. They argue that the people who perform bay'a (the electors) themselves have no authority over even the smallest matters or persons, so how can they confer authority over the entire world? The answer is a refutation by analogy: in a court, a witness has no authority over the defendant, yet his testimony empowers the judge to rule on the defendant. Similarly, the electors' bay'a can empower the imam. The answer does not claim they have authority, but that their act empowers the imam by God's permission.

Key Terms
شبهة
بيعة
قدرة
شاهد
قاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bay'a requires that the electors themselves have authority to transfer.
Correction: Razi's analogy shows that bay'a is like a witness's testimony: the witness has no authority, yet his statement empowers the judge. Similarly, bay'a is a mechanism by which Allah grants authority to the imam.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Razi use to answer the first doubt?
A) The relationship between a master and slave
B) The witness and judge in court
C) The father and son
D) The scholar and student

Key Takeaway
The first Twelver objection is refuted by the analogy that just as a witness empowers a judge without having authority, the electors' bay'a empowers the imam without them possessing authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pledge allegiance to a leader (whether in a mosque, community, or nation), do you believe you are transferring your own authority or recognizing God's delegation? How does this affect your responsibility in choosing that leader?
?
Consider a time you felt powerless to influence a situation. How does the analogy of the witness and judge challenge you to see that your small act—like testifying or speaking truth—can empower something far greater than yourself? Will you act on this today?
?
If God alone grants authority, yet uses the bay'a of ordinary people to empower a leader, what does that imply about the weight of your silence or your voice in matters of leadership? Are you content to be a passive subject rather than an active elector who bears witness to truth?

Second Twelver Objection and Its Refutation
Razi presents the second Twelver objection and answers it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Twelver objection that bay'a leads to fitna and Razi's refutation using criteria for preference and a fallback procedure.

Core Idea
Leadership selection by bay'a can be managed to avoid conflict.

Razi addresses a practical objection: if every region claims their own candidate, how can election avoid chaos? He provides criteria for preference and a fallback procedure to resolve deadlock, showing that fitna is not inevitable.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Twelver objection is presented: that bay'a leads to fitna because each group considers its own candidate and contract as preferable.
Notice the language of 'fitna' (strife) and 'tarjih' (preference).
2
Identify Razi's criteria
In the answer, Razi lists five criteria for preference: knowledge, asceticism, age, lineage, and popular inclination. Identify each one.
These criteria are meant to resolve conflict when multiple candidates are proposed.
3
Understand the fallback procedure
If all criteria are equal and preference is impossible, Razi says the parties should withdraw (mutadafi'an) and initiate a new contract. Grasp how this prevents fitna.
This implies a structured process rather than endless conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن إثبات الإمامة بالبيعة والعقد يفضي إلى الفتنة ؛ لأن كل أهل بلد يقولون: الإمام منا ،أولى، والعقد الصادر منا أرجح، ولا يمكن ترجيح البعض على البعض فيفضي إلى الهرج وإثارة الفتنة ومعلوم أن المقصود من نصب الإمام إزالة الفتنة بقدر الإمكان، فنصب الإمام بطريق البيعة يفضي إلى التناقض، فكان باطلا . والجواب عن الثاني : أن الترجيح يحصل إما بزيادة العلم أو الزهد أو السن أو النسب أو كثرة ميل الخلق إليه فإن حصل الاستواء في الكل وتعذر الترجيح فيتدافعان ويبتدئان بعقد آخر.
The second doubt: That establishing the imamate by bay'a and contract leads to strife (fitna), because each group of people in a land say: 'The imam is from us, more worthy, and the contract from us is preferable.' And it is impossible to prefer one over another, which leads to chaos and incitement of fitna. And it is known that the purpose of appointing an imam is to remove fitna as much as possible, so appointing an imam by way of bay'a leads to contradiction, so it is invalid. The answer to the second: That preference (tarjih) is attained either by increase in knowledge, or asceticism, or age, or lineage, or the people's greater inclination toward him. If equality in all [these] occurs and preference becomes impossible, then they mutually withdraw (mutadafi'an) and initiate another contract.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage addresses the concern that election by bay'a would cause conflict among regions each claiming their candidate. The answer admits that if no preference can be established, the two parties should step back and initiate a new contract. This implies that the process can be managed, and fitna is not inevitable.

Key Terms
فتنة
ترجيح
تدافع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bay'a necessarily leads to fitna and is therefore invalid.
Correction: Razi shows that with clear preference criteria and a withdrawal procedure, bay'a can be conducted without inevitable fitna.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is one criterion for preferring one candidate over another in the case of conflict?
A) Wealth
B) Knowledge
C) Physical strength
D) Number of supporters

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that bay'a can avoid fitna through objective criteria for preference and a fallback procedure of mutual withdrawal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are part of a group decision, do you insist on your own preference or are you willing to withdraw if you see no good outcome? How does Razi's fallback procedure challenge your pride?
?
Think of a time you contributed to fitna by insisting on your own way. How would applying the criteria of knowledge, piety, age, lineage, and popular inclination have changed the outcome?
?
Are you ready to accept a leader who might not be your first choice, based on objective criteria? What does your heart's resistance reveal about your sincerity towards unity?

Third Twelver Objection and Its Refutation
Razi presents the third Twelver objection and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the Twelver objection that inability to appoint judges implies inability to appoint an imam, by appealing to divine permission and juridical flexibility.

Core Idea
Razi counters an analogy with a theological and legal nuance.

This lesson presents the third Twelver objection to the Sunni theory of imamate and Razi's response. The objection argues from the lesser to the greater: if the electors cannot appoint a judge, how can they appoint an imam? Razi answers by noting that God's permission may differ, and that some jurists allow arbitrary appointment. This brief exchange highlights key differences in how the two traditions understand the appointment of religious authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic text of the objection and its translation. Notice how the Twelvers draw an analogy between imamate and judgeship.
2
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi gives two distinct arguments. Find them in the Arabic. The first appeals to divine permission, the second to the permissibility of arbitrary rule.
3
Evaluate the refutation
Consider whether Razi's answer fully resolves the objection. Does it address the underlying assumption about the electors' authority?
This step encourages critical thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : أن منصب الإمامة أعلى وأعظم من منصب القضاء والحسبة، وأهل البيعة لما لم يتمكنوا من نصب القاضي والمحتسب فبأن لا يتمكنوا من نصب الإمام الأعظم أولى. والجواب عن الثالث : أنه لا استبعاد في أن يأذن الله تعالى في تولية الإمامة ولا يأذن في تولية القضاة وأيضًا فالتحكم جائز على مذهب بعض الفقهاء.
The third doubt: That the office of imamate is higher and greater than the office of judgeship and the hisba (market inspector). And since the people of bay'a were not able to appoint the judge and the muhtasib, then it is even more appropriate that they are not able to appoint the supreme imam. The answer to the third: There is no improbability that God Most High permits the appointment of the imamate and does not permit the appointment of judges. Also, arbitrary rule (al-tahakkum) is permissible according to the school of some jurists.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
The objection uses an argument from analogy: if the electors cannot even appoint a judge or a market inspector, how can they appoint an imam? The answer deflects by allowing that God's permission may differ between offices. Also, some jurists allow arbitrary appointment, implying that the election process is not subject to the same constraints.

Key Terms
قضاء
حسبة
تحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a group cannot appoint lower officials, they cannot appoint a higher official.
Correction: God's permission for appointment may vary by office, and some legal schools allow non-elected appointment for the imam.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that imamate is higher than judgeship?
A) He denies that imamate is higher
B) He says God may permit imamate by bay'a but not judgeship
C) He says the electors can appoint imams but not judges
D) He says judges are appointed by imams

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Twelver analogy by appealing to God's differential permission and the permissibility of arbitrary appointment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you trust that God's permission applies differently to different aspects of your life, just as He may permit different processes for leadership? When you face a situation where one method fails, do you assume no other method is possible, or do you consider that God may have a different path?
?
The objection assumed that inability in a lower matter implies inability in a higher one. In your own life, do you sometimes think that if you cannot handle a small responsibility, you are unfit for a greater one? How does Razi's refutation challenge that fear?
?
Razi appeals to the permissibility of arbitrary rule (tahakkum) as a valid option. In your spiritual journey, are you willing to accept God's appointments even when they seem arbitrary or beyond your understanding? Are you holding on to a specific process that you think must be followed, while neglecting the possibility that God works in different ways?

The Fourth Twelver Objection and Razi's Refutation
Razi presents the fourth Twelver objection and his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Twelver argument that the imam must be appointed by divine text (nass) because he is God's deputy, and Razi's response that the community's choice can serve as a revelation of that divine appointment.

Core Idea
The imam is God's deputy, but his appointment can be revealed through the community's choice.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents and refutes the fourth Twelver objection regarding the appointment of the imam. The objection argues that since the imam is a deputy of God and the Prophet, only God or the Prophet can appoint him. Razi's response turns this around by asking why the community's selection cannot itself be the means by which God's appointment is made known.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the logic: imam = deputy → deputy must be appointed by the principal → so only God/Prophet can appoint.
Notice the use of 'نائب' (deputy) and 'نص' (text).
2
Identify the key terms
Pay attention to 'نائب', 'اختيار' (choice), and 'كشف' (reveal). These are central to the argument.
3
Read the refutation
Notice Razi's strategy: he restates the objection, then asks a counter-question that opens a new possibility.
His answer reformulates the objection.
4
Analyze the logical move
The objection assumes only direct appointment counts. Razi introduces the possibility of indirect revelation (كشف) through the community's choice.
This preserves the idea that the imam is still God's deputy.
5
Consider the broader implication
This response allows for an elective process while maintaining divine authorization, a key point in Sunni political theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : الإمام نائب الله تعالى ونائب رسوله ونيابة الغير لا تحصل إلا بإذن ذلك الغير، فوجب أن لا يثبت الإمام إلا بنص الله ورسوله، فثبت أن الإمامة لا تثبت إلا بالنص. والجواب عن الرابع : وهو قولهم: إذا كان نصب الإمام من الأمة كان الإمام نائب الأمة لا نائب الله تعالى فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يكون اختيار الأمة شخصا معينا يكشف عن كونه نائب الله تعالى؟
The fourth doubt: The imam is the deputy (na'ib) of God Most High and the deputy of His Messenger, and the deputyship of another is not obtained except by permission of that one. Therefore, it is necessary that the imam is not established except by the text (nass) of God and His Messenger. So it is established that imamate is not established except by nass. The answer to the fourth: Which is their statement: 'If the appointment of the imam is from the ummah, then the imam is the deputy of the ummah, not the deputy of God Most High.' So his answer: Why is it not permissible that the choice of the ummah of a specific person reveals that he is the deputy of God Most High?
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
The fourth objection argues that since the imam acts as deputy for God and the Prophet, only they can appoint him. The answer reformulates the objection: the Twelvers say if the ummah appoints, the imam represents the ummah, not God. Razi then retorts: why cannot the ummah's choice be a means of revealing God's appointment? This allows for the possibility that God works through the community's selection.

Key Terms
نائب
اختيار
كشف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi denies that the imam is God's deputy.
Correction: Razi maintains that the imam is God's deputy, but argues that his deputyship can be revealed through the community's choice, not necessarily by direct text.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer to the fourth doubt, what can the community's choice of a specific person reveal?
A) That the imam is a deputy of the ummah
B) That the imam is a deputy of God Most High
C) That the imam is a deputy of the Prophet
D) That the imam is independent of divine authority

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the claim that imamate requires direct divine text by arguing that the community's choice can reveal whom God has appointed as deputy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the leaders in your community—whether religious, political, or social—do you see their authority as coming from God or merely from human choice? How does Razi's idea that communal choice can reveal God's deputy challenge you to look for divine will in the decisions of the community? What would it mean for you to submit to a leader chosen by the community as if that leader were God's deputy?
?
Have you ever experienced a situation where a group decision or election revealed something true about God's plan for you? If so, how did you respond? If not, are you willing to trust that God can work through the collective wisdom of believers? This week, commit to praying for guidance when your community makes decisions, and ask God to reveal His will through that process.
?
Razi defends the possibility that the ummah's choice can uncover God's appointed deputy. In your own life, are you more inclined to resist or accept authority that comes through human processes? Is there an area where you have rejected community decisions because you thought they lacked divine backing? How might you change your attitude to see God's hand even in imperfect human systems?

Fifth Twelver Objection: Certain Knowledge or Presumption?
Razi presents and refutes the fifth Twelver objection regarding knowledge of the imam's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Twelver argument that the imam's special attributes can only be known by God, and Razi's response that presumption (al-zann) is sufficient for appointment.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge of the imam's attributes is not required; strong presumption is enough.

Razi addresses the fifth objection from the Twelver sect, which argues that since only Allah knows who truly possesses the imam's essential qualities (infallibility, superiority, knowledge, and inner faith), the imam can only be divinely appointed. Razi's response introduces a key Sunni principle: the community may rely on strong presumption rather than certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the four attributes the Twelvers list as essential for the imam, and note their conclusion that only divine appointment (nass) is possible.
2
Identify Razi's counter-claim
Razi rejects that certain knowledge is required; he states that presumption (al-zann) is the actual condition. Compare the epistemic demand.
3
Reflect on significance
Consider why this distinction matters in Sunni political theory: it opens the door for human selection (ijma') based on apparent qualities.
This is a foundation for Sunni imamate theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وأن يكون أفضل الخلق كلهم، وأن يكون أعلم الأمة كلهم، وأن يكون مسلما فيما بينه وبين الله ولا اطلاع لأحد من الخلق على هذه الصفات والله تعالى هو العالم بها، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن لا يصح نصب الإمام إلا بالنص. والجواب عن الخامس : أنا لا نسلم أن العلم القطعي بحصول تلك الصفات شرط بل الشرط حصول الظن فقط.
The fifth doubt: That the imam must be infallible (wajib al-'isma), and be the best of all creation, and be the most knowledgeable of the entire ummah, and be a Muslim between himself and God, and none of the creation have knowledge of these attributes, and God Most High is the one who knows them. And if that is the case, it is necessary that the appointment of the imam is not valid except by nass. The answer to the fifth: We do not accept that certain knowledge (al-'ilm al-qat'i) of the attainment of those attributes is a condition; rather, the condition is only the attainment of presumption (al-zann).
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
The fifth objection argues that since only God knows who truly possesses the required perfect attributes (infallibility, superiority, knowledge, inner faith), only God can designate the imam. Razi's answer is that certain knowledge is not necessary; a strong presumption (zann) suffices. This is a key point in Sunni political theory: the community's best guess based on apparent qualities is enough to establish imamate.

Key Terms
عصمة
ظن
علم قطعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sunni scholars require certain knowledge of the imam’s attributes before accepting his appointment.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that only presumption (al-zann) is required, not certain knowledge. Certainty is not a condition.

Checkpoint
Razi's response to the fifth doubt is that the imam's appointment requires what level of knowledge about his attributes?
A) Certain knowledge
B) Presumption (zann)
C) No knowledge
D) Direct revelation

Key Takeaway
Razi teaches that the community's reliable judgment (zann) is sufficient for appointing an imam, rather than requiring certain knowledge of hidden attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you require absolute certainty in every religious leader you follow, or have you ever trusted based on strong presumption? What would it take for you to accept that human judgment (zann) is enough for choosing a leader?
?
When you evaluate a scholar or imam, do you secretly demand that they be infallible and the most knowledgeable, or do you accept that they are merely the best available? How does that affect your readiness to obey them?
?
If you knew that a leader you follow has a hidden flaw you cannot be certain about, would that shake your allegiance? Or can you commit to following them based on strong presumption, as Razi argues? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on certainty versus trust?

The Fifth Doubt: Necessity of Nass for Imamate
Imam Razi states the fifth doubt requiring nass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth doubt that argues the Imam must be appointed by explicit divine text (nass) because his essential qualities are known only to God.

Core Idea
The Imam's hidden qualities require divine designation.

Imam Razi presents a key objection from the Shi'a perspective, arguing that since the Imam must possess infallibility and other unseen attributes, only God can designate him. This sets the stage for the subsequent refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the four conditions listed for the Imam: infallibility, superiority, knowledge, and inner soundness with God.
These conditions are said to be known only to God.
2
Understand the conclusion
The doubt concludes that because these qualities are hidden, appointment must be by nass (textual designation). Identify this logical leap.
3
Prepare for the answers
Consider what objections might be raised. This will help you appreciate the answers in the following lessons.
The next lessons cover the refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وأن يكون أفضل الخلق كلهم، وأن يكون أعلم الأمة كلهم، وأن يكون مسلما فيما بينه وبين الله ولا اطلاع لأحد من الخلق على هذه الصفات والله تعالى هو العالم بها، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن لا يصح نصب الإمام إلا بالنص.
والجواب عن الأول : أنه منقوض بما أن الشاهد لا قدرة له على التصرف في المدعى عليه ثم إن القاضي بقوله يصير متمكنا من التصرف فيه، فكذا ههنا.
والجواب عن الثاني : أن الترجيح يحصل إما بزيادة العلم أو الزهد أو السن أو النسب أو كثرة ميل الخلق إليه فإن حصل الاستواء في الكل وتعذر الترجيح فيتدافعان ويبتدئان بعقد آخر.
والجواب عن الثالث : أنه لا استبعاد في أن يأذن الله تعالى في تولية الإمامة ولا يأذن في تولية القضاة وأيضًا فالتحكم جائز على مذهب بعض الفقهاء.
والجواب عن الرابع : وهو قولهم: إذا كان نصب الإمام من الأمة كان الإمام نائب الأمة لا نائب الله تعالى فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يكون اختيار الأمة شخصا معينا يكشف عن كونه نائب الله تعالى ؟
والجواب عن الخامس : أنا لا نسلم أن العلم القطعي بحصول تلك الصفات شرط بل الشرط حصول الظن فقط.
Fifth doubt: The Imam must be infallible, and must be the best of all creation, and must be the most knowledgeable of the entire community, and must be sound in his relationship with God with no one from creation having knowledge of these attributes—God Most High is the Knower of them—and if that is the case, then the appointment of an Imam cannot be valid except by textual designation (nass).
The answer to the first: It is refuted by the fact that the witness has no power to act upon the defendant, then the judge by his statement becomes able to act upon him—likewise here.
The answer to the second: Preponderance (tarjīḥ) is achieved either by superiority in knowledge, asceticism, age, lineage, or the people's inclination toward him. If equality occurs in all respects and preponderance is impossible, then they supersede each other and begin another contract.
The answer to the third: There is no improbability in that God Most High permits the investiture of the Imamate but does not permit the investiture of judges. Also, ruling by arbitrary will (taḥakkum) is permissible according to some jurists.
The answer to the fourth—their statement: If the appointment of the Imam is from the community, then the Imam is the deputy of the community, not the deputy of God Most High. The answer: Why is it not permissible that the community's choice of a specific person reveals that he is the deputy of God Most High?
The answer to the fifth: We do not grant that certain knowledge (ʿilm qaṭʿī) of obtaining those attributes is a condition; rather the condition is only obtaining probable knowledge (ẓann).
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer set. It presents the fifth doubt raised by opponents regarding the necessity of textual designation (nass) for the imamate. The doubt argues that the Imam's hidden qualities can only be known by God, so appointment must be by divine text. The author then answers five objections that might be raised against this argument. The first objection uses an analogy between the Imam and a witness; the author refutes it by referring to the judge's authority. The second objection concerns preponderance among candidates; the author lists criteria for preference. The third objection questions the permissibility of God permitting imamate but not judgeships; the author notes God's discretion and that some jurists accept arbitrary rule (tahakkum). The fourth objection claims the Imam would be the community's deputy, not God's; the author responds that community choice can reveal divine appointment. The fifth objection demands certain knowledge of the Imam's qualities; the author rejects this, stating that only probable knowledge (zann) is required.

Key Terms
عصمة
نص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fifth doubt, why must the Imam be appointed by nass?
A) Because the community cannot agree
B) Because the Imam's essential qualities are known only to God
C) Because the Imam must be from the Quraysh
D) Because the previous Imam designated him

Key Takeaway
The fifth doubt argues that since the Imam's necessary attributes are knowable only to God, his appointment must be by explicit divine text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only God truly knows who is most pious and knowledgeable, why do you trust your own choice of who to follow? Does your heart submit to the one appointed by God or the one you prefer?
?
The Imam must be sound in his relationship with God—something no one else can verify. Is your relationship with God sound in a way that others would be mistaken about you? What would it mean if the person you look up to is actually corrupt in their hidden life?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that God appoints leaders through revelation, or do you prefer to have a say? What does your preference reveal about your reliance on God's wisdom versus your own?

Answers to the First Three Objections on Imamate
Imam Razi answers three objections about nass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the first three objections against the necessity of nass for imamate.

Core Idea
Divine appointment can be known through analogy, preponderance criteria, and God's discretion.

After presenting the doubt, Imam Razi anticipates three objections. The first compares the Imam to a witness, the second concerns choosing among candidates, and the third questions why God would permit imamate but not judgeship. Razi provides clear answers for each.

Guided Path
1
Read the three answers
Read the Arabic and translation for the first three answers. Notice each objection is introduced with 'والجواب عن'.
The answers are concise and use reasoning from analogy and jurisprudential principles.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first objection analogizes the Imam to a witness. Razi refutes by pointing to the judge's authority. See how this analogy is overturned.
3
Examine the second and third answers
The second answer lists criteria for preponderance. The third answer argues that God may permit one role and not another, and that tahakkum (arbitrary rule) is allowed by some jurists.
Consider how these answers strengthen the proof for nass.

The Final Two Answers: Community Choice and Probable Knowledge
Imam Razi answers the last two objections about nass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi answers the objections that the Imam would be the community's deputy and that certainty of his attributes is required.

Core Idea
Community choice can reveal divine appointment, and only probable knowledge suffices.

The last two objections challenge the source of the Imam's authority and the required level of knowledge about his qualities. Razi responds that the community's choice can serve as a sign of divine will, and that certainty is not necessary—only reasonable confidence (zann) is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth and fifth answers
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: each answer begins with 'والجواب عن' followed by the objection.
The fourth answer is a rhetorical question; the fifth is a direct rejection.
2
Understand the fourth answer
The objection says the Imam would be the community's deputy. Razi asks: why can't the community's choice reveal divine appointment? Understand this shift in perspective.
3
Grasp the fifth answer
The objection demands certain knowledge. Razi states that only probable knowledge (zann) is required. This is a key point in kalam methodology.
This answer softens the requirement without abandoning the need for evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وأن يكون أفضل الخلق كلهم، وأن يكون أعلم الأمة كلهم، وأن يكون مسلما فيما بينه وبين الله ولا اطلاع لأحد من الخلق على هذه الصفات والله تعالى هو العالم بها، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن لا يصح نصب الإمام إلا بالنص.
والجواب عن الأول : أنه منقوض بما أن الشاهد لا قدرة له على التصرف في المدعى عليه ثم إن القاضي بقوله يصير متمكنا من التصرف فيه، فكذا ههنا.
والجواب عن الثاني : أن الترجيح يحصل إما بزيادة العلم أو الزهد أو السن أو النسب أو كثرة ميل الخلق إليه فإن حصل الاستواء في الكل وتعذر الترجيح فيتدافعان ويبتدئان بعقد آخر.
والجواب عن الثالث : أنه لا استبعاد في أن يأذن الله تعالى في تولية الإمامة ولا يأذن في تولية القضاة وأيضًا فالتحكم جائز على مذهب بعض الفقهاء.
والجواب عن الرابع : وهو قولهم: إذا كان نصب الإمام من الأمة كان الإمام نائب الأمة لا نائب الله تعالى فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يكون اختيار الأمة شخصا معينا يكشف عن كونه نائب الله تعالى ؟
والجواب عن الخامس : أنا لا نسلم أن العلم القطعي بحصول تلك الصفات شرط بل الشرط حصول الظن فقط.
Fifth doubt: The Imam must be infallible, and must be the best of all creation, and must be the most knowledgeable of the entire community, and must be sound in his relationship with God with no one from creation having knowledge of these attributes—God Most High is the Knower of them—and if that is the case, then the appointment of an Imam cannot be valid except by textual designation (nass).
The answer to the first: It is refuted by the fact that the witness has no power to act upon the defendant, then the judge by his statement becomes able to act upon him—likewise here.
The answer to the second: Preponderance (tarjīḥ) is achieved either by superiority in knowledge, asceticism, age, lineage, or the people's inclination toward him. If equality occurs in all respects and preponderance is impossible, then they supersede each other and begin another contract.
The answer to the third: There is no improbability in that God Most High permits the investiture of the Imamate but does not permit the investiture of judges. Also, ruling by arbitrary will (taḥakkum) is permissible according to some jurists.
The answer to the fourth—their statement: If the appointment of the Imam is from the community, then the Imam is the deputy of the community, not the deputy of God Most High. The answer: Why is it not permissible that the community's choice of a specific person reveals that he is the deputy of God Most High?
The answer to the fifth: We do not grant that certain knowledge (ʿilm qaṭʿī) of obtaining those attributes is a condition; rather the condition is only obtaining probable knowledge (ẓann).
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer set. It presents the fifth doubt raised by opponents regarding the necessity of textual designation (nass) for the imamate. The doubt argues that the Imam's hidden qualities can only be known by God, so appointment must be by divine text. The author then answers five objections that might be raised against this argument. The first objection uses an analogy between the Imam and a witness; the author refutes it by referring to the judge's authority. The second objection concerns preponderance among candidates; the author lists criteria for preference. The third objection questions the permissibility of God permitting imamate but not judgeships; the author notes God's discretion and that some jurists accept arbitrary rule (tahakkum). The fourth objection claims the Imam would be the community's deputy, not God's; the author responds that community choice can reveal divine appointment. The fifth objection demands certain knowledge of the Imam's qualities; the author rejects this, stating that only probable knowledge (zann) is required.

Key Terms
ظن
نائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Imam must be known with certainty to be the best.
Correction: The author states that only probable knowledge (zann) is required, not certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth answer, what is the condition regarding knowledge of the Imam's attributes?
A) Certain knowledge (ilm qat'i)
B) Probable knowledge (zann)
C) No knowledge at all
D) Consensus of the scholars

Key Takeaway
The community's choice can indicate divine appointment, and only probable knowledge of the Imam's qualities is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision where the outcome is uncertain, do you wait for absolute certainty before acting, or do you rely on strong probability as sufficient? This lesson challenges the claim that only certain knowledge is valid; how does that apply to your daily choices?
?
The fourth objection assumes that community choice and divine appointment are opposites. In your own life, are there areas where you see human effort and divine will as mutually exclusive rather than complementary? Examine a specific situation.
?
This passage shows that God can work through human selection. When you are part of a community decision (like choosing a leader), do you act as if your choice can reveal God's will? What would change if you took that seriously?

Proving Abu Bakr's Imamate by Consensus and Elimination
Razi presents a two-premise proof for Abu Bakr's imamate by consensus and elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi proves Abu Bakr's imamate using consensus and elimination of other candidates.

Core Idea
The rightful imam is proven by consensus and elimination of alternatives.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a concise proof for the imamate of Abu Bakr. He argues that the Muslim community unanimously agreed that the caliph after the Prophet was one of three people: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas. By disproving the claims of Ali and al-Abbas, only Abu Bakr remains. The proof rests on two premises: first, that the consensus limited the candidates to these three; second, that Ali was in a position of incapacity to press his claim, despite his courage and supporters. This argument is foundational in Sunni political theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument structure
Read the passage to see that it presents a two-premise proof leading to a conclusion. The conclusion is that Abu Bakr is the rightful imam because the other two candidates are invalid.
The proof is deductive: if A or B or C, and not B nor C, then A.
2
Examine the first premise
The first premise states that the community agreed the rightful caliph is one of three: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas. The author justifies this by noting that the Ansar abandoned their claim after Abu Bakr's argument, leaving only these three.
This is a claim of consensus (ijma).
3
Examine the second premise and its evidence
The second premise asserts that Ali was in a state of incapacity to demand his right. The evidence includes his failure to act despite having courage, family support, and offers from al-Abbas and al-Zubayr.
The evidence is historical reports (riwayat).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the first premise limits candidates and the second removes Ali (and implicitly al-Abbas), Abu Bakr is necessarily the imam. Reflect on how this argument combines consensus and historical circumstance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع: في الدلالة على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبو بكر
والمعتمد في المسألة أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إما أبو بكر، وإما علي وإما العباس [؟] وإذا بطل القول بأن الإمام هو علي أو العباس [؟] وجب القطع بأن الإمام هو أبو بكر
واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الإمام بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة.
واعلم أن الأنصار طالبوا الخلافة لأنفسهم في أول الأمر، وقالوا: منا أمير ومنكم أمير ، فلما ناظرهم أبو بكر في ذلك تركوا قولهم، فصار ذلك القول باطلا بإجماع الأمة، وكان من نظر في كتب السير علم وتيقن اتفاق الأمة على أن الإمام بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ليس إلا أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة.
المقدمة الثانية : أن عليا [عليه السلام] كان بعد وفاة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم في العجز إلى حيث لا يمكنه طلب حق نفسه، وما كان أبو بكر [رضي الله عنه] في القوة والتسلط بحيث يمكنه غصب الحق من علي [؟].
والدليل عليه : أن عليا [عليه السلام] كان في غاية الشجاعة والشهامة، وكانت فاطمة [عليها السلام] زوجة له، وكان الحسن والحسين [عليهما السلام] ابنيها، وكان العباس [رضي الله عنه] مع علو منصبه معه.
فإنه يروى في الأخبار أن العباس قال لعلي امدد يدك أبايعك حتى يقول الناس عم رسول الله بايع ابن عم رسول الله، ولا يختلف عليك اثنان، والزبير كان مع غاية شجاعته مع علي، فإنه يروى أنه سل سيفه وقال لا أرضى بخلافة أبي بكر.
Chapter Four: On the indication that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) is Abu Bakr.
The relied-upon point in this issue is that the community (umma) is unanimous that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) is either Abu Bakr, or Ali, or al-Abbas [?]. If the statement that the Imam is Ali or al-Abbas [?] becomes invalid, then it is necessarily concluded that the Imam is Abu Bakr.
Know that this proof is based on premises:
The first premise: That the community is unanimous that the Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) is one of these three.
Know that the Ansar initially demanded the caliphate for themselves, saying: 'An amir from us and an amir from you.' When Abu Bakr argued with them about that, they abandoned their claim, so that claim became invalid by consensus of the community. Whoever examines the books of [؟] knows and is certain of the community's agreement that the Imam after the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) is only one of these three.
The second premise: That Ali [peace be upon him] after the death of the Messenger (peace be upon him) was in a state of incapacity such that he could not demand his own right, while Abu Bakr [may God be pleased with him] had such strength and dominance that he could usurp the right from Ali [?].
The evidence for it: Ali [peace be upon him] was extremely courageous and chivalrous, and Fatima [peace be upon her] was his wife, and al-Hasan and al-Husayn [peace be upon them] were her sons, and al-Abbas [may God be pleased with him] despite his high standing was with him.
It is related in reports that al-Abbas said to Ali: 'Extend your hand so I may pledge allegiance to you, so that people will say: The uncle of the Messenger of God has pledged allegiance to the son of the uncle of the Messenger of God, and no two persons will differ over you.' And al-Zubayr, despite his great courage, was with Ali. It is related that he unsheathed his sword and said: 'I am not satisfied with the caliphate of Abu Bakr.'
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a structured proof for the imamate of Abu Bakr. It begins by stating the relied-upon view: the community agreed that the rightful Imam after the Prophet is one of three: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas. If the claims of Ali and Abbas are invalidated, Abu Bakr is necessarily the Imam. The proof is built on two premises. The first premise is that the community's consensus limited the candidates to these three. The author explains that the Ansar initially claimed the caliphate for themselves but abandoned it after Abu Bakr's argument, leaving only these three. The second premise is that Ali was in a state of incapacity after the Prophet's death and could not demand his right, while Abu Bakr had the power to usurp it if he wished—but he did not. The evidence for this premise is that despite Ali's courage, the support of Fatima, his sons, and al-Abbas, and al-Zubayr's willingness to fight, Ali did not assert his claim. The author cites reports: al-Abbas offered to pledge allegiance to Ali, and al-Zubayr drew his sword in opposition to Abu Bakr, yet Ali remained inactive, showing his incapacity to pursue the caliphate.

Key Terms
إجماع
إمامة
خلافة
مقدمة
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for Abu Bakr's imamate relies solely on consensus without considering practical circumstances.
Correction: The proof actually combines consensus (first premise) with historical evidence of Ali's incapacity (second premise). It shows that consensus limited the field, and then practical inability eliminated Ali.

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, why was Ali unable to claim his right to the caliphate?
A) He lacked courage
B) He was in a state of incapacity despite his qualities
C) He did not want it
D) The people rejected him

Key Takeaway
Razi proves Abu Bakr's imamate by showing that the community's consensus limited the candidates to three, and then eliminating Ali and al-Abbas based on Ali's incapacity to press his claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that Ali did not press his claim despite having strong support. Reflect on a time when you were convinced you were right but chose to remain silent. Was it out of wisdom or weakness? What would change if you examined your motives with the same rigor Razi applies to Ali?
?
The proof relies on the community's consensus to narrow the options. In your own life, do you seek the collective wisdom of the believers when making important decisions, or do you trust your own judgment alone? How might your spiritual life improve if you submitted to the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna in matters of creed and practice?
?
Razi's argument eliminates alternatives to reach truth. What false beliefs or attachments are you still holding onto that need to be eliminated from your heart? Are you willing to let them go for the sake of truth, even if it costs you comfort or pride?

Razi's Argument for Abu Bakr: The First Premise
Razi states the argument and first premise for Abu Bakr's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's syllogistic proof for Abu Bakr's imamate and how the first premise establishes consensus on three candidates.

Core Idea
The community agrees the imam is one of three; eliminate two, Abu Bakr remains.

This passage opens the fourth chapter of Razi's Al-Arba'in, where he presents the rational argument for Abu Bakr's rightful leadership. He builds his case on a series of premises. Here, he states the overall syllogism and then develops the first premise: that the Muslim community after the Prophet (SAW) was in consensus that the next imam could only be one of three men: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas. Understanding this premise is crucial because it sets the boundaries for the subsequent argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the overall claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that Razi starts by stating the conclusion (Abu Bakr is the rightful imam) and then presents the logical structure.
The passage is a compressed syllogism; pay attention to the 'if...then' structure.
2
Break down the syllogism
Identify the three components: (1) consensus on three candidates, (2) elimination of two, (3) necessity of the third. Write them in your own words.
This is a classic form of argument by elimination (talzīq).
3
Analyze the evidence for the first premise
Razi supports the claim of consensus by referencing the Ansar's initial claim and their subsequent withdrawal after Abu Bakr's rebuttal. Consider why this historical event demonstrates that only three candidates remained.
The Ansar's claim was abandoned, leaving only the three mentioned.
4
Reflect on the significance of the three candidates
Why did Razi choose Abu Bakr, Ali, and al-Abbas? Think about their roles and relationships to the Prophet. This will be important for later premises.
Al-Abbas is included as a candidate from the Prophet's clan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع: في الدلالة على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبو بكر. والمستند في المسألة أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إما أبو بكر، وإما علي، وإما العباس. وإذا بطل القول بأن الإمام هو علي أو العباس وجب القطع بأن الإمام هو أبو بكر. واعلم أن هذا الدليل مبني على مقدمات: المقدمة الأولى: أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الإمام بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة. واعلم أن الأنصار طالبوا الخلافة لأنفسهم في أول الأمر، وقالوا: منا أمير ومنكم أمير، فلما ناظرهم أبو بكر في ذلك تركوا قولهم، فصار ذلك القول باطلا بإجماع الأمة. وكان من نظر في كتب السير علم وتيقن اتفاق الأمة على أن الإمام بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ليس إلا أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة.
Chapter Four: On the proof that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is Abu Bakr. The basis of the matter is that the community is in consensus that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is either Abu Bakr, or Ali, or al-Abbas. And if the statement that the Imam is Ali or al-Abbas is invalid, then it is necessary to be certain that the Imam is Abu Bakr. Know that this proof is built upon premises: The first premise: That the community is in consensus that the Imam after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is one of these three. And know that the Ansar sought the caliphate for themselves at the beginning of the matter, and said: 'An emir from us and an emir from you.' When Abu Bakr debated them on that, they abandoned their statement, so that statement became invalid by the consensus of the community. And whoever has looked into the books of biographies knows and is certain of the community's agreement that the Imam after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is only one of these three.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fourth chapter, where Razi establishes the proof that Abu Bakr is the rightful Imam after the Prophet (SAW). He first states the overall argument: the community (umma) agrees that the imam after the Prophet is one of three people: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas. If two of these (Ali and al-Abbas) are eliminated as possibilities, then only Abu Bakr remains, so his imamate is certain. Razi then explains that this proof is built on three premises. He presents the first premise: the community is in consensus that the imam is one of these three. He supports this by noting that the Ansar initially claimed the caliphate, saying 'An emir from us and an emir from you,' but after Abu Bakr's argument, they withdrew their claim. Thus, that claim became unanimously rejected. He adds that anyone who studies the historical sources (sira) will be certain that only these three candidates were seriously considered. This lesson covers only the first premise; the subsequent premises will follow.

Key Terms
إمام
خلافة
إجماع
أبو بكر
علي
العباس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Razi asserts that the consensus is about Abu Bakr alone, but here it is only about the three candidates.
Correction: The first premise states that the community agreed the next imam could be one of three: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas. The consensus is not on Abu Bakr exclusively at this stage.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, who does the community agree are the possible imams after the Prophet?
A) Abu Bakr and Umar
B) Abu Bakr, Ali, and al-Abbas
C) Ali and al-Abbas only
D) Abu Bakr only

Key Takeaway
The first premise of Razi's proof for Abu Bakr's imamate is the consensus of the community that the imam is one of three: Abu Bakr, Ali, or al-Abbas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ansar withdrew their claim after debate with Abu Bakr. In your own life, when have you stubbornly held onto an opinion despite clear evidence, and what would it mean for you to be as humble as the Ansar?
?
Razi assumes the consensus of the sahaba is a valid proof. Do you personally trust the collective decision of the sahaba in matters of leadership, or do you find yourself doubting it? What is the basis for your trust or doubt?
?
If you were in the position of the Ansar, would you have the courage to abandon your initial position for the sake of unity? What would you need to change in your character to submit to truth even when it costs you privilege?

The Second Premise: Ali's Supporters and the Absence of a Clear Text
Razi enumerates Ali's supporters and argues that their inaction disproves a clear text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the presence of Ali's powerful supporters and their failure to act on a clear textual designation to argue against the existence of such a text.

Core Idea
If Ali had a clear divine text, his supporters would have used it.

Razi begins his second premise by asserting that after the Prophet's death, Ali was unable to claim his right while Abu Bakr was powerful. He then marshals evidence from Ali's supporters — al-Abbas, al-Zubayr, Abu Sufyan, and the Ansar — to show that if there had been a clear textual designation (nass), these supporters would have known and acted upon it. This passage sets the stage for his conclusion that the absence of such action proves no nass existed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Razi's argumentative style. Notice how he combines historical reports with logical inference.
2
Identify the supporters
List al-Abbas, al-Zubayr, Abu Sufyan, and the Ansar. Note what each offered or said to show support for Ali.
Each supporter had a specific reason or offer.
3
Understand the hypothetical
Razi argues that if there had been a clear nass, these supporters would have known it and used it to oppose Abu Bakr. Why does he think they would have done so?
Consider the Ansar's existing hostility to Abu Bakr.
4
Ask the key question
Why does Razi use this hypothetical to argue against nass? What does the silence of the supporters imply?
This is the core of his proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية: أن علياً عليه السلام كان بعد وفاة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم في العجز إلى حيث لا يمكنه طلب حق نفسه، وما كان أبو بكر رضي الله عنه لها في القوة والتسلط بحيث يمكنه غصب الحق من علي. والدليل عليه: أن علياً عليه السلام كان في غاية الشجاعة والشهامة، وكانت فاطمة عليها السلام زوجة له، وكان الحسن والحسين رضي الله عنهما ابنيه، وكان العباس رضي الله عنه مع علو منصبه معه. فإنه يروى في الأخبار أن العباس قال لعلي: امدد يدك أبايعك حتى يقول الناس: عم رسول الله بايع ابن عم رسول الله، ولا يختلف عليك اثنان. والزبير كان مع غاية شجاعته مع علي، فإنه يروى أنه سل سيفه وقال: لا أرضى بخلافة أبي بكر. وأما أبو سفيان فإنه قال: أرضيتم يا بني عبد مناف أن تلي عليكم تيم؟ والله لأملأن الوادي عليكم خيلاً ورجلاً. وأما جملة الأنصار فإنهم كانوا أعداء أبي بكر، وذلك لأنهم طلبوا الإمامة لأنفسهم، فدفعهم أبو بكر عنها بقوله: «الأئمة من قريش». فلو كان علي عليه السلام منصوصًا عليه نصًا ظاهرًا لعرفوه، ولو عرفوه لقالوا لأبي بكر: نحن أردنا أن نأخذ الخلافة لأنفسنا، فلما منعتنا عنها فنحن نمنعك أيضًا عن الظلم، ونسلمها إلى مستحقها. فإن من المعلوم أن الخصم القوي إذا وجد مثل هذا الطعن لا يتركه. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الإمامة لو كانت حقًا لعلي بالنص لكان في غاية القدرة على أخذها ومنع الظالم المنازع فيها. وأما أبو بكر فمعلوم أنه ما كان معه عسكر ولا شوكة ولا مال، وعند الروافض أنه كان ضعيفًا جبانًا. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك استحال في مثل علي مع كثرة أسباب الأمرة والقوة والشوكة في حقه أن يكون عاجزًا في يد شيخ ضعيف ليس له مال ولا عسكر ولا قوة بدن ولا قوة قلب، يبلغ ذلك العجز إلى حيث لم يخرج عن داره ولم يظهر المحاربة والمنازعة بوجه من الوجوه. وهذا مما لا يقبله العقل البتة. واعلم أن أحوال الإثنى عشرية في هذا الباب عجيبة، وذلك لأنهم إذا وصفوا عليًا بالشجاعة والشوكة بالغوا في ذلك الوصف بحيث يخرجونه عن المعقول، وإذا تكلموا في هذه المسألة وصفوا عليًا بالعجز ويبالغون فيه مبالغة يخرجونه عن المعقول.
The second premise: That Ali, peace be upon him, after the death of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was in a state of inability to the extent that he could not seek his own right, while Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, was in a state of power and domination such that he could usurp the right from Ali. The evidence for this: That Ali, peace be upon him, was at the peak of courage and chivalry, and Fatima, peace be upon her, was his wife, and al-Hasan and al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with them, were his sons, and al-Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, was with him despite his high status. For it is narrated in reports that al-Abbas said to Ali: 'Extend your hand so that I may pledge allegiance to you, such that the people say: The uncle of the Messenger of Allah has pledged allegiance to the cousin of the Messenger of Allah, and no two will differ over you.' And al-Zubayr, with all his courage, was with Ali, for it is reported that he unsheathed his sword and said: 'I am not satisfied with the caliphate of Abu Bakr.' As for Abu Sufyan, he said: 'Are you satisfied, O sons of Abd Manaf, that Taym should rule over you? By Allah, I will fill the valley with horses and men against you.' And as for the whole group of the Ansar, they were enemies of Abu Bakr, because they sought the imamate for themselves, but Abu Bakr repelled them with his statement: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' So if Ali, peace be upon him, had been explicitly designated by a clear text, they would have known it, and if they had known it, they would have said to Abu Bakr: 'We wanted to take the caliphate for ourselves, but since you prevented us from it, we also prevent you from injustice, and we hand it over to its rightful owner.' For it is known that a strong opponent, when he finds such an objection, does not abandon it. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if the imamate had been the right of Ali by text, he would have been at the utmost ability to take it and to prevent the unjust contender from it. As for Abu Bakr, it is known that he had no army, no power, and no wealth, and according to the Rawafid, he was weak and cowardly. When the matter is so, it is impossible for one like Ali, given his many causes of leadership, strength, and power, to be helpless in the hands of a weak old man who has no wealth, no army, no physical strength, and no strength of heart, to such an extent that he did not come out of his house and did not show struggle or contention in any way. This is completely unacceptable to reason. And know that the condition of the Twelvers in this matter is strange, because when they describe Ali with courage and power, they exaggerate in that description to the point of removing him from reason, and when they speak about this issue, they describe Ali with inability and exaggerate in it to the point of removing him from reason.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is the second premise of Razi's proof for Abu Bakr's imamate. Razi aims to show that Ali was unable to claim his right despite having many supporters, while Abu Bakr was powerful. The premise states that after the Prophet's death, Ali was in a state of inability to demand his right, whereas Abu Bakr was strong enough to seize it. Razi provides evidence: Ali had great courage, his wife Fatima, his sons Hasan and Husayn, and al-Abbas with his high status. He cites reports: al-Abbas offered to pledge allegiance to Ali; al-Zubayr drew his sword in objection to Abu Bakr; Abu Sufyan offered to support Ali; and the Ansar were hostile to Abu Bakr because he denied them the caliphate. Razi argues that if Ali had a clear textual designation (nass), these supporters would have known it and would have objected to Abu Bakr, saying they would not let him usurp the right. Since they did not do so, it shows there was no such text. Razi concludes that if the imamate were Ali's right by text, he would have been able to take it. But he did not, so it cannot be his right. He then criticizes the Twelvers for inconsistency: they exaggerate Ali's courage in other contexts but depict him as helpless here. This premise sets up the third premise that since Ali and al-Abbas did not claim the imamate despite ability, they must have recognized Abu Bakr's right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is attacking Ali's honor or courage.
Correction: Razi is not attacking Ali; he uses the historical fact of Ali's silence and inaction to argue that there was no clear textual designation for his imamate.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why would the Ansar have supported Ali if he had a clear text?
A) Because they loved Ali
B) Because they would have recognized his right and used it to oppose Abu Bakr
C) Because they were always against Abu Bakr
D) Because the text was in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the failure of Ali's powerful supporters to act on a clear text as evidence that no such text existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you believe you are right but strong arguments and evidence seem to support the other side, do you examine your assumptions with the same rigor Razi applies to the early Muslims? Reflect on a specific situation where you had to choose between loyalty to a person and loyalty to evidence. What did you learn about yourself?
?
Razi uses the silence of Ali's supporters as evidence. Think of a time when your own silence—or the silence of others—was misinterpreted. Did your silence reflect agreement, fear, or the absence of a compelling argument? How can this lesson teach you to better understand the reasons behind people's actions rather than jumping to conclusions?
?
If you had been among Ali's supporters and knew of a clear text, would you have spoken out? What fears or pressures might have held you back? Now, consider your own faith: are there truths you know but fail to act upon because of social pressure or personal cost? What is one truth you will commit to living out more boldly, starting today?

The Impossible Conclusion: Ali's Inability and the Twelver Contradiction
Razi concludes that Ali's inaction disproves nass and criticizes Twelver inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's conclusion that Ali's inability to claim his right despite power proves the imamate was not his by text, and his critique of Twelver inconsistency.

Core Idea
If Ali had the power to take the caliphate, he would have; since he didn't, he had no right to it.

Having argued that Ali's supporters would have acted on a clear text, Razi now concludes that Ali's inability to seize the caliphate despite his strength proves no such text existed. He contrasts Ali's power with Abu Bakr's weakness and criticizes the Twelvers for their inconsistent portrayal of Ali.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion
Read the passage where Razi states that if the imamate was Ali's by text, he would have been able to take it and prevent the unjust contender.
2
Contrast Ali and Abu Bakr
Notice Razi's description of Abu Bakr as weak, without army or wealth, according to the Rawafid. Compare this with Ali's strength.
3
Unpack the impossibility
Razi says it is rationally impossible for a strong man like Ali to be helpless before a weak old man. Why does he consider this impossible?
Think about the natural consequences of power.
4
Consider the Twelver inconsistency
Razi points out that Twelvers exaggerate Ali's courage in other contexts but depict him as helpless here. How does this contradiction weaken their position?

This passage is the second premise of Razi's proof for Abu Bakr's imamate. Razi aims to show that Ali was unable to claim his right despite having many supporters, while Abu Bakr was powerful. The premise states that after the Prophet's death, Ali was in a state of inability to demand his right, whereas Abu Bakr was strong enough to seize it. According to Razi, why is it impossible that Ali was helpless before Abu Bakr?
A) Because Ali had courage, supporters, and resources while Abu Bakr had no army or wealth
B) Because Ali was a prophet and could not be defeated
C) Because Abu Bakr was actually weak but had divine support

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you ever like the Twelvers Razi criticizes, praising a person's strength in one breath and excusing their weakness in another to protect your own beliefs? When have you been inconsistent in evaluating someone you admire, and what would it take to be honest about that inconsistency today?
?
Razi's argument forces you to consider that inaction can be a sign of lack of right. Is there an area in your life where you have avoided acting because deep down you know you have no divine mandate to do so? What would it cost you to confront that inaction and seek clarity from God?
?
The passage ends with Razi marveling at the Twelvers' contradiction. Have you ever held two contradictory beliefs about yourself or about God without noticing? How can you train yourself to see the inconsistencies in your own heart and repent from self-deception?

Razi's Third Premise: Ali and Abbas' Silence Proves Abu Bakr's Imamate
Razi concludes that since Ali and Abbas did not claim the imamate, it must be Abu Bakr's right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the historical fact that Ali and Abbas did not contest Abu Bakr's caliphate to logically conclude that Abu Bakr must be the rightful imam.

Core Idea
Ali and Abbas' silence forces the conclusion: Abu Bakr is the rightful imam.

Razi now presents the third premise of his argument, building on the consensus that the imam is one of three candidates. By observing that Ali and al-Abbas did not contend for the imamate despite being able to, Razi constructs a dilemma that leads in both directions to Abu Bakr's imamate. This passage is a masterful example of theological reasoning combined with historical fact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi sets up a dilemma: the silence of Ali and Abbas must be explained either by inability or ability.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
Razi argues that if the imamate were the right of Ali or Abbas, their silence would be a major sin, disqualifying them. If the imamate is not their right, it must belong to Abu Bakr. Trace both branches.
This is the heart of the argument.
3
Understand the role of the second premise
Razi relies on the previous premise (second premise) to eliminate the possibility of inability. This is crucial for the dilemma to work.
The second premise established that Ali and Abbas had the power to contend.
4
Reach the conclusion
No matter which branch you follow, the conclusion is the same: Abu Bakr is the rightful imam. This shows the strength of Razi's reasoning.
The conclusion is inescapable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة: أن نقول: لما ثبت بالإجماع أن الإمام أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة، فنقول: وجدنا عليًا وعباسًا رضي الله عنهما تركا المنازعة مع أبي بكر، وذلك الترك إما للعجز أو للقدرة. لا جائز أن يكون للعجز لما قررنا في المقدمة الثانية. فثبت أنهما تركا المنازعة مع القدرة على المنازعة. فإن كانت الإمامة حقًا لواحد منهما، كان ترك هذه المنازعة معصية كبيرة، وذلك يوجب انعزالهما. وإذا ثبت انعزالهما ثبت القول بإمامة أبي بكر؛ لأن الإمام لا بد أن يكون موجودًا. وإن لم تكن الإمامة حقًا لهما وجب أن تكون حقًا لأبي بكر لئلا يخرج الحق عن جميع أقوال الأمة. فثبت أنه لا بد على كل حال من الاعتراف بإمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه.
The third premise: That we say: Since it is established by consensus that the Imam is one of these three, we say: We find that Ali and al-Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them, abandoned contention with Abu Bakr. That abandonment is either due to inability or due to ability. It cannot be due to inability, as we established in the second premise. So it is established that they abandoned contention despite having the ability to contend. If the imamate were the right of one of them, then this abandonment of contention would be a major sin, and that would necessitate their disqualification. And if their disqualification is established, then the statement of the imamate of Abu Bakr is established, because there must be an Imam. And if the imamate is not their right, then it must be the right of Abu Bakr, so that the truth does not exit all the statements of the community. Thus it is established that in any case, it is necessary to acknowledge the imamate of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is the third premise and the conclusion of Razi's proof. He reasons: Since we have established by consensus that the imam is one of three (Abu Bakr, Ali, al-Abbas), we observe that both Ali and al-Abbas refrained from contending with Abu Bakr. That refraining must be either due to inability or due to ability (i.e., by choice). It cannot be due to inability, because the second premise proved they were powerful. So they refrained despite being able to contend. Now, if the imamate were the right of one of them, then refraining from claiming it would be a major sin, which would disqualify them from the imamate. If they are disqualified, then the imamate must belong to Abu Bakr, because an imam must exist. On the other hand, if the imamate is not their right (i.e., it belongs to Abu Bakr), then Abu Bakr is the imam in any case. Thus, in both scenarios, we must acknowledge Abu Bakr as the rightful imam.

Key Terms
منازعة
عجز
قدرة
معصية
انعزال
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi claims that Ali and Abbas actually committed a sin by not disputing Abu Bakr.
Correction: Razi makes a conditional argument: if the imamate were their right, then their silence would be sinful; but since they are sinless, the imamate cannot be their right. He does not accuse them of sin.

Checkpoint
According to the third premise, what would be the consequence if the imamate were the right of Ali or al-Abbas?
A) They would have been rightful imams
B) Their abandonment of contention would be a major sin
C) Abu Bakr would still be the imam
D) The community would be without an imam

Key Takeaway
Razi's third premise demonstrates that the historical fact of Ali and Abbas's silence logically compels the conclusion that Abu Bakr is the rightful imam, regardless of which branch of the dilemma one follows.

The Argument from the Silence of Ali and Abbas for Abu Bakr's Imamate
Razi argues from the silence of Ali and Abbas to prove Abu Bakr's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the silence of Ali and Abbas as a proof for Abu Bakr's imamate, and the logical structure of this argument.

Core Idea
Silence from a rightful claim implies either inability or sin – either way, Abu Bakr’s imamate stands.

Razi continues his series of arguments for the imamate of Abu Bakr by presenting a third premise based on the observed behavior of Ali and Abbas. He argues that their refraining from contesting Abu Bakr's succession cannot be due to inability, so it must be a conscious choice. This choice, if they had a right to the imamate, would be a sin disqualifying them; if not, the right must belong to Abu Bakr. Thus, in either scenario, Abu Bakr's imamate is confirmed. This lesson unpacks that logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the conditional reasoning.
2
Understand the premise
The argument assumes there are only three candidates: Ali, Abbas, and Abu Bakr. This is based on consensus.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Razi presents a dilemma: either the silence was due to inability (already disproven) or due to ability. Then a second dilemma: either the imamate was their right (so silence is sin) or not (so right is Abu Bakr's).
Follow each branch.
4
Consider the objection
Razi mentions the objection that Ali avoided fighting to prevent fitna, but claims it is already refuted.
This is a key point for further study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة أن نقول : لما ثبت بالإجماع أن الإمام أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة، فنقول: وجدنا عليا وعباسا وتركا المنازعة مع أبي بكر، وذلك الترك إما للعجز أو للقدرة، لا جائز أن يكون للعجز لما قررنا في المقدمة الثانية، فثبت أنهما تركا المنازعة مع القدرة على المنازعة فإن كانت الإمامة حقا لواحد منهما، كان ترك هذه المنازعة معصية كبيرة وذلك يوجب انعزالهما، وإذا ثبت انعزالهما ثبت القول بإمامة أبي بكر لنا وإن لم تكن الإمامة حقا لهما وجب أن تكون حقا لأبي بكر لئلا يخرج الحق عن جميع أقوال الأمة، فثبت أنه لا بد على كل حال من الاعتراف بإمامة أبي بكر الأولى بها. واعلم أنه لا كلام للمخالف على هذا الدليل إلا كلامهم المشهور من أن عليا إنما ترك المحاربة لأجل الفتنة والخوف، ونحن أبطلنا هذا الكلام، فيبقى هذا الدليل عن المعارضة سالما.
The third premise: we say: since it is established by consensus that the imam is one of these three, we say: we found Ali and Abbas refraining from contestation with Abu Bakr, and that refraining is either due to inability or due to ability. It is not possible that it was due to inability, because of what we established in the second premise. So it is established that they refrained from contestation while having the ability to contest. If the imamate was a right for one of them, then this refraining would be a grave sin and would necessitate their deposition. And if their deposition is established, then the statement of Abu Bakr's imamate is established for us. And if the imamate was not a right for them, then it must be a right for Abu Bakr, lest the truth be excluded from all statements of the ummah. So it is established that it is necessary in any case to acknowledge the imamate of Abu Bakr, the most deserving of it. And know that the opponent has no objection to this proof except their famous statement that Ali only refrained from fighting because of tribulation and fear, and we have refuted this statement, so this proof remains safe from opposition.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument in Razi's refutation of the Shia claim for Ali's imamate. It begins by establishing the third premise: based on consensus, the imam after the Prophet is one of three: Ali, Abu Bakr, or Abbas. Razi then observes that both Ali and Abbas refrained from contesting Abu Bakr's succession. This refraining must be due either to inability or ability. Since the second premise (not fully repeated here) established that Ali was not weak (he had strength and supporters), the refraining cannot be due to inability; hence it was done while they had the ability to contest. If the imamate was truly a right for one of them, then their refraining from claiming that right when able would be a grave sin, which would disqualify them from the imamate. Thus, either they are disqualified (making Abu Bakr the imam by default) or the imamate was never their right but rather Abu Bakr's. In either case, Abu Bakr's imamate is affirmed. Razi then addresses the common Shia objection that Ali refrained only to avoid fitna (tribulation and bloodshed), stating that this objection has already been refuted elsewhere, leaving the proof intact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ali's silence implies he consented to Abu Bakr's imamate, which would be a sign of his approval.
Correction: Razi argues that if Ali had a rightful claim, his silence would be a sin, not approval. The silence is used as evidence for Abu Bakr, not for Ali's consent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, what is the reason the silence of Ali and Abbas cannot be due to inability?
A) They were weak.
B) The second premise already established they were not weak.
C) They had no supporters.
D) They were afraid.

Key Takeaway
Razi’s argument shows that regardless of whether Ali and Abbas had a rightful claim to the imamate or not, their silence in the presence of ability to contest leads inevitably to the conclusion that Abu Bakr is the rightful imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali and Abbas had the ability to contest but remained silent. When you see injustice or a truth being suppressed, do you speak up even when you have the power to do so? Where have you remained silent out of fear of conflict, and what would it take for you to break that silence?
?
Razi argues that if the imamate were their right, their silence would be a sin. Consider a time when you knew something was right but you stayed quiet because it was easier. How does that silence weigh on your conscience? What step can you take today to address that?
?
The dilemma forces a conclusion: either the rightful claimants sinned, or the truth lies with Abu Bakr. There is no neutral ground. In your own faith, are there hard conclusions you have avoided because they demand a change in your life or relationships? What is one truth you have been avoiding, and what would it cost you to embrace it fully?
